The Benefit of Proving Fundamentals for Accepting Prophetic Teachings
Al-Nasafi argues that once fundamentals are proven, all prophetic statements are true and this refutes dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how establishing fundamental theological proofs (the world's creation, the Creator's oneness and wisdom, and the prophethood's infallibility) makes all prophetic statements necessarily true and provides a powerful method for refuting dualists.

Core Idea
Proven fundamentals guarantee the truth of all prophetic teachings.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi concludes a discussion on the practical benefit of establishing foundational proofs. It shows that once we prove the world's creation, the Creator's attributes, and the prophethood's reliability, we can confidently accept everything the Prophet conveys, even if we cannot fully comprehend its wisdom. Al-Nasafi also highlights how this approach cuts off objections from dualists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note how the author builds a logical chain.
2
Identify the premises
List the four premises al-Nasafi mentions: (1) world is created, (2) Maker exists and is one and wise, (3) prophethood is established, (4) the Messenger is infallible.
These are the fundamental proofs.
3
Understand the conclusion
From these premises, al-Nasafi concludes that everything the Prophet said is true, even if we cannot grasp the wisdom. Ask yourself: why does the conclusion follow?
Because a verified source guarantees the truth of its content.
4
See the debate application
Al-Nasafi recommends this method when debating Magians or dualists. Why does this cut off objections? Reflect on how a solid foundation prevents endless disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنا إذا بينا بالدليل حدوث العالم، وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته وحكمته، وثبوت الرسالة وعصمة الرسول، ثبت بهذه المقدمات الصادقة أن جميع ما بينه الرسول حق وصدق، وفيه حكمه بليغة، وإن قصرت عقولنا وعجزت أفهامنا عن الوقوف على جهة الحكمة، فمن تكلم مع المجوس أو الثنوية ينبغي أن يتكلم على هذا الوجه فإنه أقطع لشغب الخصوم والله المحمود على التوفيق.
For when we demonstrate by proof the originatedness of the world, the establishment of the Maker, His oneness and wisdom, the establishment of prophethood and the infallibility of the Messenger, it is established by these true premises that everything the Messenger explained is truth and reality, and in it are his wise judgments, even if our intellects fall short and our understandings are incapable of grasping the aspect of wisdom. So whoever debates with the Magians or the dualists should debate in this manner, for it cuts off the clamor of opponents. Allah is praised for granting success.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument about the benefit of establishing fundamental theological proofs. Al-Nasafi argues that once we have proven by evidence that the world is created, that there is a Creator who is one and wise, and that prophethood and the infallibility of the Prophet are established, then from these true premises it follows that everything the Prophet conveys is true and correct. Even if our minds cannot fully understand the wisdom behind some rulings, we must accept them because the source is verified. He then recommends this method when debating with Magians (Zoroastrians) or dualists, as it cuts off their objections. The passage emphasizes that proper theological method not only establishes truth but also provides effective rebuttals against adversaries.

Key Terms
الرسالة (prophethood)
عصمة الرسول (infallibility of the Messenger)
المجوس (Magians)
الثنوية (dualists)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That we need to fully comprehend all revealed wisdom before accepting it
Correction: Al-Nasafi says even if our intellects fall short and our understandings are incapable of grasping the wisdom, we must accept because the source (the Prophet) is proven truthful and infallible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is established when we prove the world's creation, the Creator's oneness and wisdom, and the Prophet's infallibility?
A) The Prophet's teachings are true even if we do not understand their wisdom
B) The world is eternal
C) The Creator is an accident
D) The Magians are correct

Key Takeaway
When fundamental proofs are established, all prophetic teachings are accepted as true, even if we don't fully understand the wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a teaching of the Prophet that you currently struggle to accept or follow because you don't see its wisdom? After reading this passage, will you commit to accepting it fully, trusting that the source is proven true, even if your intellect falls short?
?
When you encounter someone who rejects Islamic teachings, do you tend to argue about specific rulings or start from the fundamental proofs of God's existence and prophethood? What would it cost you to adopt al-Nasafi's method in your next conversation?
?
This passage says 'even if our intellects fall short and our understandings are incapable of grasping the aspect of wisdom.' Where in your life have you rejected or doubted something because it seemed irrational? Will you now examine whether your rejection stems from a failure to first verify the source's truthfulness?

The Principle of Inseparable Attachment and Non-Causality
The author states a principle about inseparable attachment not implying causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why inseparable attachment between two things does not automatically mean one causes the other, and what additional condition is needed for causality.

Core Idea
Inseparability is not causality—attachment alone does not prove causation.

This lesson explores a foundational principle in Islamic theology: just because two things are always found together, we cannot assume one is the cause of the other. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, uses clear examples to illustrate this point, building up to the critical condition that must be met for causality to be inferred.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, logical style of the text.
2
Identify the opening principle
The passage begins with a statement: 'A place in which that meaning is found will only be for that meaning.' Understand what this means for the relationship between an attribute and its subject.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives examples: motion makes something 'moving', not existence or self-subsistence. Also black, white, combined, separated, etc. See how each example illustrates the principle that an attribute is attributed for its own meaning, not another.
These examples are key to grasping the general rule.
4
Understand the conclusion about causality
From the examples, the author draws a general rule: inseparable attachment (coexistence in existence and non-existence) does not automatically imply causality. One thing attaching to another inseparably does not make it a cause.
This is the core theological point.
5
Note the condition for causality
The passage ends with a crucial condition: for inseparable attachment to imply causality, it must be impossible to attribute the attribute to any other meaning. Identify this condition and reflect on its significance.
Without this condition, inseparability alone is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محل وجد فيه ذلك المعنى، لن يكون إلا لذلك المعنى، ومن ادعى إطلاقه عليه لا لذلك المعنى بل لأمر آخر كان مخطئا في دعواه، كمن ادعى أن ما قامت به الحركة لم يسم متحركا لقيام الحركة به بل لوجوده أو لقيامه بالذات كان مخطئا في دعواه، وكذا في الأسود والأبيض والمجتمع والمتفرق وغير ذلك من الصفات فكذا هذا وجاء من هذا أن كل معنى تعلق بشيء تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، ويدور معه وجودا وعدما لا يوجب تعلقه به تعلق العلة بالمعلول، ولا يوجب تعدي أحدهما إلى الغائب تعدية الآخر على الإطلاق، بل بشريطة استحالة إضافته إلى معنى آخر.
A place in which that meaning is found will only be for that meaning. Whoever claims that it is attributed to it not for that meaning but for another reason is mistaken in his claim, like one who claims that that to which motion attaches is not called 'moving' due to the attachment of motion to it but due to its existence or its subsistence by itself — he is mistaken in his claim. Likewise regarding black, white, combined, separated, and other attributes, and so this one. And from this follows that every meaning that attaches to a thing with an inseparable attachment, and coexists with it in existence and non-existence, does not necessitate that its attachment to it is the attachment of cause to effect, nor does it necessitate that the one extends to the absent as the other does absolutely, but only under the condition that it is impossible to attribute it to another meaning.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle in kalam about the relationship between a meaning (معنى) and its subject. The author begins by stating that if a meaning is found in a place, it must be for that meaning alone; any claim that it is attributed to that place for a different reason is mistaken. He gives examples: motion makes something moving, not existence or subsistence by itself. The same applies to other attributes. Then he draws a general rule: When two things are inseparably linked and co-exist in existence and non-existence, this does not automatically mean that one is the cause of the other. For such a causal relationship to hold, it must be impossible to attribute the attribute to any other meaning. So inseparability alone is insufficient.

Key Terms
معنى
تعلق
علة
معلول
دوران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are always together, one must be the cause of the other.
Correction: The passage clarifies that inseparable attachment does not automatically imply causality; an additional condition is needed: it must be impossible to attribute the attribute to any other meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what additional condition is required for an inseparable attachment to imply causality?
A) The two things must be equal in definition.
B) It must be impossible to attribute the attribute to any other meaning.
C) The subject must be essential to the attribute.
D) The attribute must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Inseparability alone does not prove causality; for causality, it must be impossible to attribute the effect to any other cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a personal quality or habit that you assume makes you who you are (e.g., 'I am patient because I pray'). Does this lesson challenge that assumption? How might you test whether that quality is truly the cause or merely attached? What would it mean for your self-understanding if it is only attached?
?
When you judge someone's character based on a single action, are you treating that action as the cause of their identity? How can the condition in this passage ('impossible to attribute to another meaning') protect you from making unfair, final judgments about others?
?
Consider a difficulty in your life that you think is caused by a specific sin or shortcoming. Inseparable attachment might make you believe the sin inevitably leads to that difficulty. What does this lesson teach you about looking for alternative causes? Could the difficulty have another source? How might this shift your response to it?

Critique of Defining Existence as Substance or Accident
The author criticizes the method of defining existence by dividing it into substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the definition of existence as substance or accident and how this relates to the conditions of a valid definition.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be comprehensive; dividing existence into substance and accident fails this condition.

In this passage, the author continues his critique of alternative methodologies in kalam. He examines the common practice of defining the existent by dividing it into substance and accident, a method he deems corrupt. By analyzing the conditions of a proper definition, he shows why this approach is insufficient and how it reveals deeper methodological disagreements between the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Haqq.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the passage once, then the translation, to get the overall argument.
Notice the technical term 'تحديد مقسم'.
2
Identify the two descriptions and the impossibility of combination
The passage says it is impossible for one existent to be both substance and accident. Ask yourself why this makes the definition not comprehensive.
3
Understand the condition of comprehensiveness (جامع)
The author states that a valid definition must be comprehensive (جامع). This definition fails because it cannot cover all instances in one subject.
The reference to al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge illustrates a similar error.
4
Consider the sectarian dimension
The author notes that this objection only works for Ahl al-Haqq, because the Mu'tazila allow divisive definitions and cannot escape the Christians' objection.
This shows how methodology impacts theological positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحديد الموجود بأنه جوهر أو عرض فاسد، لأنه تحديد مقسم وهو فاسد، لأن جمع الوصفين جميعا في محل واحد محال، إذ يستحيل موجود جوهر وعرض، وإثبات كل وصف في محل على حده ليس بجامع، ومن شرط صحة الحد أن يكون جامعا على ما مر في إفساد كلام الجبائي في تحديده العلم أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة أو دليل، وهذا الجواب مما لا يستقيم إلا على أصول أهل الحق فأما المعتزلة المجوزون التحديد المقسم فلا يمكنهم الخروج عن إلزام النصارى، والله الموفق.
And defining the existent as either substance or accident is corrupt, because it is a divisive definition (تحديد مقسم) and it is corrupt, because bringing together both descriptions in a single subject is impossible, since it is impossible for an existent to be both substance and accident. And establishing each description in a separate subject is not comprehensive. And one of the conditions for the validity of a definition is that it be comprehensive, as has passed in the refutation of al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge as 'belief about a thing as it is based on necessity or proof'. And this answer is only sound according to the principles of the people of truth. As for the Mu'tazila who allow divisive definition, they cannot escape the Christians' objection. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage addresses a different kind of argument: defining existence by dividing it into substance and accident. The author calls this 'تحديد مقسم' (divisive definition), i.e., defining a genus by its species. He argues that this is invalid because a proper definition must be comprehensive (جامع) and mutually exclusive (مانع). Here, the two descriptions (substance and accident) cannot be combined in a single subject, so the definition is not comprehensive. Moreover, the definition does not actually define existence itself but divides it. He references al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge to illustrate a similar error. The author notes that this objection only holds according to the principles of Ahl al-Haqq (the Ash'ari/Maturidi school), not for the Mu'tazila who permit divisive definitions. Thus, the Mu'tazila cannot reject the Christians' claim that God is a substance using this argument.

Key Terms
تحديد مقسم
جامع
مانع
العلم
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dividing existence into substance and accident is a valid definition of existence.
Correction: The author shows it is a divisive definition (تحديد مقسم) that fails the condition of comprehensiveness (جامع), because the two descriptions cannot be combined in a single subject and assigning them separately does not provide a unified definition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the definition of existence as substance or accident to be corrupt?
A) Because the two descriptions are not mutually exclusive.
B) Because the definition does not include accidents of existence.
C) Because it is a divisive definition (تحديد مقسم) that cannot be comprehensive.
D) Because the Mu'tazila reject it.

Key Takeaway
A definition that divides a genus into species without a comprehensive principle is corrupt; the definition of existence as substance or accident fails because it cannot be applied to a single existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a definition or categorization about Allah or His attributes that you later realized was inadequate? What in this lesson challenges you to be more careful in how you define or describe your faith, especially when it comes to matters that are beyond full human comprehension?
?
The author says the Mu'tazila, because they permitted divisive definitions, could not refute the Christians' objection. Are there areas in your own beliefs where you tolerate logical inconsistency because it is comfortable or convenient? What would it cost you to adopt a more rigorous approach to theology?
?
You now know that a valid definition must be comprehensive and exclusive. Do you approach your own understanding of God and existence with the same rigor, or do you settle for vague or contradictory descriptions? Will you commit to testing every belief you hold about Allah against the criteria of sound reasoning and textual consistency?

Resolving Apparent Contradictions Between Revelation and Reason
Nasafi refutes anthropomorphic interpretation by prioritizing rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why anthropomorphic scriptural expressions must be interpreted metaphorically to avoid contradiction with definitive rational proofs.

Core Idea
Revelation and reason cannot contradict; ambiguous texts yield to decisive proofs.

Imam al-Nasafi addresses a common challenge: how to handle verses and hadith that seem to describe God in physical terms. He points out that these texts are inherently open to interpretation, whereas rational proofs are unambiguous. Therefore, the literal meaning cannot be taken at face value, as that would pit revelation against reason, both of which are divine proofs.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the premise
Nasafi begins by stating that solving this issue requires a premise: scriptural language can have multiple meanings, while rational proofs are clear. Understand that this premise is the key to the argument.
The premise is that scriptural texts are muhtamila (capable of interpretation) and rational proofs are not.
2
Identify the two types of proofs
Notice the distinction: the words in the Book and Sunna might imply anthropomorphism, but they are open to ta'wil. In contrast, the rational proofs (huquq 'aqliyya) are decisive. Reason itself is a means of knowledge and a proof from God.
Intellects are among the causes of knowledge and are God's proof upon creation.
3
Understand the consequence of literalism
If you take these verses literally, you establish a contradiction between the Book and rational proofs. But both are proofs of God, and God's proofs cannot conflict. Contradiction would imply foolishness and ignorance in God, which is impossible.
Therefore, the only sound approach is to interpret the ambiguous in light of the clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما تعلقهم بتلك الشبهة السمعية فنقول : لابد في حلها من تقديم مقدمة فنقول: إن هذه الألفاظ الواردة في الكتاب والسنن المروية التي يوهم ظاهرها التشبيه وكون الباري تعالى جسما متبعضا متجزئا كانت كلها محتملة لمعان وراء الظاهر والحجج المعقولة التي بيناها غير محتملة، والعقول من أسباب المعارف، وهي حجة الله تعالى. وفي حمل هذه الآيات على ظواهرها على ما حملت المجسمة والمشبهة إثبات المناقضة بين الكتاب والدلائل المعقولة، وهي كلها حجج الله تعالى ومن تناقضت حججه فهو سفيه جاهل بما حد الحجج ومقاديرها، والله تعالى حكيم لا يجوز عليه السفه، عالم لا يجهل ولو حملت هذه الآيات على ما يوافق حجج الله
As for their attachment to that auditory doubt, we say: In its resolution a premise must be presented. So we say: These words occurring in the Book and the transmitted Sunna, whose apparent meaning suggests anthropomorphism and that the Exalted Creator is a body composed of parts and divisible—all of them are capable of meanings beyond the apparent, while the rational proofs we have presented are not capable [of alternative interpretation]. And intellects are among the means of knowledge, and they are God's proof. And in interpreting these verses according to their apparent meanings, as the anthropomorphists and assimilationists have interpreted them, is to establish contradiction between the Book and the rational proofs—and both are proofs of God Almighty. And whoever's proofs are contradictory is foolish, ignorant of the limits and measures of proofs. And God Almighty is wise, foolishness is not permissible for Him, Knowing, He does not ignore. And if these verses were interpreted in accordance with God's proofs...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage refutes the anthropomorphic interpretation of scriptural texts (shubha sam'iyya). The author begins by stating that solving this issue requires a premise: the words in the Qur'an and Sunna that seem to imply resemblance (tashbīh) or that God is a body composed of parts (mutabayyiḍ mutajazzi') are all capable (muḥtamila) of interpretations (ta'wīl) beyond the literal meaning. In contrast, the rational proofs ('aqliyya) that the author has presented are not capable of alternative interpretation—they are decisive. Reason (ʿuqūl) is one of the means (asbāb) of knowledge, and it is God's proof (ḥujja) upon creation. Therefore, to take these scriptural expressions literally, as the anthropomorphists (mujassima) and assimilationists (mushabbiha) do, results in a contradiction between revelation and reason. But both the Book and rational proofs are proofs from God, and God's proofs cannot contradict each other. Contradiction in one's proofs is a sign of foolishness (safah) and ignorance (jahl). God is wise (ḥakīm) and not foolish, and He is knowing (ʿālim) and does not ignore. Thus, the correct approach is to interpret these ambiguous verses in a way that harmonizes with the definitive rational proofs, preserving the transcendence of God.

Key Terms
الشبهة السمعية
التشبيه
المجسمة
المشبهة
الحجج المعقولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that the apparent meaning of scripture must always be accepted without question, even if it implies anthropomorphism.
Correction: Nasafi argues that scriptural texts are open to interpretation while rational proofs are decisive; taking the literal meaning would create a contradiction between revelation and reason, which is impossible since both are from God.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, why is it incorrect to take anthropomorphic verses in the Qur'an literally?
A) Because the verses are not authentic.
B) Because reason is the only source of knowledge.
C) Because it would create a contradiction between revelation and rational proofs.
D) Because the Companions never understood them literally.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural expressions suggesting anthropomorphism must be interpreted figuratively to maintain consistency with rational proofs, which are definitive divine proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse or hadith that seems to describe God in human-like terms, do you instinctively take it literally, or do you pause to consider that your understanding might be limited? Will you commit to seeking the interpretation that aligns with definitive rational proofs as taught by the scholars?
?
The author says that contradictory proofs indicate foolishness. In your own life, when you find your understanding of scripture conflicting with clear rational truths, do you tend to dismiss reason or twist scripture? How will you ensure you are not among those who ignorantly set God's proofs against each other?
?
Are you willing to trust that God's revelation and reason are in harmony, even when your initial understanding seems at odds? What is one ambiguous text you currently struggle with, and how will you apply this lesson's principle to resolve it?

The Salaf's Methodology of Tafwid Regarding Ambiguous Verses
Discussion of Salaf's tafwid methodology for mutashabihat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Salaf's methodology of tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah) for ambiguous verses and hadiths, and how major scholars applied it.

Core Idea
The Salaf affirmed the attributes but consigned the 'how' to Allah.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents the methodology of the early pious scholars (Salaf) when dealing with verses and hadiths whose apparent meanings seem to imply anthropomorphism. It explains the principle of imrar (passing them as they came) and tafwid (entrusting the true modality to Allah), and lists many distinguished scholars who adhered to this approach, including the famous statement of Imam Malik on the verse of istiwa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how it begins with a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars and then presents the chosen methodology.
Pay attention to the chain of transmission from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan.
2
Identify the core methodology
Find the key phrases: 'numurruhā kamā jā'at' and 'nūminu bihā wa lā naqūlu kayf'. Understand that this is the Salaf's way of handling mutashabihat.
This approach is called tafwid and imrar.
3
Note the list of scholars
Observe the long list of scholars who followed this method, from Hanafi imams to hadith masters. This shows the widespread acceptance of tafwid.
Mālik's statement is highlighted as a key quote.
4
Analyze Malik's statement
Reflect on Malik's three-part response: 'al-istiwa ghayr majhul, wa al-kayf ghayr ma'qul, wa al-su'al 'anhu bid'a'. What does each part mean?
This encapsulates the Salaf's position: they affirm the attribute but deny knowing its modality.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question about Malik's statement. Then consider the misconception that tafwid means complete ignorance.
The passage shows the Salaf did affirm a real meaning (istiwa is not unknown).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ه ــ الكلام على الآيات المتشابهة والأحاديث المتشابهة: ثم بعد ذلك اختلف مشايخنا رحمهم الله. فذهب بعضهم إلى أن الواجب في هذه الآيات والأحاديث أن يتلقى ما ورد من ذلك بالإيمان به والتسليم له والاعتقاد بصحته، وأن لا نشتغل بكيفيته والبحث عنه اعتقادنا أن الله تعالى مع ليس بجسم ولا شبيه بالمخلوقات، وأن جميع أمارات الحدث عنه منتفية، روي ذلك عن محمد بن الحسن، فإن نصير بن يحيى البلخي روي عن عمر بن إسماعيل بن حماد بن أبي حنيفة عن محمد بن الحسن رحمهم الله أنه سئل عن الآيات والأخبار التي فيها من صفات الله تعالى ما يؤدي ظاهره إلى التشبيه فقال: نمرها كما جاءت ونؤمن بها ولا نقول كيف وكيف، وإليه ذهب من أصحابنا رحمهم الله أبو عصمة سعد بن معاذ المروزي، وإليه ذهب أيضا مالك بن أنس إمام أهل المدينة، وعبد الله بن المبارك، وأبو معاذ خالد بن سليمان صاحب سفيان الثوري وجماعة أهل الحديث كأحمد بن حنبل وإسحاق ابن راهويه، ومحمد بن إسماعيل البخاري وأبي داود السجستاني وحكي عن مالك بن أنس أنه سئل عن قوله تعالى: الرحمن على العرش استوى فقال: الاستواء غير مجهول، والكيف غير معقول، والسؤال عنه بدعة، فلم يشتغل أحد من هؤلاء بتأويل شيء من هذه الآيات والأخبار.
H — Discussion of the ambiguous verses and the ambiguous hadiths: Then after that, our scholars (mashāyikhunā) — may Allah have mercy on them — differed. Some of them went to that what is obligatory regarding these verses and hadiths is to receive what has come of that with faith in it, submission to it, and belief in its correctness, and that we do not occupy ourselves with its modality (kayfiyya) and investigation, while we believe that Allah the Exalted is not a body (jism) nor similar to creatures, and that all indicators of temporality (ḥudūth) are negated from Him. That was narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan. For Nuṣayr ibn Yaḥyā al-Balkhī narrated from ‘Umar ibn Ismā‘īl ibn Ḥammād ibn Abī Ḥanīfa from Muhammad ibn al-Ḥasan — may Allah have mercy on them — that he was asked about the verses and reports in which there are attributes of Allah the Exalted whose apparent meaning leads to anthropomorphism (tashbīh), and he said: 'We pass them as they came (numurruhā kamā jā’at), and we believe in them, and we do not say how (kayf) — how.' And to this view went, from our companions — may Allah have mercy on them — Abū ‘Iṣma Sa‘d ibn Mu‘ādh al-Marwazī, and to this view also went Mālik ibn Anas, the imam of the people of Medina, and ‘Abdullāh ibn al-Mubārak, and Abū Mu‘ādh Khālid ibn Sulaymān the companion of Sufyān al-Thawrī, and the group of people of hadith (jamā‘at ahl al-ḥadīth) such as Aḥmad ibn Ḥanbal, and Isḥāq ibn Rāhawayh, and Muḥammad ibn Ismā‘īl al-Bukhārī, and Abū Dāwūd al-Sijistānī. And it was narrated from Mālik ibn Anas that he was asked about the saying of the Exalted: 'The Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne' (Quran 20:5). He said: 'The 'istiwa' is not unknown (ghayr majhūl), and the modality (kayf) is not comprehensible (ghayr ma‘qūl), and asking about it is innovation (bid‘a).' So none of these occupied themselves with interpreting (ta’wīl) any of these verses and reports.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the methodology of the Salaf (early pious scholars) concerning the ambiguous verses and hadiths (mutashābihāt). It begins by noting a difference of opinion among the Hanafi scholars (mashāyikhunā). One group held that the obligatory approach is to receive these texts with faith (īmān), submission (taslīm), and belief in their correctness (i‘tiqād bi-ṣiḥḥatihi), without delving into how (kayfiyya) or investigating them. This is coupled with the foundational belief that Allah is not a body (jism) nor similar to creatures, and that all indicators of temporality (amārāt al-ḥudūth) are negated from Him. The author then traces this view back to Muhammad ibn al-Ḥasan al-Shaybānī (the famous Hanafi jurist) through a chain of transmission. Muhammad ibn al-Ḥasan, when asked about verses whose apparent meaning leads to anthropomorphism (tashbīh), replied: 'We pass them as they came (numurruhā kamā jā’at), and we believe in them, and we do not say how, how.' This is the classic formulation of tafwīḍ (consigning the meaning to God) and imrār (passing without interpretation). The passage then lists other scholars who adopted the same method: Abū ‘Iṣma Sa‘d ibn Mu‘ādh al-Marwazī, Mālik ibn Anas (the founder of the Maliki school), ‘Abdullāh ibn al-Mubārak, Abū Mu‘ādh Khālid ibn Sulaymān, and the major hadith scholars such as Aḥmad ibn Ḥanbal, Isḥāq ibn Rāhawayh, al-Bukhārī, and Abū Dāwūd. The passage concludes with the famous statement of Mālik ibn Anas regarding the verse 'al-Raḥmān ‘alā al-‘arshi istawā': 'The istiwā' is not unknown, the modality (kayf) is not comprehensible, and asking about it is innovation (bid‘a).' This succinctly captures the Salafī position: they affirm the attribute (istiwa) as known in language but refrain from explaining how it applies to God, considering inquiry into modality an innovation. Thus, none of these scholars engaged in ta’wīl (interpretation) of these texts. The passage serves as evidence for the Maturidi school's adoption of tafwīḍ as a valid method, though other methodologies (such as ta’wīl) are also discussed elsewhere.

Key Terms
متشابه (mutashābih)
إمرار (imrār)
تفويض (tafwīḍ)
تأويل (ta’wīl)
كيفية (kayfiyya)
تشبيه (tashbīh)
بدعة (bid‘a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that tafwid means complete ignorance of the meaning, that the Salaf had no understanding at all.
Correction: The Salaf did affirm a real meaning for attributes like istiwa (as Mālik said, 'al-istiwa ghayr majhūl'), but they consigned the modality (kayf) to Allah, refraining from further speculation.

Checkpoint
What did Mālik ibn Anas say when asked about the verse 'al-Raḥmān ‘alā al-‘arshi istawā'?
A) The istiwā' is unknown and cannot be discussed.
B) The istiwā' is not unknown, the modality is not comprehensible, and asking about it is innovation.
C) The istiwā' means sitting on the Throne, but we do not know how.
D) The istiwā' must be interpreted metaphorically to avoid anthropomorphism.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf's methodology for ambiguous verses is to affirm the attribute, believe in it, and consign the modality to Allah without interpretation or inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse about Allah's attributes that challenges your understanding, does your heart feel at peace with the phrase 'we do not say how, how'? Do you genuinely trust that Allah knows best, or do you find yourself needing to rationalise it before you can accept it?
?
Malik declared that asking about the modality of istiwa is an innovation. Have you ever insisted on understanding 'how' an attribute applies to Allah, and what does that say about your humility before His incomparability?
?
The great imams of the past—Malik, Ahmad, Bukhari—all submitted to the text without seeking allegorical interpretation. Is your faith strong enough to imitate them, or do you find yourself demanding explanations that make you more comfortable? What would it cost you to simply say: 'I believe, and I consign the how to Allah'?

The Interpretive Method of Ahl al-Ta'wil
Defines the methodology of ahl al-ta'wil for interpreting ambiguous texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodology of ahl al-ta'wil for handling ambiguous Quranic verses and prophetic reports, including when they accept the apparent meaning and when they refrain from specifying.

Core Idea
Ahl al-ta'wil only accept the apparent meaning when no figurative interpretation is compatible with tawhid.

This passage introduces the methodology of a group known as ahl al-ta'wil (those who engage in interpretive allegorization). They deal with ambiguous texts by seeking interpretations that do not contradict the clear proofs of tawhid and the decisive verses. The author explains their two-step approach: when a text admits only one interpretation compatible with tawhid, they accept it; when it admits many, they refrain from choosing one without evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the author's description of the methodology. Notice the conditional structure.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Look for the conditional phrases: 'then whatever of that does not admit anything but the apparent... they definitively assert' and 'whatever admits many interpretations... they do not definitively assert.' Understand the distinction.
The absence of evidence specifies which interpretation is intended.
3
Examine the example of 'yad'
See how they list multiple possible meanings for 'hand' (strength, power, authority, etc.) to avoid anthropomorphism. Note that they attribute the word to Allah in a way that does not contradict tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعضهم اشتغل بصرف هذه الآيات والأخبار إلى ما يحتمل من الوجوه التي لا تناقض دلائل التوحيد والآيات المحكمة، ثم ما كان من ذلك لا يحتمل سوى الظاهر ولا تأويلا واحدا ملائما للتوحيد ودلائله قطعوا على كونه مراد الله، وما يحتمل من ذلك تأويلات كثيرة يلائم كل واحد منها ما يثبت من الدلائل لم يقطعوا على واحد منها بكونه مرادا لانعدام دليل يوجب تعيين ذلك فامتنعوا عن الشهادة على الله تعالى عند انعدام الدليل الموجب للعلم، وقالوا: نعم إن المراد بعض تلك الوجوه لا الظاهر. فقالوا: إن اليد تذكر ويراد بها القوة والقدرة والسلطنة والمملكة والحجة والغلبة واليسر والعز والغنى والكف والجارحة، فيضاف إلى الله تعالى ما لا يناقض التوحيد.
And some of them occupied themselves with diverting these verses and reports to the possible aspects (al-wujuh) that do not contradict the proofs of tawhid and the decisive verses (al-ayat al-muhkamat). Then, whatever of that does not admit anything but the apparent (al-zahir) and does not have a single interpretation (ta'wil) compatible with tawhid and its proofs, they definitively assert that it is the intended meaning of Allah. And whatever of that admits many interpretations, each of which is compatible with what is established from the proofs, they do not definitively assert any one of them to be the intended meaning, because of the absence of evidence that necessitates its specification. So they refrained from testifying concerning Allah Almighty in the absence of evidence necessitating knowledge. And they said: Yes, the intended meaning is some of those aspects, not the apparent. So they said: The hand (al-yad) is mentioned and by it are meant strength, power, authority, sovereignty, proof, dominance, ease, might, self-sufficiency, palm, and limb; so it is attributed to Allah Almighty in a way that does not contradict tawhid.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage defines the methodology of the group known as ahl al-ta'wil (those who engage in interpretive allegorization) concerning ambiguous verses (mutashabihat). The author presents their approach in a conditional manner. First, if a text admits only its apparent meaning (zahir) and there is no single interpretation compatible with tawhid, they accept the apparent as intended. Second, if a text admits many possible interpretations, each compatible with tawhid, they do not pick one arbitrarily because there is no evidence to specify which is intended. They refrain from swearing that a particular meaning is Allah's intent. They affirm that the intended meaning is some of those possible aspects, not the literal apparent. As an example, they list many possible meanings of 'yad' (hand) in Arabic—such as strength, power, authority, etc.—and say that it is attributed to Allah in a way that does not contradict tawhid. This shows their method of figurative interpretation to protect tawhid.

Key Terms
التأويل (al-ta'wil)
الظاهر (al-zahir)
الآيات المحكمة (al-ayat al-muhkamat)
الوجوه (al-wujuh)
اليد (al-yad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that ahl al-ta'wil reject all apparent meanings, always insisting on figurative interpretation.
Correction: In fact, they accept the apparent meaning when there is no compatible figurative interpretation. They only resort to multiple possibilities when the text admits them.

Checkpoint
According to the ahl al-ta'wil, when do they definitively assert that a particular interpretation is the intended meaning?
A) When the text has many possible interpretations.
B) When the text has only one possible interpretation compatible with tawhid and no other.
C) When the literal meaning is unambiguous.
D) When the text is a hadith.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-ta'wil follow a disciplined method: they accept the apparent only when there is no compatible figurative meaning, and otherwise refrain from specifying.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse or hadith that seems to attribute a human quality to Allah, do you pause and ask whether there is a compatible figurative meaning, or do you immediately reject the text or anthropomorphize? What cost does your instinct carry for your tawhid?
?
The ahl al-ta'wil refrained from testifying about Allah's intent without evidence. Reflect on a time you were certain about Allah's will without clear proof. How can you train yourself to say 'Allahu a'lam' when the evidence is not decisive?
?
The passage shows that accepting multiple possible interpretations is better than insisting on one without proof. Is there a theological issue where you have held stubbornly to one opinion despite lacking decisive evidence? What would it take to release your certainty and trust that Allah's intent is broader?

The Status of Isolated Reports in Creedal Matters
The author argues that isolated reports (khabar al-ahad) are not decisive in creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why isolated reports (khabar al-ahad) are not considered authoritative in matters of creed according to Maturidi methodology.

Core Idea
Isolated reports cannot produce certainty, so they cannot be used as proof in creed.

This passage addresses a contentious issue in Islamic theology: can isolated reports (khabar al-ahad) be used as proof in creedal matters? The author firmly argues that they cannot, because they do not yield certain knowledge. He further notes that when such reports conflict with rational proofs or definitive Quranic texts, their weakness becomes even more apparent. Understanding this principle is essential for grasping the Maturidi approach to theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the author's argument.
The passage is dense; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the main claim
What is the author's primary assertion about isolated reports? Look for the phrase 'لا توجب العلم' (they do not necessitate knowledge).
3
Understand the rationale
The author gives two reasons: even without opposition, isolated reports lack certainty; and if they contradict reason or clear revelation (al-nash al-muhkam), they are even weaker.
Consider why certainty is required in creedal matters.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this principle affects the use of hadith in theology versus jurisprudence.
In fiqh, isolated reports can be used as evidence, but in 'aqida, they are not sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما تعلقهم بالأخبار فأكثرها وردت مورد الآحاد، وهي غير موجبة للعلم لو خلت عن المعارض فلم يكن الاحتجاج بها في باب العقائد والديانات، فكيف ما ورد معارضا لدلائل العقول والنص المحكم من الكتاب على أن ما ظهر منها بين النقلة فكل ذلك محتمل للتأويل.
As for their reliance on reports, most of them came as isolated reports (khabar al-ahad), and they do not necessitate knowledge even if they were free of opposition, so they cannot be used as proof in the matters of creed and religions, so how about when they come in opposition to rational proofs and the definitive text (al-nash al-muhkam) of the Book? Moreover, what appears among the transmitters is all subject to interpretation.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an argument against using isolated reports (akhbar al-ahad) as proof in theological matters. The author states that even if such reports were free of contradictions, they do not produce certainty ('ilm). Therefore they cannot be relied upon in matters of 'aqa'id (creeds) and religions, which require certain knowledge. If they also contradict rational proofs (dala'il al-'uqul) or the definitive Quranic text (al-nash al-muhkam), they are even less acceptable. Additionally, the reports that appear among transmitters are all subject to interpretation (ta'wil), implying their meanings can be reconciled with established truths through allegorical interpretation. This reflects the Maturidi methodology that reason and clear revelation take precedence over single-narrator traditions in foundational beliefs.

Key Terms
خبر الآحاد
العلم
العقائد
الدلائل العقلية
النص المحكم
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any authentic hadith can be used as proof in theology.
Correction: Isolated reports do not produce certainty and cannot override reason and clear revelation.

Checkpoint
Why are isolated reports (khabar al-ahad) not authoritative in matters of creed according to the passage?
A) They are always fabricated
B) They do not produce certainty
C) They contradict the Qur'an
D) They are only about legal matters

Key Takeaway
Isolated reports (khabar al-ahad) do not produce certain knowledge and therefore cannot be used as evidence in matters of creed, especially when they contradict rational proofs or definitive Quranic texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

This lesson teaches that isolated reports are open to interpretation (ta'wil). How often have you forced a reconciliation that strains your intellect? Are you willing to let go of a cherished opinion if it lacks certainty?
?
The author says isolated reports do not produce knowledge even if free from opposition. This means certainty is reserved for decisive proofs. Are you comfortable living with uncertainty in some matters of faith, or do you demand absolute proof for every detail? What does that say about your reliance on Allah's guidance through clear revelation?

The Invalidity of Solitary Reports in Creed
Nasafi explains why akhbar al-ahad cannot be used in creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) cannot serve as evidence in matters of creed due to their lack of certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Solitary reports never yield certainty, so they cannot ground belief.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a key methodological issue: the use of hadith reports in theology. He distinguishes between reports that produce certain knowledge and those that do not. Solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) fall into the latter category, and therefore cannot be used to establish creedal beliefs, especially when they contradict rational proofs or decisive Quranic texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's concise argument come through.
Notice Nasafi's emphatic style: 'فكيف ما ورد معارضا...'
2
Identify the key claim
Find the statement that akhbar al-ahad are 'ghayr mujibat li-l-ilm' (not productive of knowledge) even when free from opposition.
This is the epistemological foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the implication
Recognize that because they lack certainty, they cannot be used in creed. Also note the additional reasons: opposition to rational proofs and the decisive text, and susceptibility to interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما تعلقهم بالأخبار فأكثرها وردت مورد الآحاد، وهي غير موجبة للعلم لو خلت عن المعارض فلم يكن الاحتجاج بها في باب العقائد والديانات، فكيف ما ورد معارضا لدلائل العقول والنص المحكم من الكتاب على أن ما ظهر منها بين النقلة فكل ذلك محتمل للتأويل.
As for their reliance on reports, most of them come as solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad). They do not produce knowledge even if free from opposition, so they cannot be used as proof in matters of creed and religion. How then [can they be used] when they occur opposing rational proofs and the decisive text of the Book, and whatever appears among the transmitters is all subject to interpretation (ta'wil).
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage defines the epistemological status of solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) in theological matters. The author states that such reports, even when uncontradicted, do not yield certain knowledge (laysat mujibat li-l-ilm). Therefore they cannot serve as evidence in matters of creed (aqa'id) and religion (diyanat). The argument is strengthened by noting that when these reports contradict rational proofs (dala'il al-uqul) and the decisive text (al-nass al-muhkam) of the Quran, they are even less acceptable. The author concludes that any such report appearing among transmitters is open to interpretation and cannot be taken literally.

Misconception: Any authentic hadith can be used as proof in creed unconditionally, regardless of its certainty level.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that even authentic solitary reports do not produce certain knowledge and thus cannot be used in matters of creed, especially when they contradict rational proofs or decisive Quranic texts.

Key Takeaway
Solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) do not yield certain knowledge and therefore cannot serve as evidence in matters of creed, especially when contradicted by reason or decisive revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you hear a hadith that comforts you or confirms your desires, do you instinctively accept it as true without asking whether it reaches the level of certainty required for creed? Is your faith strong enough to withstand that question?
?
If you applied Nasafi's standard to every belief you hold about Allah, the afterlife, and the unseen, how many of them would survive? Are you willing to let go of any belief that rests on uncertain ground, even if it feels precious to you?

Scholars of Ta'wil and Their Works
Sanusi references early and later scholars of ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tradition of ta'wil literature and the key scholars Sanusi references, as well as why he directs readers to their works.

Core Idea
Ta'wil of ambiguous hadith has a rich scholarly tradition.

In this brief passage, Sanusi names scholars from both early and later periods who wrote comprehensive works on the interpretation of ambiguous hadith. He advises those seeking thorough knowledge to consult those specialized books, as his own work is concise and cannot cover the topic in detail. This lesson introduces the scholars and the significance of their contributions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi names two groups: early scholars (المتقدمون) and later scholars (المتأخرون).
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic, so read it slowly.
2
Identify the scholars mentioned
Find the names: Muhammad ibn Shuja' al-Balkhi from the early period, and Abu Bakr ibn Furak from the later period. Note that 'others' are also mentioned among the early scholars.
These are two key figures in the field of ta'wil.
3
Understand Sanusi's purpose
Reflect on why Sanusi directs readers to these books. He acknowledges the existing literature and humbly states that his own book cannot cover ta'wil exhaustively.
This shows scholarly integrity and proper referencing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد صنف المتقدمون في تأويل الأخبار المتشابهة منهم محمد بن شجاع البلخي وغير وصنف من المتأخرين أبو بكر محمد بن الحسن بن فورك فيه كتابا جامعا فمن أراد الاستقصاء في معرفة ذلك فعليه بالرجوع إلى تلك الكتب إذ كتابنا هذا لا يحتمل المبالغة في بيان ذلك، وبالله التوفيق.
And the early scholars authored concerning the interpretation of ambiguous reports, among them Muhammad ibn Shuja' al-Balkhi and others. And among the later scholars, Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Furak authored a comprehensive book on it. So whoever wants thoroughness in knowing that should refer to those books, for this book of ours does not allow exaggeration in explaining that. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This is a brief note where the author references previous works on interpreting ambiguous hadith. He mentions Muhammad ibn Shuja' al-Balkhi from the early scholars and Abu Bakr ibn Furak from later ones. He advises readers to consult those specialized books for a comprehensive treatment, as his own book cannot cover the topic in detail. This shows the author's awareness of existing literature and his choice to keep his work concise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Sanusi dismisses the topic of ta'wil as unimportant.
Correction: Sanusi actually values ta'wil but directs readers to specialized works due to the limitations of his own book.

Checkpoint
Who among the following is mentioned as a later scholar who wrote a comprehensive book on ta'wil?
A) Muhammad ibn Shuja' al-Balkhi
B) Abu Bakr ibn Furak
C) Al-Maturidi
D) Al-Ash'ari

Key Takeaway
Sanusi directs readers to specialized works on ta'wil by early and later scholars, showing the depth and continuity of the tradition.

?
Sanusi humbly directs readers to more comprehensive books. Do you have the same humility in your learning, or do you rely on only one source? Which book will you commit to studying?
?
The tradition of ta'wil spans centuries. Does your own study of aqeedah reflect this depth, or do you limit yourself to modern summaries? What specific scholarly work will you read to deepen your understanding?

Interpreting Ambiguous Hadith and Understanding Divine Attributes
Author discusses ta'wil of mutashabih hadith and clarifies divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method of interpreting ambiguous hadith (mutashabih) and the correct understanding of divine attributes as qualities of majesty rather than physical properties.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphic descriptions in hadith require interpretation, not literal reading.

In this passage, the author addresses a crucial issue in Islamic theology: how to understand hadith that seem to describe Allah in human terms. He provides an example of interpreting such a report and then lays down a key principle about divine attributes like greatness and pride — they refer to majesty, not physical dimensions. This lesson will guide you through his method and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the literal translation. Notice the two main parts: the interpretation of the laughter hadith, and the explanation of attributes.
Pay attention to the terms 'ta'wil' and 'mutashabih'.
2
Identify the interpretive method
The author gives an example of interpreting a hadith about laughter. He says the interpretation is that Allah manifests pleasure and reward. Then he refers to books by early scholars on interpreting ambiguous reports. This shows that such interpretations are established.
He does not go into detail here, but he points to sources.
3
Understand the principle for attributes
The author states that attributes like 'azama and kibriya are from the perspective of majesty and highness, not thickness and hugeness. He argues that these are attributes of praise, and praise is based on such meanings, not on having large parts. This refutes the Mujassima who take them literally.
Consider how this principle applies to other attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبالغ في ضحكه أنه يظهر ناجذيه، وإذا بالغ في ذلك فكان تأويل الخبر: إظهار الله تعالى رضاه وثوابه على أوليائه. وقد صنف المتقدمون في تأويل الأخبار المتشابهة منهم محمد بن شجاع البلخي وغير وصنف من المتأخرين أبو بكر محمد بن الحسن بن فورك فيه كتابا جامعا فمن أراد الاستقصاء في معرفة ذلك فعليه بالرجوع إلى تلك الكتب إذ كتابنا هذا لا يحتمل المبالغة في بيان ذلك، وبالله التوفيق. وما يوصف الله تعالى به من العظمة والكبرياء فذاك من حيث الجلال والرفعة لا من حيث الغلظ والضخامة بدليل أنهما من صفات المدح، والمدح يكون بما ذكرنا لا بكثرة الأجزاء فيبطل تعلق المجسمة به والله الموفق.
He exaggerates in his laughter to the point that his molars appear, and when he exaggerates in that, the interpretation of the report is: Allah Almighty manifesting His pleasure and reward upon His friends. And the early scholars authored works on the interpretation of ambiguous reports, among them Muhammad ibn Shuja' al-Balkhi and others, and among the later scholars Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Furak authored a comprehensive book on it. So whoever desires thoroughness in knowing that, let him refer to those books, for this book of ours cannot bear going into detail in explaining that. And success is from Allah. And whatever Allah is described with of greatness and pride, that is from the perspective of majesty and highness, not from the perspective of thickness and hugeness, as evidence that they are attributes of praise, and praise is with what we mentioned, not with multitude of parts. So the attachment of the Mujassima to it is invalidated. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note on interpreting anthropomorphic hadith. The author first gives an example of a hadith about laughter showing molars, interpreting it as Allah's manifestation of pleasure and reward to His friends. He then mentions that early scholars like Muhammad ibn Shuja' al-Balkhi and later Abu Bakr ibn Furak wrote comprehensive works on the interpretation of ambiguous reports (ta'wil al-akhbar al-mutashabiha). The author advises referring to those books, as his own work cannot go into detail. He then clarifies that the attributes of greatness (azama) and pride (kibriya) ascribed to Allah are from the perspective of majesty (jalal) and highness (rif'a), not from physical thickness (ghilaz) or hugeness (dakhama), because these are attributes of praise, and praise is based on such meanings, not on multitude of parts. This refutes the Mujassima (anthropomorphists) who take these attributes literally.

Key Takeaway
The correct understanding of divine attributes is that they are qualities of majesty, not physical properties, and ambiguous hadith require symbolic interpretation.

The Principle of Restricting Divine Names to Revelation
Explanation of the principle that God is named only with what the Shari'ah has specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi scholars restrict naming Allah to only those names found in the Shari'ah, and why names like 'physician' or 'body' are not used.

Core Idea
We name Allah only with what He has named Himself.

This passage introduces a key Maturidi methodological principle: tawqīf—that divine names are based solely on revelation. The author explains that even if a meaning is true of Allah (like being free from illness), we do not invent a name unless the Law has taught it. This guards against speculative theology and preserves the boundaries set by revelation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of tawqīf
Read the opening sentence where the author states that we restrict ourselves in naming Allah to what the Law has indicated. This is the core methodological stance.
Tawqīf literally means 'stopping'—we stop where revelation stops.
2
Examine the examples: sound, physician, jurist
The author lists three names (ṣaḥīḥ, ṭabīb, faqīh) that are not used for Allah even though the meanings are true. Reflect on why each meaning is true but the name is not given.
These examples show that having the attribute does not automatically give permission to name.
3
Consider the case of 'body' and the added impossibility
The author argues that calling Allah 'body' is even more prohibited because not only is there no scriptural basis or consensus, but the meaning (composition) is impossible for Allah.
This adds a second layer: impossibility of meaning strengthens the prohibition.
4
Contrast with permissible names like 'Existent' and 'Eternal'
The author notes that names like 'al-mawjūd' and 'al-qadīm' are permitted because consensus (ijmā') supports them, and consensus acts like a revealed text.
Consensus (ijmā') is considered a source of tawqīf in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحت هذا الكلام إشارة إلى ابتداء طريقة لنا في المسألة اعتمد عليها كثير من أصحابنا، وهي أنا ننتهي في أسماء الله تعالى إلى ما أنهانا إليه الشرع، ألا ترى أنا لا نسميه صحيحا، وإن كانت الآفات والأسقام عنه منتفية، ولا طبيبا وإن كان عالما بالأدواء والعلل والأدوية الموضوعة لها، ولا فقيها وإن كان عالما بالأحكام وبما لها من المعاني. والشرع لم يرد بتسميته جسما فإن التوقيف في الكتاب والسنة منعدم، والإجماع غير منعقد، فلا يجوز تسميته به كما في النظائر التي مر ذكرها بل أولى، فإن هناك منع الاسم لعدم التوقيف والإجماع، وإن كانت المعاني التي لها وضعت تلك الألفاظ ثابتة، وفيما نحن فيه انعدم التوقيف والإجماع، واستحالة إثبات ما وضع له اسم الجسم وهو التألف والتركب على القديم جل ثناؤه، ثم تلك الأسماء كما منعت فهذا أولى، وهذا بخلاف الموجود والقديم، لأن الإجماع منعقد على جواز إطلاقها على الله تعالى، والإجماع توقيف بمنزلة النص فجوزنا ذلك وفيما نحن فيه الأمر بخلافه.
And under this speech is an indication of the beginning of a method for us concerning the issue, relied upon by many of our companions, which is that we restrict ourselves in the names of Allah to what the Law has indicated to us. Do you not see that we do not call Him 'sound' (ṣaḥīḥ) even though afflictions and illnesses are negated from Him, nor 'physician' (ṭabīb) even though He is knowledgeable of diseases, ailments, and the remedies prescribed for them, nor 'jurist' (faqīh) even though He is knowledgeable of rulings and of their meanings. The Law has not come with calling Him a body, for the specification (tawqīf) in the Book and the Sunnah is absent, and consensus is not established, so it is not permissible to name Him with it, just as in the analogous cases mentioned earlier—rather it is more so. For there, the name was prevented due to absence of specification and consensus, even though the meanings for which those words were coined are established; whereas in our case, specification and consensus are absent, and it is impossible to affirm the meaning for which the name 'body' was coined, which is composition (ta’alluf) and structure (tarkīb), for the Eternal, exalted be His praise. Then those names are prevented, so this is more so. And this is unlike 'the Existent' (al-mawjūd) and 'the Eternal' (al-qadīm), because consensus is established upon the permissibility of applying them to Allah, and consensus is like a specification in the position of a text, so we permitted that. But in our case, the matter is otherwise.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a methodological exposition. The author introduces a key principle of Maturidi theology: we only name God with what the Shari'ah has taught us. He gives examples: we do not call God 'sound' (even though He is free from defects), 'physician' (even though He knows all diseases), or 'jurist' (even though He knows the law), because these names were not used in revelation. Similarly, 'body' is not used because there is no scriptural basis, no consensus, and the meaning (composition) is impossible for God. The author contrasts this with names like 'Existent' and 'Eternal', which are permitted because consensus (which acts like a revealed text) supports them. The principle is that absence of revelation and consensus prevents the name, especially when the meaning is also impossible.

Key Terms
أَسْمَاء اللَّه
شَرْع
تَوْقِيف
إِجْمَاع
صَحِيح
طَبِيب
فَقِيه
تَرْكِيب
تَأَلُّف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any attribute that is true of God can be used as a name for Him.
Correction: Names must come from revelation (tawqīf) or consensus (ijmā'), not from rational inference alone.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that calling God 'body' is more prohibited than calling Him 'sound' or 'physician'?
A) Because 'body' implies composition, which is impossible for God, in addition to lacking scriptural basis.
B) Because 'sound' and 'physician' are used in the Quran.
C) Because consensus allows 'sound' but not 'body'.
D) Because the early community debated 'body' but agreed on 'sound'.

Key Takeaway
The names of Allah are based on revelation (tawqīf), not on rational inference alone, and consensus (ijmā') acts as a source of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you described Allah with a word you assumed was fine because it felt respectful—like 'Great Planner' or 'Best Friend'. Did you check if that name is revealed? Will you now commit to only using names from the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The author uses 'physician' as a disallowed name even though Allah knows all diseases. What does this tell you about your own tendency to rely on your intellect rather than revelation when speaking about Allah? Are you willing to submit your mind to the text?
?
Consensus (ijmā') is treated like revelation. Is your faith in the collective guidance of the scholars strong enough to override your own reasoning? Where in your life have you rejected a name or attribute for Allah because you thought it made sense, without checking the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah?

Critique of the Term 'Jiha' for God
Nasafi criticizes the use of 'jiha' as an innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that using the term 'jiha' (direction) for God is an innovation that should be avoided, despite agreement on the underlying meaning.

Core Idea
Correct meaning does not justify dangerous wording.

Even when two parties agree on a theological meaning, the choice of words matters. In this passage, Nasafi criticizes those who use the term 'jiha' (direction) for Allah, arguing that its apparent implication leads to disbelief and that there is no scriptural basis for such language. He emphasizes the duty to protect correct meaning by avoiding crude expressions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the rhetorical questions Nasafi poses.
2
Identify the key arguments
Nasafi argues that the disagreement is only in expression, not meaning. Both sides agree God is not located in places. The problem is the 'monstrous expression' that implies disbelief.
3
Understand the principle of safe wording
When revelation is silent, it is obligatory to avoid terms that could lead to false beliefs. Reflect on why this is a fundamental methodological point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقدرة، والتدبير دون الذات فهذا منهم خلاف في العبارة، فأما في المعنى فقد ساعدونا على استحالة تمكنه في الأمكنة، ونحن ساعدناهم أنه عالم بالأمكنة كلها وكلها تدبيره غير أن لهم غنية عن إطلاق هذه العبارة الوحشية في هذا المراد. ومن الذي اضطرهم إلى إطلاق هذه العبارة التي ظاهرها يوجب ما هو كفر وضلال، وأي ضرورة دعتهم إلى ذلك، ولم يرد به نص لا في كتاب، ولا في الأحاديث المشهورة؟ فإذا الواجب علينا عند انعدام النص صيانة هذا المعنى عن هذا اللفظ الوحشي، وبالله النجاة والمعونة.
And power and management without the essence—this from them is a difference in expression. As for meaning, they have agreed with us on the impossibility of His being located in places, and we have agreed with them that He is knowledgeable of all places and all of them are under His management, except that they could do without uttering this monstrous expression for this intended meaning. Who compelled them to utter this expression whose apparent meaning necessitates what is disbelief and misguidance? And what necessity called them to that, when no text has come regarding it, neither in the Book nor in the well-known hadiths? So when the text is absent, it is obligatory upon us to safeguard this meaning from this monstrous wording. And with Allah is deliverance and help.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the opponents' terminology. Nasafi argues that while both parties agree in meaning—God is not spatially located and He knows all places—the opponents use a crude expression (al-ʿibāra al-waḥshiyya) that implies something false. He asks who forced them to use a term whose apparent meaning entails kufr and ḍalāl, especially since there is no scriptural support for it. The conclusion is that when revelation is silent on a wording, the duty is to avoid such dangerous expressions and preserve the correct meaning with safe language.

Key Terms
جهة (jiha)
عبارة (expression)
نص (text/revelation)
كفر (disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that as long as the theological meaning is correct, any wording is acceptable.
Correction: Nasafi warns that even correct meaning can be corrupted by dangerous wording that implies falsehood, especially when there is no scriptural basis. The expression itself must be safe.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is it wrong to use the term 'jiha' (direction) for God?
A) It contradicts the Quran
B) Its apparent meaning implies disbelief and there is no scriptural support
C) It is grammatically incorrect
D) It denies God's knowledge of places

Key Takeaway
When revelation is silent, it is obligatory to avoid terms whose apparent meaning leads to disbelief, even if the intended meaning is correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Critique of Abu Ishaq's Response on Direction
Critique of Abu Ishaq's reply as not answering the question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author, al-Nasafi, criticizes Abu Ishaq's response as not answering the question but merely presenting a new proof.

Core Idea
A valid refutation must answer the question, not start a new argument.

In theological debates, it is not enough to present a valid argument; one must directly address the opponent's objection. Abu Ishaq attempted to refute the claim that God is in a direction, but al-Nasafi points out that he merely initiated a new proof without responding to the specific question. This lesson examines the methodological error and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the author's rhetorical question at the beginning.
2
Identify the core criticism
Find the sentence where al-Nasafi states that Abu Ishaq did not answer the question but started a new proof. This is the main claim.
3
List the three sub-arguments
The passage lists three reasons why direction is impossible for God. Identify each one and note how they are connected.
The three arguments are: limitation leads to division/partition, direction implies composition/connection with bodies, and direction allows encompassing.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider the difference between a direct answer (انفصال) and a new proof (ابتداء دليل). Why does the author insist on this distinction? How does it affect the strength of a refutation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بماذا أجاب. ثم اشتغل أبو إسحاق بالجواب، ولم يأت بما هو انفصال عن هذا السؤال، بل أتى بما هو ابتداء دليل في المسألة، من أنه لو كان بجهة لكان محدودا، وما جاز عليه التحديد جاز الانقسام والتجزؤ. ولأن ما جاز عليه الجهة جاز عليه الوصف والتركيب، وهو أن يتصل به الأجسام، وذا باطل بالإجماع. ولأنه لو جاز عليه الجهة لجازت إحاطة الأجسام به على نحو ما قررنا، وهذا كله ابتداء الدليل وليس بدفع للسؤال.
With what did he answer? Then Abu Ishaq occupied himself with the answer, but he did not bring what is a separation from this question; rather he brought what is an initiation of a proof in the issue: that if He were in a direction, He would be limited, and whatever admits limitation admits division and partition. And because whatever admits direction admits description and composition, namely that bodies connect to Him, and that is invalid by consensus. And because if direction were permissible for Him, it would be permissible for bodies to encompass Him in the manner we established. All of this is initiation of the proof and not a repulsion of the question.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage criticizes Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini's response to the question about direction (jiha) for God. The author, al-Nasafi, points out that Abu Ishaq did not actually answer the question; instead, he began a new proof from scratch. The proof is that if God were in a direction, He would be limited, and what is limited can be divided and partitioned. Additionally, if He were in a direction, He would be described as composite and subject to connection with bodies, which is impossible by consensus. Moreover, allowing direction would allow bodies to encompass Him, which is also impossible. However, all of this, though valid, is merely a new argument, not a direct refutation of the opponent's position. The author therefore shows that the response missed the mark methodologically.

Key Terms
جهة
تحديد
انقسام
تجزؤ
وصف
تركيب
إحاطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any argument against direction is sufficient as a refutation.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the argument must address the opponent's specific objection, not merely present a separate proof.

Checkpoint
What is the author's criticism of Abu Ishaq's response?
A) It was too brief.
B) It did not answer the question but started a new proof.
C) It was based on an invalid argument.
D) It agreed with the opponent.

Key Takeaway
A proper refutation in kalam must directly address the opponent's objection, not merely present a separate proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone questions your beliefs, do you immediately recite a memorized proof, or do you first ensure you have understood their specific doubt? This lesson shows that even a true argument fails if it misses the question. How will you change your approach in the next discussion?
?
Think of a recent theological debate you engaged in. Did you actually answer the person's core concern, or did you fall back on a prepared argument? What did that reveal about your motivation—was it to be right or to guide them?
?
Are you willing to admit when your response does not directly address the question? This lesson forces you to examine the difference between a valid proof and a valid refutation. What are you more attached to: your argument or the truth of the matter? Commit now to ask yourself before speaking: 'Am I answering the question or initiating a proof?'
