Two Approaches on Mutashabihat Verses
The author presents two opinions of early Maturidi scholars regarding how to deal with mutashabihat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main approaches of early Maturidi scholars toward ambiguous (mutashabihat) verses: tafwid (consigning meaning to Allah) and ta'wil (interpretation without certainty).

Core Idea
Early Maturidis agreed on tanzih but differed on whether to interpret mutashabihat or leave them to Allah.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents the two major approaches of the early Maturidi scholars (mashayikh) regarding verses that seemingly attribute human-like qualities to Allah. Both groups affirm divine transcendence (tanzih), but they differ on the method: one refrains from interpretation (tafwid), while the other engages in explaining possible meanings that do not contradict tawhid. Understanding these approaches is key to appreciating the Maturidi methodology and its commitment to preserving both revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description of the two groups come through.
Notice that the passage begins with 'Then after that, our mashayikh differed...'
2
Identify the first approach
Find the description of the group that says these verses are mutashabihat and that they do not engage in ta'wil. Notice that they simply recite the verses as they came and affirm that God is not in a place.
This is the tafwid approach—consigning the meaning to Allah.
3
Identify the second approach
Find the description of the group that does engage in explaining possible meanings. Note that they say the verses bear various meanings besides the literal ones and that they do not claim certainty about which meaning God intended.
This is the ta'wil approach—but without decisive proof.
4
Compare the common ground
Both groups agree that God is transcendent (tanzih) and reject any literal spatial or directional interpretation. Identify the sentence that states this agreement.
The phrase 'He is not located in a place nor confined in a direction' expresses the shared belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك اختلف مشايخنا رحمهم الله - منهم من قال في هذه الآيات: إنها متشابهة نعتقد فيها أن لا وجه لإجرائها على ظواهرها، ونؤمن بتنزيلها (ولا نشتغل بتأويلها) ونعتقد أن ما أراد الله تعالى بها حق. وهؤلاء يطلقون ما ورد به الشرع فيقولون: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ويقولون: إنه تعالى القاهر فوق عباده، وكذا كل آية في هذا. وما يروي عن السلف من ألفاظ يوهم ظاهرها إثبات الجهة والمكان فهو محمول على هذا. وبين السلف اختلاف (في الألفاظ التي يطلقون فيها كل ذلك اختلاف) منهم في العبارة مع اتفاقهم في المعنى، أنه تعالى ليس بمتمكن في مكان ولا متحيز في جهة. ومنهم من يشتغل ببيان احتمال الآيات معانى مختلفة سوى ظواهرها ويقولون: نعلم أن المراد بعض ما تحتملها الألفاظ من المعاني التي لا تكون منافية للتوحيد والقدم، ولا يقطعون على مراد الله تعالى لانعدام دليل يوجب القطع على المراد وتعين بعض المعاني ثم العرش يذكر، ويراد به السرير المحفوف بالملائكة الذي هو أعظم المخلوقات وهو ظاهر في الشريعة، ويذكر ويراد به الملك.
Then after that, our mashāyikh (may God have mercy on them) differed—among them were those who said about these verses: They are mutashābihāt (ambiguous); we believe that there is no basis for applying them to their literal meanings, and we affirm their revelation (and do not engage in their interpretation), and we believe that what God intends by them is true. And these people utter what the Law has brought, saying: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne,' and they say: He is the Almighty above His servants, and likewise every verse in this category. And what is narrated from the Salaf of expressions whose outward meaning suggests affirming direction and place is interpreted in this way. And among the Salaf there is difference (in the expressions they use in all that—difference) in wording while agreeing on the meaning: that He is not located in a place nor confined in a direction. And among them are those who occupy themselves with explaining that the verses bear various meanings besides their literal ones, and they say: We know that the intended meaning is some of the meanings that the words may bear, which are not contrary to tawḥīd and qidam (eternity), and they do not decide on the intended meaning of God because of the absence of a proof that would compel certainty as to the intended meaning and the specification of some meanings. Then the Throne is mentioned; it may mean the bed surrounded by angels which is the greatest of created beings, and that is apparent in the Law; and it may mean sovereignty.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage classifies the two main approaches of early Māturīdī scholars (mashāyikh) regarding the mutashābihāt verses (verses with ambiguous or anthropomorphic expressions). The first group holds that these verses are ambiguous (mutashābihāt) and that one should refrain from interpreting them (taʼwīl). They affirm the revelation of the verses, believe that God's intended meaning is true, and simply recite the verses as they came, e.g., saying 'ar-Raḥmānu ʿalā l-ʿarshi stawā' without further explanation. They caution that any reports from the Salaf that seem to imply location or direction must be understood as affirming transcendence (tanzīh), and they agree that God is not in a place or direction. The second group actively engages in explaining that these verses have multiple possible meanings besides the literal one. They affirm that the true meaning is among those possibilities that do not contradict tawḥīd and God's eternity (qidam). However, they do not claim certainty about which specific meaning God intended, since there is no decisive proof (dalīl qāṭiʿ) to specify one. As an example, the word 'arsh (throne) may refer literally to the great creature that surrounds the angels, or it may mean sovereignty (mulk). The author thus presents both approaches as legitimate within the School, with the common ground being the rejection of anthropomorphism and the affirmation of tanzīh.

Key Terms
متشابه (mutashābih)
تأويل (taʼwīl)
تنزيه (tanzīh)
ظاهر (ẓāhir)
قديم (qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the first group (tafwid) simply accepts anthropomorphic meanings.
Correction: They in fact affirm tanzih and reject any literal spatial interpretation, as the passage states: 'He is not located in a place nor confined in a direction.'

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the two groups of scholars regarding mutashabihat?
A) The first group denies that the verses are from God
B) The first group refrains from interpreting the verses, while the second group offers possible interpretations without certainty
C) The second group believes God is literally on the Throne
D) The first group engages in detailed ta'wil while the second avoids it

Key Takeaway
Early Maturidi scholars agreed on tanzih but differed in their method for dealing with mutashabihat: some practiced tafwid and others engaged in ta'wil without certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you refrain from searching for meaning, or do you try to interpret while acknowledging your fallibility? ?
This lesson shows that early scholars agreed on tanzih but differed in method. Are you quick to judge those who interpret differently, or do you recognize the shared intention of protecting God’s transcendence? What assumptions about others’ faith do you need to abandon?
?
The second group says, “We do not decide on the intended meaning of God because of the absence of proof.” Are you often tempted to claim certainty about God’s intent where no decisive proof exists? How can you cultivate humility before the Qur’an in your daily recitation and study?

The Rule for Interpreting Words with Two Meanings When Applied to God
Nasafi explains how words with two meanings are interpreted when attributed to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi scholars like Nasafi interpret ambiguous terms attributed to God, ensuring that no impossible meaning is ascribed to Him.

Core Idea
When a word has two meanings, only the possible meaning applies to God.

This lesson introduces a key principle in Maturidi theology: when a word can mean two things, one compatible with God and one not, we must understand it in the compatible sense. Nasafi explains this using the examples of 'qadara' (to decree) and 'ajab' (wonder).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the examples and the general rule.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the words 'qadara' and 'ajab' in the passage. See how each is handled: 'qadara' is about decreeing without prior weakness, and 'ajab' is about approval without ignorance.
3
Understand the general rule
Nasafi states: when a word has two meanings, one impossible for God and one possible, only the possible meaning is understood when the word is attributed to God.
4
Notice the role of custom
For 'qadara', Nasafi notes that any implication of prior weakness comes from customary inference, not from the word itself. This shows that linguistic meaning must be distinguished from customary associations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجهل. ويقال : قدر وكان ذلك بعد العجز وهذا الإطلاق جائز في الله تعالى على إرادة تحقق العلم والقدرة بدون سابقة الجهل والعجز ، فكذا هذا على أن اللفظ الموضوع لمعنيين يستحيل أحدهما على الله تعالى ولا يستحيل الآخر يفهم منه إذا أضيف إلى الله تعالى ما لا يستحيل عليه دون ما يستحيل عليه كلفظة العجب، فإن العجب في اللغة عبارة عن استحسان الشيء مع الجهل بسببه، فإذا أضيف إلى الله تعالى في مثل قوله : بَلْ عَجِبْتَ وَيَسْخَرُونَ في قراءة من قرأ بضم التاء يفهم منه الاستحسان فحسب، ففي اللفظ الذي ما وضع للضعف بل وضع لنفاذ السلطنة والتصرف، وتثبت فيه سابقة الضعف لا بدلالة اللفظ بل بوقف العادة لأن لا يفهم منه ما يستحيل على الله أولى والله الموفق.
Ignorance. And it is said: He decreed and that was after incapability, and this application is permissible regarding Allah, exalted, intending the realization of knowledge and power without prior ignorance and incapability. Likewise, when a word is placed for two meanings, one of which is impossible for Allah while the other is not, if it is attributed to Allah, only what is not impossible for Him is understood from it, not what is impossible. Like the word 'wonder': for 'wonder' in language is an expression of approving something while being ignorant of its cause. When it is attributed to Allah in such as His statement 'Rather, you wonder and they mock' in the recitation of one who reads with damm on the tā, only approval is understood from it. Regarding the word that is placed not for weakness but for the effectiveness of authority and disposal, and in it prior weakness is established not by linguistic indication but by customary inference, because it is more appropriate that what is impossible for Allah not be understood from it. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage explains a linguistic principle for interpreting terms attributed to God. The author begins with the example of 'qadara' (to have power/decree): it is permissible to say God 'qadara' after a time when He did not, but this must be understood as the realization of knowledge and power without prior ignorance and inability. Then he states the general rule: when a word has two meanings, one impossible for God (e.g., implying a defect) and one possible, when attributed to God only the possible meaning is understood. The example of 'ajab' (wonder) is given: in human language it implies surprise with ignorance, but when attributed to God in Quran 37:12 (in one recitation), it means approval only. Similarly, the word 'qadara' is not for weakness but for effectuating authority, and any implication of prior weakness is not from the language itself but from customary inference, so it is more appropriate not to understand what is impossible for God.

استحسان
سابقة الجهل
العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributing 'wonder' (ajab) to God implies ignorance, similar to human wonder.
Correction: Nasafi explains that when attributed to God, 'ajab' means only approval, without any ignorance, because the impossible meaning (ignorance) is rejected.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, when a word has two meanings, one impossible for God, how is it interpreted when attributed to God?
A) It is taken in both meanings.
B) It is taken in the possible meaning.
C) It is interpreted figuratively.
D) It is negated entirely.

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, any word attributed to God must be understood in a sense that does not imply imperfection, even if the same word has a different meaning when applied to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The principle of only applying possible meanings to God requires intellectual humility. Is there a 'customary' assumption you hold about God that you now realize is rooted in human experience rather than divine revelation? Will you surrender it?

Proving Divine Attributes from Names
Classification of name signification for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine names can indicate either absolute existence or a meaning beyond existence, and how this classification supports proving real attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
Divine names either signify mere existence or a real attribute beyond it.

Imam Nasafi introduces a new method for proving divine attributes, starting from the names of Allah that are established by definitive texts and consensus. He argues that these names must signify something—either just existence itself or a distinct attribute. This passage sets up the logical options we must consider.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the opening: 'Then we say: for the People of Truth there are methods...'
The passage is incomplete; we only have the first part of the method.
2
Identify the two categories of name signification
The passage states that names either indicate absolute existence (like 'thing' or 'existent') or a meaning beyond existence. Write down these two categories.
3
Explore the subcategory: quiddity
The second category is further divided: the meaning beyond existence is either a quiddity (a specific essence that distinguishes one type from another) or something else (the text cuts off). Consider examples like 'horse' or 'human'.
The quiddity is the whatness of a thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لأهل الحق في المسألة طرق منها: طريقة الاستدلال بالاسم الثابت بالنصوص التي لا ريب في ثبوتها، والإجماع الذي لا مخالفة فيه في الأمة وهذه الطريقة أن يقال: إن الله تعالى يسمى حيا قادرا عالما سميعا بصيرا باقيا . ثم التسمية إما أن كانت وضعت لتدل على مطلق الوجود كلفظة الموجود والشيء . وإما أن كانت وضعت لتدل على معنى وراء الوجود. وذلك المعنى إما مائية يمتاز بها النوع من النوع كاسم الفرس والبغل والحمار والآدمي والأسد . فإن لكل مسمى من هذه الأشياء مائية يمتاز بها عن الآخر، من
Then we say: for the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) there are methods in this matter, among them: the method of proof by the name established (al-ism al-thābit) by texts whose establishment is beyond doubt, and by the consensus (ijmāʿ) that has no opposition in the community. And this method is that it is said: Indeed Allah, exalted, is called Living, Powerful, Knowing, Hearing, Seeing, Eternal. Then the naming either was coined to indicate absolute existence (muṭlaq al-wujūd) like the term 'the existent' and 'thing'. Or it was coined to indicate a meaning beyond existence (maʿnā warā' al-wujūd). And that meaning is either a quiddity (māhiyya) by which one type is distinguished from another, like the name of horse, mule, donkey, human, and lion. For each of these named things has a quiddity by which it is distinguished from the other, from
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage begins a new method for proving divine attributes, introduced as one of several methods used by Ahl al-Haqq. The method is called 'proof by the name established by texts and consensus'. The argument proceeds: God is called by names such as Living, Powerful, Knowing, etc. These names either signify mere existence (like the word 'thing') or a meaning beyond existence. If they signify a meaning beyond existence, that meaning could be a quiddity (māhiyya) that distinguishes one type from another, as in the names of different animals. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating an enumeration of possibilities that continues in the original work. The method sets up a classification of how names relate to attributes, leading to the conclusion that divine names imply real attributes.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بالاسم الثابت
مائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine names are mere labels without any real meaning.
Correction: The passage shows that names signify either existence or a real attribute; they are not empty labels.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The names of Allah are not mere labels; they either point to His existence or to real attributes, and this semantic distinction is the foundation of the proof.

Alternative Argument: Knowledge and Power as Distinct Attributes
Nasafi notes that many Hanafi-Maturidi scholars use this as a direct proof of attributes being distinct from essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why many Hanafi-Maturidi scholars treat the argument against essential attributes as a primary proof, and how it establishes the distinction of attributes from essence.

Core Idea
For many Maturidis, the impossibility of being powerful by that by which one knows settles the case.

In this brief passage, Nasafi notes a methodological point: many of his fellow Maturidi scholars consider the preceding refutation not merely a response to a rival view, but itself a foundational proof. They argue that if God's knowledge and power were identical to His essence, a logical impossibility would follow. This lesson explores that argument and why it is treated as a direct proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key phrase 'ابتداء دليل' (a primary proof).
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument: If God were knowing by essence and powerful by essence, then He would be powerful by that by which He knows, which is impossible. Why is that impossible? Because it conflates two distinct attributes.
This builds on earlier lessons on attribute classification.
3
Appreciate the methodological significance
Many scholars take this simple impossibility as enough proof. They do not need a separate elaborate demonstration. This shows confidence in the argument's clarity.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how treating a contradiction as a direct proof simplifies theological reasoning. Reflect on why this approach might be convincing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثير من أصحابنا من يجعل هذا الكلام ابتداء دليل في المسألة فيقولون لو كان تعالى عالما بنفسه قادرا بنفسه لكان قادرا بما به يعلم وهو محال ويقررون على ما مر وبالله العصمة.
And many of our companions make this statement a primary proof in the issue, saying: If the Exalted were knowing by His essence and powerful by His essence, then He would be powerful by that by which He knows, which is impossible. And they elaborate as has passed. And success is from God.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note. The author informs us that many of the Maturidi scholars (أصحابنا) use the preceding line of reasoning (the argument that if knowledge and power were identical to essence, then God would be powerful by that by which He knows) as a direct and primary proof (ابتداء دليل) for the distinction of attributes from essence. They do not need to go through all the subsidiary arguments; they take the central impossibility as sufficient. The author affirms this approach and ends with a prayer for divine success.

The First Type of Analogical Reasoning: Direct Indication
Nasafi defines the first type of analogical reasoning using everyday examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen, which relies on direct causal indication as exemplified by smoke indicating fire and a building indicating a builder.

Core Idea
If you see the effect, you can infer the cause directly.

Nasafi introduces the foundational method of analogical reasoning in kalam, which is based on observable signs. This first type is straightforward: when we witness a clear sign in the world, it directly points to something else. Smoke points to fire, a building points to a builder. This kind of reasoning is used to infer the existence of the Creator from the order of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice how Nasafi states the two types before elaborating the first.
2
Identify the first type
The passage defines the first type as witnessing something that indicates the existence of something not absent from our senses. Focus on the examples: smoke/fire and building/builder.
These are clear causal relationships.
3
Understand the examples
Consider why smoke indicates fire and a building indicates a builder. This is because they are cause-effect pairs that we observe consistently.
4
Reflect on theological application
Think about how this type of reasoning is used in kalam to argue for the existence of God from the order and design of the universe.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا أيضا طريقة الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب وهي على نوعين: أنا إذا شاهدنا شيئا يدل على وجود غير غائب عن حواسنا كدلالة الدخان على النار، ودلالة البناء على الباني - والثاني: أنا إذا علمنا تعلق شيء بشيء تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، ورأينا استحالة إضافة ما يجري منها مجرى الحكم للآخر إلى غيره، ثم يثبت عندنا بالدليل ثبوت أحدهما فى الغائب علمنا بثبوت الآخر ضرورة. مثاله: أنا لما شاهدنا جسما مضيئا محرقا دائم الحركة، وعلمنا أنه نار ثم ثبت عندنا أن ببغداد نارا بدليل عرفنا أنه جوهر مضيء محرق دائم الحركة. وإذا شاهدنا جسما متحركا، أو أسود، وعلمنا أنه كان متحركا لقيام الحركة به، وأسود لقيام السواد به،
And we also have the method of reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen, and it is of two types: First, that we witness something that indicates the existence of something not absent from our senses, like the indication of smoke to fire, and the indication of construction to the builder. Second, that we know the connection of one thing to another in a connection that is inseparable, and we see the impossibility of attributing what is in the manner of a judgment of the other to something else; then when it is established for us by evidence that one of them is established in the unseen, we know necessarily that the other is established. Example: When we witnessed a luminous, burning, constantly moving body, and we knew it is fire, then it is established for us that in Baghdad there is a fire by evidence, we know that it is a luminous, burning, constantly moving substance. And when we witnessed a moving body, or a black one, and we knew that it was moving due to motion subsisting in it, and black due to blackness subsisting in it,
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage classifies the method of analogical reasoning from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) into two types. The first type is direct causal indication: we observe a sign in the witnessed world that directly points to something else, like smoke indicating fire or a building indicating a builder. The second type is based on necessary concomitance: we know that two things are inseparably connected, such that if one is present, the other must be present. Then, if we have evidence that one of them exists in the unseen, we necessarily know that the other exists. The author provides an example: we observe a luminous, burning, constantly moving body and identify it as fire. If we then have evidence that there is fire in Baghdad, we know it must be a luminous, burning, constantly moving substance. Similarly, if we see a moving body, we know it moves due to motion subsisting in it, and if black, due to blackness subsisting in it. This method is crucial for establishing divine attributes: from the witnessed attributes of creatures, we infer attributes of the Creator in an appropriate manner.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب
الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this reasoning about God implies He has physical properties like a human builder.
Correction: The analogy is purely about causality and existence, not modality or physical resemblance.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the first type of reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen?
A) Reasoning by necessary concomitance
B) Reasoning by direct causal indication like smoke to fire
C) Reasoning by scripture
D) Reasoning by consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The first type of analogical reasoning is direct causal indication: from a clear sign in the witnessed world, we infer its unseen cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you—your own body, the sky, the earth—do you immediately see them as signs pointing to a Creator, or do you take them for granted? This lesson teaches that every effect points to its cause. What is one specific sign today that you have overlooked? Will you pause right now to acknowledge its source?
?
You have just learned that a building proves a builder. Your own existence is far more complex than any building. Have you truly internalized that your very being is a sign of Allah's existence and power? If you believe this, why do you still act as though you are self-made? Commit today to trace one blessing—your breath, your eyesight, your ability to think—back to its Origin and thank Him for it.
?
The analogy you studied is simple: smoke means fire. Yet, do you live as though the signs of Allah in your life are equally clear? When you face a difficulty, do you see it as a sign from Him calling you to turn back? Or do you ignore it? This week, choose one repeated event (e.g., a trial, a blessing, a coincidence) and force yourself to treat it as a direct indication from Allah. What will you do differently?

The Second Type of Analogical Reasoning: Inseparable Concomitance
Nasafi explains the second type of analogical reasoning based on necessary concomitance and provides an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of analogical reasoning, which relies on an inseparable connection between two things, such that establishing one in the unseen necessitates the other.

Core Idea
If two things are inseparably connected, knowing one in the unseen guarantees the other.

Having covered direct causal indication, Nasafi now explains a more subtle method. This second type involves a necessary correlation: two things are so tightly linked that they cannot exist apart. When we prove one exists in the unseen, we automatically know the other exists too. The example of fire and its properties illustrates this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second type
Read the Arabic passage from 'والثاني' onwards and its translation. Note the careful phrasing.
This is a more complex definition than the first type.
2
Understand the condition of inseparability
The key is that the connection is 'la infikak' (no separation). This means they are necessarily concomitant.
Also, it is impossible to attribute the judgment of one to something else.
3
Study the example of fire
The example shows that if we know fire exists in Baghdad, we know it must have properties like being luminous and burning. This is because fire and those properties are inseparable.
4
Reflect on theological application
This method is used to infer divine attributes: from the existence of a quality in creation (like knowledge) we infer the existence of that attribute in God, since it is inseparable from perfection.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا أيضا طريقة الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب وهي على نوعين: أنا إذا شاهدنا شيئا يدل على وجود غير غائب عن حواسنا كدلالة الدخان على النار، ودلالة البناء على الباني - والثاني: أنا إذا علمنا تعلق شيء بشيء تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، ورأينا استحالة إضافة ما يجري منها مجرى الحكم للآخر إلى غيره، ثم يثبت عندنا بالدليل ثبوت أحدهما فى الغائب علمنا بثبوت الآخر ضرورة. مثاله: أنا لما شاهدنا جسما مضيئا محرقا دائم الحركة، وعلمنا أنه نار ثم ثبت عندنا أن ببغداد نارا بدليل عرفنا أنه جوهر مضيء محرق دائم الحركة. وإذا شاهدنا جسما متحركا، أو أسود، وعلمنا أنه كان متحركا لقيام الحركة به، وأسود لقيام السواد به،
And we also have the method of reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen, and it is of two types: First, that we witness something that indicates the existence of something not absent from our senses, like the indication of smoke to fire, and the indication of construction to the builder. Second, that we know the connection of one thing to another in a connection that is inseparable, and we see the impossibility of attributing what is in the manner of a judgment of the other to something else; then when it is established for us by evidence that one of them is established in the unseen, we know necessarily that the other is established. Example: When we witnessed a luminous, burning, constantly moving body, and we knew it is fire, then it is established for us that in Baghdad there is a fire by evidence, we know that it is a luminous, burning, constantly moving substance. And when we witnessed a moving body, or a black one, and we knew that it was moving due to motion subsisting in it, and black due to blackness subsisting in it,
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage classifies the method of analogical reasoning from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) into two types. The first type is direct causal indication: we observe a sign in the witnessed world that directly points to something else, like smoke indicating fire or a building indicating a builder. The second type is based on necessary concomitance: we know that two things are inseparably connected, such that if one is present, the other must be present. Then, if we have evidence that one of them exists in the unseen, we necessarily know that the other exists. The author provides an example: we observe a luminous, burning, constantly moving body and identify it as fire. If we then have evidence that there is fire in Baghdad, we know it must be a luminous, burning, constantly moving substance. Similarly, if we see a moving body, we know it moves due to motion subsisting in it, and if black, due to blackness subsisting in it. This method is crucial for establishing divine attributes: from the witnessed attributes of creatures, we infer attributes of the Creator in an appropriate manner.

Key Terms

الانفكاك
الضرورة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this implies God has physical properties like fire.
Correction: The analogy is limited to the relation of attribute and subject, not physical composition.

C) The two things must be of the same substance
D) One must be the cause of the other

Key Takeaway
The second type of analogical reasoning relies on an inseparable connection: if one is proven in the unseen, the other is known necessarily.

Two Types of Inference and Their Conditions
Al-Nasafi defines the two types of inference from the shāhid to the ghā'ib and applies them to prove Allah is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of inference from the witnessed to the unseen in Islamic theology, their conditions of concomitance and reversal, and how this reasoning is applied to prove Allah's transcendence beyond physical composition.

Core Idea
Inference from the seen to the unseen requires an inseparable causal link, not mere analogy.

Al-Nasafi begins by distinguishing two ways we reason from what we observe (al-shāhid) to what is beyond observation (al-ghā'ib). The first is simple indication, like smoke pointing to fire. The second, more powerful method, relies on an inseparable connection between two things, such that when we know one exists in the unseen, the other must also exist. He then lays down the logical conditions for this reasoning and applies it to the most important theological conclusion: that Allah is not a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: al-Nasafi first defines two types, then gives examples, then explains the conditions, then applies them.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two types of inference
Find where al-Nasafi states 'the first type' and 'the second type'. Understand the difference: type one is direct indication, type two is based on an inseparable connection.
The second type requires establishing that the connection cannot be broken.
3
Understand the conditions: ittirād and in'ikās
Al-Nasafi says that for the second type to be valid, the cause must have two properties: ittirād (concomitance in existence) and in'ikās (concomitance in non-existence). Ask yourself: why are these necessary?
These conditions ensure the connection is truly necessary, not accidental.
4
See how this proof shows Allah is not a body
Al-Nasafi applies the method to the attribute 'being a body' in the witnessed world, showing it is inseparably connected to composition (tarkīb). Since composition is impossible for Allah, He cannot be a body.
This is a key argument for divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا أيضا طريقة الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب وهي على نوعين: أنا إذا شاهدنا شيئا يدل على وجود غير غائب عن حواسنا كدلالة الدخان على النار، ودلالة البناء على الباني. والثاني: أنا إذا علمنا تعلق شيء بشيء تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، ورأينا استحالة إضافة ما يجري منها مجرى الحكم للآخر إلى غيره، ثم يثبت عندنا بالدليل ثبوت أحدهما في الغائب علمنا بثبوت الآخر ضرورة. مثاله: أنا لما شاهدنا جسما مضيئا محرقا دائم الحركة، وعلمنا أنه نار ثم ثبت عندنا أن ببغداد نارا بدليل عرفنا أنه جوهر مضيء محرق دائم الحركة. وإذا شاهدنا جسما متحركا، أو أسود، وعلمنا أنه كان متحركا لقيام الحركة به، وأسود لقيام السواد به، ثم علمنا بدليل قام أن في الغائب متحركا علمنا أن الحركة كانت قائمة به، وكذا الأسود مع قيام السواد به، وهذا لأن قيام الحركة بالجسم علة لاستحقاقه الاتصاف بكونه متحركا لأن قيام الحركة لا ينفك عن اتصاف المحل بكونه متحركا، إذ لا يتصور قيام حركة بمحل لا يكون المحل متحركا بها، ولا وجود متحرك لقيام الحركة به فوجد الأطراد والانعكاس اللذان هما شرطان في العلل العقلية، ويستحيل إضافة كونه متحركا إلى غير الحركة التي قامت به، يظهر ذلك عند الصفات القائمة بالمتحرك، فإنه يتصور وجود كل منها، ولا اتصاف للجسم بكونه متحركا وبهذا يقع الفرق بين وصف العلة الجاري مجرى الحد وبين الخاصية الشاملة للأشخاص والأزمنة جميعا كقوة الضحك في الآدمي، وبهذا عرفنا أن الباري جل وعلا ليس بجسم لأن كون القائم بالذات في الشاهد جسما تعلق بالتركب تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، وعرف استحالة إضافة كونه جسما إلى غير التركب بسبر الصفات، فعرفنا أن وجود أحدهما وهو كونه جسما في الغائب يوجب وجود الآخر، وهذا أصل لا وجه لمن عقل وأنصف ولم يكابر لإنكاره.
And we also have the method of inference from the witnessed to the unseen, and it is of two types: (1) We observe something that indicates the existence of something else not absent from our senses, like the indication of smoke to fire, and the indication of a building to the builder. (2) The second: We know that something is connected to something else with a connection that cannot be separated, and we see the impossibility of attributing what is like a judgment of one to other than the other, then it is established for us by evidence the existence of one of them in the unseen, we know the existence of the other necessarily. Example: When we observe a luminous, burning, perpetually moving body, and we know that it is fire, then it is established for us that in Baghdad there is fire by evidence, we know that it is a luminous, burning, perpetually moving substance. And when we observe a moving body or a black one, and we know that it was moving because of the subsistence of movement in it, and black because of the subsistence of blackness in it, then we know by an evidence that exists that in the unseen there is a moving thing, we know that movement subsisted in it, and likewise the black thing with the subsistence of blackness in it. This is because the subsistence of movement in the body is the cause of its deserving the attribute of being moving, because the subsistence of movement is inseparable from the attribute of the locus as being moving by it, since it is inconceivable that a movement subsists in a locus without the locus being moving by it, nor the existence of a moving thing without the subsistence of movement in it. Thus, concomitance (al-ittirād) and reversal (al-in'ikās) are found, which are the two conditions in rational causes. And it is impossible to attribute its being moving to other than the movement that subsisted in it. That becomes clear with the attributes subsisting in the moving thing, for the existence of each of them is conceivable, and there is no attribution of the body as being moving. And by this the distinction is made between the description of the cause that runs like a definition and the property that encompasses all individuals and times, like the power of laughter in a human. And by this we know that the Creator, exalted and high, is not a body, because the fact that what subsists in the essence in the witnessed is a body is connected to composition (al-tarkīb) with an inseparable connection, and we know the impossibility of attributing its being a body to other than composition by examining the attributes (sabr al-ṣifāt). So we know that the existence of one of them, namely its being a body in the unseen, necessitates the existence of the other. And this is a principle that has no face (i.e., no way to deny) for one who is intelligent, fair, and not obstinate.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of inferential reasoning from the witnessed (al-shāhid) to the unseen (al-ghā'ib) used in kalām. The first type is simple indication (dalāla), like smoke indicating fire. The second type is based on an inseparable connection (ta'alluq lā infikāk) between two things, such that when one is known to exist in the unseen, the other is necessarily known. Al-Nasafi gives examples: fire and its properties, movement and the moving object. He then explains that for this reasoning to be valid, two conditions must be met: ittirād (concomitance in existence) and in'ikās (concomitance in non-existence). These are the conditions for rational causality. He then applies this to prove that Allah is not a body, because if something subsisting in essence is a body, it is necessarily composed (tarkīb). Since composition is impossible for Allah, He cannot be a body. The method is called sabr (examining attributes) to establish the cause.

غائب
استدلال
اطراد
انعكاس
علة

سبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second type of inference from the witnessed to the unseen is just analogy (qiyās) based on similarity.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that the second type is based on an inseparable connection (ta'alluq lā infikāk) and must satisfy ittirād and in'ikās, which are conditions for rational causation, not mere analogy.

Checkpoint

D) Tarkīb and taḥlīl.

Key Takeaway
The valid inference from the witnessed to the unseen requires an inseparable causal connection that meets the conditions of ittirād and in'ikās, and this method shows that Allah, being free from composition, is not a body.

What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that every created thing is a shāhid pointing to the ghā'ib? The Method of Sabr and the Principle of Correlation
Al-Nasafi explains the method of sabr and uses tard and aks to prove God is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses the method of sabr (testing) with the principles of tard (constant conjunction) and aks (constant absence) to establish an intellectual cause, and how this reasoning proves that God is not a body.

Core Idea
The sabr method uses strict cause-effect conditions to prove that bodies are composite, so God cannot be a body.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presents a sophisticated methodological tool for proving divine transcendence: the method of sabr, which relies on the principle of correlation. He explains that a true cause must have both constant conjunction (tard) and constant absence (aks). Applying this to the relationship between body and composition, he argues that if God were a body, He would be composite, which is impossible. The passage is a powerful example of rational theology in the Maturidi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow. The passage is dense; take your time.
The passage contains technical terms like sabr, tard, and aks.
2
Understand the method of sabr
Identify how al-Nasafi establishes a cause (illa) using tard and aks. Look for the two conditions explicitly mentioned.
These conditions are essential for intellectual causality.
3
Distinguish between essential cause and common property
Notice the distinction between a cause that defines (like motion for moving) and a common property (like laughter for humans). This sharpens the reasoning.
4
Apply the reasoning to body and composition
See how al-Nasafi transfers the observed relationship between body and composition to the unseen, concluding that God cannot be a body.
This is the key argument for God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبر
ثم علمنا بدليل قام أن في الغائب متحركا علمنا أن الحركة كانت قائمة به، وكذا الأسود د مع قيام السواد به، وهذا لأن قيام الحركة بالجسم علة لاستحقاقه الاتصاف بكونه متحركا لأن قيام الحركة لا ينفك عن اتصاف المحل بكونه متحركا، إذ لا يتصور قيام حركة بمحل لا يكون المحل متحركا بها، ولا وجود متحرك لقيام للحركة به فوجد الأطراد والانعكاس اللذان هما شرطان في العلل العقلية، ويستحيل إضافة كونه متحركا إلى غير الحركة التي قامت به، يظهر ذلك عند الصفات القائمة بالمتحرك، فإنه يتصور وجود كل منها، ولا اتصاف للجسم بكونه متحركا وبهذا يقع الفرق بين وصف العلة الجاري مجرى الحد وبين الخاصية الشاملة للأشخاص والأزمنة جميعا كقوة الضحك في الآدمي، وبهذا عرفنا أن الباري جل وعلا ليس بجسم لأن كون القائم بالذات في الشاهد جسما تعلق بالتركب تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، وعرف استحالة إضافة كونه جسما إلى غير التركب بسبر الصفات، فعرفنا أن وجود أحدهما وهو كونه جسما في الغائب يوجب وجود الآخر،
وهذا أصل لا وجه لمن عقل وأنصف ولم يكابر لإنكاره.
Sabr (testing). Then we know by an established proof that if there is a moving thing in the unseen, we know that motion was subsisting in it, and likewise blackness with the subsistence of blackness in it. This is because the subsistence of motion in the body is the cause of its deserving the attribute of being moving, because the subsistence of motion is inseparable from the attribute of the locus being moving by it, for it is inconceivable that motion subsists in a locus that is not moving by it, nor can a moving thing exist without motion subsisting in it. Thus correlation (al-ittirād) and inversion (al-inkis) are found, which are the two conditions in intellectual causes. It is impossible to attribute being moving to something other than the motion that subsists in it. This becomes clear regarding the attributes that subsist in the moving thing, for each of them can be conceived to exist, and the body is not described as moving without that. And by this the distinction is made between the description of the cause that runs as a definition and the common property shared by all persons and times, like the capacity for laughter in humans. And by this we know that the Exalted Creator is not a body, because being a self-subsistent entity in the witnessed world is a body tied to composition in an inseparable manner. And the impossibility of attributing being a body to other than composition is known through examining (sabr) attributes. So we know that the existence of one of them—namely its being a body in the unseen—necessitates the existence of the other. This is a principle that has no loophole for one who is rational, fair, and not obstinate to deny.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the method of sabr (testing attributes) and establishes the principle of correlation (tard wa 'aks) as conditions for intellectual causality. The author begins by stating that if we observe a moving thing in the unseen, we can infer that it has motion, just as in the witnessed world motion necessitates being moving. He explains that motion is the cause (illa) of being moving, and this relationship has two essential features: constant conjunction (tard)—whenever motion exists, the thing is moving—and constant absence (aks)—whenever motion is absent, the thing is not moving. These two conditions confirm that the cause is genuine. He distinguishes between a cause that defines the essence (like motion for moving) and a common property (like the capacity for laughter in humans) that does not define. Applying this to the body, he argues that in the witnessed world, being a body is inseparably tied to composition (tarkib). Therefore, if something in the unseen were a body, it would necessarily have composition. Since God is not composed, He cannot be a body. The passage concludes that this reasoning is irrefutable for any rational and fair person.

Key Terms
سبر
اطراد
انعكاس
علة عقلية
جسم
تركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the method of sabr is arbitrary.
Correction: The method of sabr follows strict conditions of tard (constant conjunction) and aks (constant absence), making it a rigorous intellectual tool.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what two conditions must a relationship meet to be considered a true intellectual cause (illa aqliyya)?
A) Tard and 'aks
B) Sabr and tarkib
C) Qiyas and illa
D) Shahid and gha'ib

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's method of sabr, using tard and aks, demonstrates that if God were a body, He would be composite, which is impossible, thus establishing His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says this principle is irrefutable for one who is rational and fair and not obstinate. Are you truly open to being corrected when your understanding of God contradicts what the evidence clearly shows? What would you have to change in your heart to become that person?
?
Al-Nasafi uses the example of 'capacity for laughter' as a non-defining property. Many of your religious practices may be common properties shared by others without defining your essence. What would it mean for your relationship with God to be a defining cause, not just a common property? Will you commit to one concrete change this week to make that happen?
?
If God were a body, you would have limits to your worship—a finite god cannot inspire infinite devotion. Now that you know God is beyond composition, does your worship reflect that limitless majesty? What specific attitude or action in your prayer needs to be elevated to match who He is?

Refuting Mu'tazila and Mujassima Through Contradictions
Nasafi explains dialectical refutation of Mu'tazila and Mujassima

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses dialectical consistency to refute both the Mu'tazila and Mujassima by exposing their internal contradictions.

Core Idea
Use your opponent's own principles against them.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a powerful dialectical strategy. Instead of directly arguing from scripture or reason, Nasafi shows how each sect's own accepted premises can be turned back on them. By identifying a principle both groups agree on and then later violate, he reveals their inconsistency. This is a classic kalam technique of refutation through contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle about the unseen
Nasafi begins by stating that the unseen (al-gha'ib) does not necessarily have the same attributes as the physical world (al-shahid). For example, a knower in the physical world must have knowledge, but that does not apply in the unseen. This is the key principle both groups initially accept.
This principle is crucial for the dialectical move.
2
See how the Mu'tazila contradict themselves
The Mu'tazila agree with this principle when arguing against the Mujassima about the body. They deny that God has a body because bodies in the physical world are composed, but the unseen may not be. However, they then use the opposite logic when arguing for attributes: they insist God must have knowledge because in the physical world a knower has knowledge. This is a contradiction.
Their own method is turned against them.
3
See how the Mujassima contradict themselves
Similarly, the Mujassima accept the principle regarding attributes, but then violate it when arguing for a body. They agree that the unseen may differ from the physical world in some respects, but then insist God has a body based on physical world analogies.
Both groups are inconsistent.
4
Apply the strategy
Nasafi concludes that anyone skilled in debate can refute the Mu'tazila using the issue of the body, and refute the Mujassima using the issue of attributes. The key is to use the opponents' own accepted premises against them.
This is a powerful dialectical tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغائب لا علم له كتجويز علم لذات لا يكون به عالما، أو كتجويز متحرك في الغائب لا حركة له أو أسود لا سواد له. وقد ساعدتنا المعتزلة على هذه الجملة في مسألة الجسم على المجسمة، ثم ناقضت في هذه المسألة وساعدتنا المتجسمة على هذه الجملة في المسألة ثم ناقضت في هذه المسألة. وساعدتنا المتجسمة على هذه الجملة في المسألة ثم ناقضت في مسألة الجسم فينتقض كل كلام للمعتزلة على المجسمة بهذه المسألة، فصاروا مبطلين تلك المسألة على أنفسهم بهذه المسألة، فإنه لما جاز وجود عالم في الغائب ولا علم له مع أن تعلق العلم بكون الذات الذي قام به عالما تعلق العلل بالمعلول على ما مر من مراعاة شريطة ذلك، جاز أيضا في الغائب وجود جسم لا تركب له، وإن كان تعلق في الشاهد التركب بالاتصاف بالجسم تعلق العلل بالمعلول. ويتبين بالوقوف على هذه الجملة أن ما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة محال فاسد خارج عن قضية العقول، ولزمهم على هذا الأصل جواز وصف الغائب بكونه جسما ومتحركا وساكنا وأبيض وأسود، وغير ذلك من الصفات الذميمة. ثم من كان بصيرا بصفة الجدل، لطيف التجوز عن خدع المغالطين لا يورد عليه معتزلي شيئا ولا ينقضي عن إلزام إلا ويمكنه إبطال ذلك عليه بفصل الجسم. ولا يأتي مجسم بشبهة ولا يعترض على حجة إلا ويمكن مناقضته في ذلك بفصل الصفات، لأن كلا الفريقين ناقض. وابن الراوندي سماه كتاب علل هشام صحح فيه قول هشام بالتجسيم وأبطل فيه كل كلام للمعتزلة بمسألة الصفات. ثم من وقف على هذه الجملة أمكنه دفع كل كلام للمعتزلة على هذه الطريقة بأهون سعي.
The unseen has no knowledge, such as allowing knowledge for a self that is not knowing by it, or allowing a mobile in the unseen that has no motion, or black that has no blackness. The Mu'tazila agreed with us on this statement in the issue of the body against the Corporealists, then they contradicted in this issue. And the Corporealists agreed with us on this statement in the issue, then they contradicted in this issue. And the Corporealists agreed with us on this statement in the issue, then they contradicted in the issue of the body. So every statement of the Mu'tazila against the Corporealists is invalidated by this issue, and they become invalidators of that issue against themselves by this issue. For when it is permissible for a knowing being to exist in the unseen without knowledge, while the attachment of knowledge to the essence by which it is knowing is like the attachment of causes to effects as previously mentioned regarding the observance of its condition, it is also permissible for a body without composition to exist in the unseen, even if in the physical world composition is attached to being described as body like the attachment of causes to effects. By pausing at this statement it becomes evident that what the Mu'tazila went to is impossible, corrupt, and outside the judgment of intellects. And upon this principle they are compelled to allow describing the unseen as being a body, moving, still, white, black, and other blameworthy attributes. Then whoever is insightful about the nature of debate, subtle in avoiding the tricks of fallacious disputants, no Mu'tazili can bring anything against him nor escape from an obligation except that he can invalidate it by the chapter on body. And no Corporealist comes with a sophism nor objects to a proof except that he can refute it by the chapter on attributes, because both groups contradict. And Ibn al-Rawandi wrote a book called 'The Causes of Hisham' in which he affirmed Hisham's statement of corporealism and invalidated all statements of the Mu'tazila by the issue of attributes. Then whoever pauses at this statement can repel every statement of the Mu'tazila in this way with minimal effort.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents a methodological strategy for refuting both the Mu'tazila and the Mujassima (Corporealists) by exposing their internal contradictions. Nasafi begins by stating a principle about the unseen (al-gha'ib): it is not necessary that the unseen has the same concomitants as the physical world (al-shahid). For example, just because in the physical world a knower must have knowledge, that does not mean that in the unseen a knower must have knowledge. Similarly, a moving thing must have motion, etc. The Mu'tazila agreed with this principle when applied to the body: they argued against the Mujassima that just because bodies in the physical world are composed, that does not mean God (the unseen) must be composed. However, the Mu'tazila contradicted themselves when they applied a different standard to attributes: they argued that since in the physical world knowledge is attached to a knower as effect to cause, therefore God must have knowledge. This, Nasafi notes, is inconsistent. The Mujassima also agreed with the principle regarding attributes but contradicted regarding the body. Thus, each group's own logic can be turned against them. Nasafi then explains that by understanding this, one can refute the Mu'tazila using their own acceptance of the attribute analogy, and the Mujassima using their acceptance of the body analogy. He cites Ibn al-Rawandi's book 'The Causes of Hisham' which affirmed Hisham's corporealism but refuted the Mu'tazila using the attribute issue. The overall lesson is that dialectical skill involves using an opponent's principles against them.

Key Terms
الغائب (al-gha'ib)
الشاهد (al-shahid)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
المجسمة (al-Mujassima)
الجسم (al-jism)
الصفات (al-sifat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking Nasafi endorses corporealism.
Correction: Nasafi is using the opponents' own logic dialectically, not asserting corporealism.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what principle do both the Mu'tazila and Mujassima agree on but then contradict?
A) That the unseen has the same attributes as the physical world
B) That the analogy between the physical world and the unseen is valid only in one direction
C) That the unseen is not necessarily like the physical world in all respects
D) That God must have a body

Key Takeaway
Both the Mu'tazila and Mujassima are inconsistent in applying the same principle, allowing refutation through their own logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you apply a standard to others but fail to apply it to yourself? How does that inconsistency weaken your integrity, and what concrete step will you take this week to align your practice with your principles?
?
Nasafi shows that both sects contradict themselves. When you argue with someone about faith, are you more focused on winning the debate or on being consistent with truth? What would it look like for you to be as rigorous with your own views as you are with others'?
?
The passage ends by promising that those who grasp this method can 'repel every statement with minimal effort.' But having an intellectual tool is not the same as using it for good. Are you willing to use your ability to spot contradictions to build others up, or to tear them down? Examine your motive right now.

Note on omitting other argumentative methods
Author states he will omit other methods of his school to avoid lengthening the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author chose to omit other argumentative methods and how this reflects his concern for brevity and focus.

Core Idea
Sometimes what is omitted is as intentional as what is included.

After presenting three essential argumentative methods, the author acknowledges that his school has additional methods. However, he deliberately refrains from including them. This short note reveals an important principle: scholarly works often omit valid lines of reasoning to maintain focus and conciseness. Let us examine this methodological choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

The author uses a polite expression 'رحمهم الله' for his school.
2
Understand the translation
Read the English translation. Observe that the author says there are other methods but he refrains from mentioning them.
3
Analyze the author's reasoning
The author states that the most correct in planning (التدبير) is to omit these methods to avoid making the book lengthy. What does this tell you about his priorities?
Consider the balance between comprehensiveness and conciseness.
4
Reflect on scholarly methodology
Think about why scholars might omit valid arguments. Does omission imply weakness? How does this affect the reader's trust?
This connects to the learning objective.

This reflects the author's concern for brevity and focus on the most essential proofs.

Two Errors in the Mu'tazila's Causal Argument
Nasafi enumerates two errors in the Mu'tazila's causal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two specific errors the author identifies in the Mu'tazila's causal reasoning and why they are considered invalid according to rational principles.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's causal argument fails due to over-extending cause and relying on generality.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla where he critiques the Mu'tazila's use of causal analogy in theology. He points out two fundamental flaws in their reasoning and explains why such arguments collapse when measured against the standards of usul and rational thought. Let's read his analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very direct and critical tone.
2
Identify the two errors
The passage explicitly states 'from two aspects' (من وجهين). Find the two errors: the first about establishing a ruling beyond the cause's capacity, the second about the method of generality.
3
Understand why the second error is corrupt
The author says the corruption is 'apparent' because it goes against rational judgment. Ask yourself: why would assuming generality be irrational?
This connects to the idea that causes are specific, not universal.
4
Reflect on the concluding criticism
The author concludes by characterizing the Mu'tazila's habit as exaggeration and illusion. Consider how this relates to the two errors.
This is a broader methodological critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكان الخطأ فيه من وجهين أحدهما - إثبات الحكم لعلة زيادة على قدر العلة وهو محال، والثاني طريق العموم والشمول كما هو حكم علة الخصوم، وفساد هذا ظاهر، لما أن كل ذلك خارج عن قضية العقول، وجهل بخاصة الحد والمحدود والعلة والمعلول، وهذا ليعلم أن من دأب المعتزلة التهويل بلا تحصيل والتعويل على التخييل، وكل كلام لهم لو عرض على قوانين الأصول، ومقتضيات العقول لظهر أنه بريق السراب بقيمة[؟]، والله الموفق.
So the error in it is from two aspects: one - establishing the ruling for a cause in excess of the capacity of the cause, and that is impossible, and the second is the method of generality and inclusion as is the ruling of the cause of the opponents, and the corruption of this is apparent, because all of that is outside the judgment of intellects, and ignorance of the specificity of the definition and the defined, and the cause and the effect. And this is to know that the habit of the Mu'tazila is exaggeration without actualization and reliance on illusion, and all of their speech, if it were presented to the laws of principles and the requirements of intellects, it would appear that it is the sparkle of a mirage [?], and Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazila's method of reasoning by analogy in theology. The author states that their error is twofold: first, they attribute a ruling to a cause beyond what that cause can actually entail, which is absurd; second, they apply general and inclusive reasoning as if their own causal reasoning is valid. He declares the corruption of this approach obvious, as it violates rational principles and shows ignorance of proper definitions. He then concludes that the Mu'tazila's habit is to exaggerate without real substance and rely on illusions, and that their arguments, when tested against the principles of usul and reason, are like a mirage.

Key Terms
علة
حكم
حد
معلول
قوانين الأصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects all causal reasoning in theology.
Correction: The author only critiques the Mu'tazila's misuse of causal analogy, not causal reasoning per se.

Checkpoint
What are the two aspects of error in the Mu'tazila's argument according to the author?
A) Establishing a ruling for a cause beyond its capacity, and using generality.
B) Ignorance of definitions and the Mu'tazila's habit of exaggeration.
C) Both A and B.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's causal argument is invalid because it overextends the cause and assumes generality, violating rational principles and revealing their reliance on illusion rather than substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are convinced of an argument, do you test its limits or assume it applies universally? What would it take for you to apply the same scrutiny the author applied to the Mu'tazila to your own most cherished beliefs?
?
The author says the Mu'tazila relied on 'exaggeration without actualization' and 'illusion.' In what area of your life—faith, work, relationships—might you be overconfident in your reasoning without grounding it in reality? What will you do this week to check?
?
This passage ties error to ignorance of proper definitions. Think of a concept you use often but have not defined precisely. How might a clearer definition protect you from falling into a similar error as the Mu'tazila?

Tracing the Transmission of a Theological Argument
The author traces the chain of transmission for the previous argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a key theological argument was historically transmitted and attributed to al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Attribution of a theological idea is not always as straightforward as it seems.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla provides a fascinating glimpse into how theological ideas were transmitted and attributed. It focuses on an argument about God's knowledge, tracing its chain back not to al-Ash'ari's own writings but to his student's student. Understanding this history is crucial for appreciating the nuances of early kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the names of the scholars mentioned.
2
Identify the chain of transmission
The passage presents a claim, a correction, and an alternative chain. Find each element: what was claimed, how it was corrected, and the actual chain of transmission.
The chain is: al-Bahili (student of al-Ash'ari) → al-Isfarayini → the argument.
3
Reflect on the historical significance
Ask yourself why the author takes the trouble to correct this attribution. What does this tell us about the development of theological schools?
This connects to the broader theme of how traditions are preserved and modified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخذه ثم إن بعض أصحاب الأشعري نسب هذا الجواب إلى الأشعري، ولا يوجد هذا في شيء من كتبه نصا، وإنما اعتمد عليه أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني وهو عن أبي الحسن الباهلي تلميذ الأشعري. وقد ذكر أبو منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله - في كتبه ما يكون إشارة إلى هذا الجواب وإن كان ظاهر مذهبه ما هو اختيار أوائل أصحابنا
He took it, then some of the followers of al-Ash'ari attributed this answer to al-Ash'ari, but this is not found in any of his books explicitly. Rather, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini relied on it, and it is from Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, a student of al-Ash'ari. And Abu Mansur al-Maturidi - may God have mercy on him - mentioned in his books what indicates this answer, although the apparent position of his school is what was chosen by the early masters of our companions.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a historical note. The author informs us that some Ash'aris attributed the previous argument to al-Ash'ari, but it is not found in his writings. Instead, it came through Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini from Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, a direct student of al-Ash'ari. Additionally, al-Maturidi alludes to this argument in his works, even though his school's explicit position is different, favoring the early Maturidi masters.

أسفراييني
باهلي

Key Takeaway
The argument was not explicitly in al-Ash'ari's books; it came via al-Bahili and al-Isfarayini, and al-Maturidi alluded to it.

Avoiding the Term 'Ghayr' for Divine Attributes
Methodological stance on avoiding the term 'other' for attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some verified scholars of the Maturidi tradition avoid the term 'other' (ghayr) for divine attributes, and what they affirm instead.

Core Idea
The key is inseparability, not terminology.

This passage presents a methodological caution from verified scholars of the attributes tradition. Instead of getting entangled in debates about whether attributes are 'other' than God, they focus on affirming the necessary inseparability of attributes from the divine essence. This approach sidesteps linguistic disputes and avoids any implication of multiplicity of eternals. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the scholar's opening statement: he does not engage with the term 'other' at all.
The passage is dense; read slowly to absorb the flow.
2
Identify the core affirmations
List what the scholar affirms: existence of God, attributes, their inseparability, and that they are proven by proof. Notice he does not need the term 'other'.
The scholar emphasizes that essence without attributes is inconceivable.
3
Understand the critique of Mu'tazila
The scholar criticizes the Mu'tazila for busying themselves with linguistic disputes over 'ghayr'. Consider why he calls this 'bankruptcy'.
The real need is affirming an eternal being with perfect attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(د) ومن محققي أصحاب الصفات من يقول : أنا لا أتعرض للفظة الغير بالنفي ولا بالإثبات بل أقول : الله تعالى موجود وله صفات يستحيل عليها العدم كما يستحيل على الذات، وأثبت هذه الصفات بالدليل على حسب ما أثبت الذات، وأقول: لا يتصور بقاء الذات مع عدم الصفات ولا بقاء الصفات مع الذات، ولا حاجة إلى التسمية بالغير وعدم التسمية فإن كان في مثل هذا يطلق اسم الغير لغة لا أبالي بعد أن لا أجوز بقاء الذات مع عدمها، ولا بقاءها مع عدم الذات، لا أجوز غير الذات قائما بذات موصوفا بصفات الكمال بل أقول ذلك مستحيل، وإن كان لا يطلق اسم الغير على ذلك فلا منفعة لي فيه أيضا وإلا فالاشتغال بهذا الكلام من إفلاس المعتزلة لما أن حقيقته ترجع إلى الاختلاف أن اسم الغير في اللغة ينطلق على ماذا، ولا حاجة بنا إلى معرفة حقيقة هذا الاسم في اللغة، بل الحاجة إلى إثبات قديم له صفات الكمال، ونفي ما وراء قديم واحد إذا كان ذلك الثاني قائما بالذات موصوفا بصفات الكمال لما عرفنا بطلان ذلك وامتناعه، وما نحن فيه ليس من هذا القبيل. والله الموفق.
(D) And among the verified scholars of the attributes there is one who says: I do not engage with the term 'other' (ghayr) either by negation or by affirmation, but I say: God Almighty is existent and He has attributes whose non-existence is impossible just as it is impossible for the essence. And I affirm these attributes by proof just as I affirmed the essence. And I say: it is inconceivable that the essence remains without the attributes, nor that the attributes remain without the essence. There is no need to name them as 'other' or not to name them. For if in such a case the name 'other' is applied linguistically, I do not care, as long as I do not allow the essence to remain without them, nor them to remain without the essence. I do not allow an 'other' of the essence subsisting by the essence described with attributes of perfection; rather I say that is impossible. And if the name 'other' is not applied to that, then there is also no benefit for me in it. Otherwise, busying oneself with such talk stems from the bankruptcy of the Mu'tazila, because its reality reduces to disagreement about what the name 'other' is applied to in language. We have no need to know the reality of this name in language. Rather, the need is to affirm an eternal [being] having attributes of perfection, and to negate anything besides a single eternal if that second [thing] subsists by the essence described with attributes of perfection, because we have known its falsity and impossibility. And what we are dealing with is not of this category. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents the methodological position of some verified scholars (muhaqqiqun) from among the proponents of attributes (ashab al-sifat). They avoid engaging with the term 'other' (ghayr) altogether, neither affirming nor negating it for the attributes. Instead, they affirm that God exists and has attributes that are necessarily inseparable from His essence (i.e., impossible to cease). They affirm these attributes by proof just as they affirm the essence, without needing to call them 'other' or 'not other'. The key is that the essence cannot exist without the attributes, nor the attributes without the essence. The scholar says that if linguistically the term 'other' is applicable, it does not bother him as long as the inseparable relation is maintained. He explicitly rejects the possibility of an 'other' that subsists by the essence and is described with attributes of perfection — that would be associating a second eternal with God, which is impossible. He criticizes the Mu'tazila for focusing on such linguistic disputes, calling it a sign of their bankruptcy (iflas). The real need is to affirm an eternal being with perfect attributes and to negate any other eternal that could subsist by itself and be described with perfection. The case of divine attributes is not of that kind (i.e., they are not independent entities). Thus, the safe approach is to avoid the term 'ghayr' and stick to the essential affirmations.

Key Terms
محقق (verified scholar)
لفظة الغير (term 'other')
صاحب الصفات (proponent of attributes)
قديم (eternal)
إفلاس (bankruptcy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Maturidi position requires using the term 'ghayr' for attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that some verified scholars actively avoid it, focusing instead on inseparability.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the reason for avoiding the term 'ghayr' for attributes?
A) Because the Mu'tazila invented the term
B) Because the term implies multiplicity of eternals
C) Because the attributes are not real
D) Because the term is linguistically incorrect

Key Takeaway
Verified scholars avoid the term 'other' for attributes because the real concern is maintaining inseparability, not linguistic labeling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Analogy on Divine Speech
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite analogy by denying the particular premise for divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite analogy by distinguishing the genus of divine speech from human speech.

Core Idea
Universal analogy is sound; it's the particular premise that fails for God's speech.

In this passage, Nasafi responds to a key objection from the Mu'tazila who argue that since human speech consists of letters and sounds, divine speech must likewise be composed. Nasafi accepts the general principle of analogy between the seen and unseen, but he shows that the specific claim that all speech is of the genus of letters and sounds is rejected by major Sunni authorities. This distinction preserves the uncreated nature of the divine attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: Nasafi first mentions he could add more arguments, then addresses the specific doubt.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the refutation structure.
2
Identify the Two Premises
The Mu'tazilite argument relies on a universal premise (equivalence of kinds between shahid and gha'ib) and a particular premise (speech in shahid is of letters/sounds). Identify both in the text.
3
Understand Nasafi's Refutation
Nasafi accepts the universal premise but denies the particular premise for divine speech. Notice how he cites Ibn Kullab and al-Qalanisi as authorities who held this distinction.
This shows that the analogy is invalid because the genus is not shared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووراء هذه الطرق طرق آخر معقولة راجعة إلى لطيف الكلام ذكرناها في تصنيف لنا في هذه المسألة على انفراد، تركنا ذكرها هنا لئلا يطول. والله الموفق. وأما الشبهات التي تعلقوا بها فنقول لهم: إن التسوية في الأجناس بين الشاهد والغائب ثابتة في العقول، مقدمة كلية صادقة مسلمة، غير أن ما ذكروا من المقدمة الثانية الجزئية، وهي أن الكلام في الشاهد من جنس الحروف والأصوات مقدمة ممنوعة مجحودة وقع النزاع فيها بين المتكلمين، فذهب عبد الله بن سعيد القطان المعروف بابن كلاب من متقدمي أهل السنة وأئمتهم في الكلام، وأبو العباس القلانسي من متكلمي أهل الحديث إلى أن كلام العباد من جنس الحروف والأصوات وكلام الله ليس من جنس الحروف والأصوات، فيلزمهما هذه الشبهة من
And beyond these ways there are other rational ways relating to subtle speech which we have mentioned in a work of ours on this issue separately; we have omitted them here to avoid length. And Allah is the Giver of success. As for the doubts (shubuhat) which they have clung to, we say to them: The equivalence in kinds between the witnessed (al-shahid) and the absent (al-gha'ib) is established in intellects as a universal premise, true and accepted. However, what they have mentioned as the second particular premise, that speech in the witnessed is of the genus of letters and sounds, is a premise that is denied and rejected; dispute has arisen about it among the mutakallimun. For 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan known as Ibn Kullab, among the early Ahl al-Sunna and their imams in kalam, and Abu al-'Abbas al-Qalanisi among the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith, held that the speech of servants is of the genus of letters and sounds, while the speech of Allah is not of the genus of letters and sounds. This doubt would then apply to them from...
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazilite analogical arguments (shubuhat) that compare divine speech to human speech. Nasafi first notes that there are additional subtle arguments he has developed elsewhere, which he omits for brevity. Then he responds to the specific doubt: The principle of analogical reasoning (taswiya fi al-ajnas) between the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib) is accepted as a universal premise. However, the second premise that 'speech in the witnessed is of the genus of letters and sounds' is contested. Nasafi identifies two early Sunni authorities—Ibn Kullab and al-Qalanisi—who held that human speech is indeed composed of letters and sounds, but divine speech is not. This distinction breaks the analogy. The text ends abruptly (the page cut off), but the point is that the Mu'tazilite argument erroneously assumes a common genus, which the Sunni theologians explicitly deny.

Misconception: Since human speech consists of letters and sounds, divine speech must also consist of letters and sounds.
Correction: Nasafi shows that Allah's speech is not of the genus of letters and sounds; it is a different kind, as held by Ibn Kullab and al-Qalanisi.
