The Samarkand Scholars' Cautious Phrasing on the Quran
Nasafi explains the cautious phrasing of Samarkand scholars regarding the Quran's createdness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Samarkand scholars used cautious wording regarding the Quran's createdness and how they distinguished the eternal attribute from its created expressions.

Core Idea
The eternal Quran is God's attribute, not the physical text.

Nasafi presents the careful theological language of the Maturidi scholars of Samarkand. They affirmed that the Quran as God's speech is uncreated, but they avoided saying 'the Quran is uncreated' in an absolute sense. Why? They feared that listeners might mistakenly think the written and recited words are themselves eternal. This lesson unpacks their precise distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's wording sink in.
2
Identify the twofold statement
Notice that the scholars say both 'the Quran is God's speech and attribute' and 'it is written, recited, memorized' but not residing in these. They distinguish the attribute from its indicators.
3
Understand the reason for caution
They avoid the absolute 'Quran is uncreated' to prevent the misunderstanding that the letters and sounds are eternal. This is a response to the Hanabila.
4
Analyze the analogy
Consider the analogy: God is mentioned by tongues, worshipped in niches, written on paper—without His essence dwelling there. Likewise, the Quranic expressions indicate the attribute without containing it.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
The eternal attribute versus the created physical forms. This distinction is central to Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكانت مشايخنا من أئمة سمرقند (٤) الذين جمعوا بين علم الأصول والفروع، عباراتهم في هذا أن يقولوا: القرآن كلام الله تعالى وصفته وكلام الله غير مخلوق وكذا صفته، ولا يقولون على الإطلاق أن (٥) القرآن غير (٦) مخلوق، لئلا يسبق إلى وهم السامع أن هذه العبارات المتركبة من الحروف والأصوات ليست بمخلوقة، كما يقوله الحنابلة، ويقولون : إن القرآن مكتوب في مصاحفنا، مقروء بألسنتنا، محفوظ في صدورنا، غير (۱) حال فيها، أي: الكتابة الدالة عليه في مصاحفنا، والقراءة الدالة عليه في ألسنتنا ، وحفظ الألفاظ الدالة عليه في صدورنا لا ذاته. كما يقال : الله تعالى مذكور بألسنتنا معبود في محاريبنا، وهو مكتوب على هذه الكاغدة. ولم يرد بذلك حلول ذاته في الألسنة والمحاريب والكاغدة، بل وجود ذكره وعبادته وكتابة العبارات الدالة على ذاته دون ذاته. فكذا هذا.
And our masters among the imams of Samarkand who combined the science of principles and branches, their expressions in this are that they say: The Quran is the speech of Allah the Exalted and His attribute, and the speech of Allah is uncreated, and likewise His attribute. And they do not say absolutely that the Quran is uncreated, lest it come to the mind of the listener that these expressions composed of letters and sounds are not created, as the Hanabila say. And they say: Indeed the Quran is written in our copies, recited by our tongues, memorized in our hearts, not residing in them, i.e., the writing indicating it in our copies, the recitation indicating it on our tongues, and the memorization of the expressions indicating it in our hearts, not its essence. Just as it is said: Allah the Exalted is mentioned by our tongues, worshipped in our prayer niches, and He is written on this paper. But by that it is not intended that His essence resides in tongues, prayer niches, and paper, but rather the existence of His mention, worship, and the writing of the expressions indicating His essence, not His essence. So likewise this.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage explains the cautious theological expression of the Maturidi scholars of Samarkand. They affirm that the Quran is the speech of God and His attribute, and that speech is uncreated. However, they avoid saying 'the Quran is uncreated' as an absolute statement, fearing it may lead listeners to think that the actual written or recited words (composed of letters and sounds) are eternal, which is the position of the Hanabila. Instead, they clarify that the Quran is written in copies, recited by tongues, and memorized in hearts, but the writing, recitation, and memorization are of the expressions that indicate the attribute, not the attribute itself. They draw an analogy: we say God is mentioned by tongues, worshipped in prayer niches, and written on paper, but we do not mean His essence resides there; rather, we mean the acts of mentioning, worshipping, and writing expressions that point to His essence. Similarly, the physical Quranic expressions are created and indicate the eternal speech.

Key Terms
القرآن
غير مخلوق
مشايخنا
مكتوب
محفوظ
حنابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran being uncreated means that the physical book, printed words, or recited sounds are eternal.
Correction: The Samarkand scholars teach that the eternal attribute is the speech of God, not the created letters and sounds that indicate it. The physical copies and recitations are created indicators, not the attribute itself.

Checkpoint
Why do the Samarkand scholars avoid saying 'the Quran is uncreated' absolutely?
A) They believe the Quran is created.
B) They fear confusion that the physical expressions are eternal.
C) They follow the Hanabila.
D) They deny that God has speech.

Key Takeaway
The Samarkand scholars affirmed that the Quran is God's uncreated attribute, while its written and recited expressions are created indicators.

Al-Basri's Linguistic Solution
Al-Basri proposes that speech is attributed to the first speaker by linguistic convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Husayn al-Basri uses linguistic convention to argue that speech is attributed to the original speaker.

Core Idea
Speech is attributed to the first speaker by linguistic custom.

Imam Nasafi has presented an objection regarding speech attribution. Now he introduces a potential solution by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who appeals to the Arabic language's practice of attributing speech to the original speaker. This lesson examines al-Basri's linguistic argument and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp al-Basri's argument.
Pay attention to the examples he uses.
2
Identify al-Basri's main claim
Al-Basri claims that Arabic linguistic practice requires attributing speech to the first speaker in a real sense. Locate this statement.
3
Analyze the two examples
Examine the examples of the Prophet's speech and Qur'an recitation. How do they support the claim? Note the role of linguists in the first example.
The second example emphasizes that recitation is a performance, not original speech.
4
Reflect on the editorial note
Notice that the author includes this passage in al-Basri's own words to show the depth of his knowledge. Consider why this might be important for the wider discussion.
This prepares for subsequent critique by Nasafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأراد أبو الحسين البصري الانفصال عن هذا الكلام فزعم أن اللغة العربية تقتضي إضافة الكلام إلى أول من تكلم به على سبيل الحقيقة، ألا ترى أنا إذا سمعنا كلام النبي من بعض الرواة، قال أهل اللغة: إن هذا الكلام كلام النبي عليه السلام، فيضيفونه إلى أول من تكلم به، وكذلك إذا تلا الواحد منا القرآن أن يقول: هذا كلام الله تعالى بمعنى أن الله تعالى أول من تكلم به والناس يتلونه ويحتدون عليه حكيت السؤال بلفظه ليعلموا مبلغ علم من هو عندهم وإرث علوم أسلافهم، والزائد على من سبقه منهم، الواقف على ما غفل عنه سلفه لجودة قريحته وقوة خاطره وغزارة علمه.
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri wanted to separate from this discourse and claimed that the Arabic language requires attributing speech to the first who spoke it in a real sense. Do you not see that if we hear the speech of the Prophet from some narrators, the people of language say: 'This speech is the speech of the Prophet, peace be upon him,' so they attribute it to the first who spoke it. Similarly, when one of us recites the Qur'an, he says: 'This is the speech of God Most High,' meaning that God Most High is the first who spoke it, and people recite it and conform to it. I have related the question in its wording so that they may know the extent of the knowledge of one who is in their view, and the inheritance of the sciences of their predecessors, and one who exceeds those who preceded him among them, who stands upon what his predecessors neglected, due to the quality of his intellect, the strength of his mind, and the abundance of his knowledge.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who attempts to solve the problem by a linguistic argument. He claims that in Arabic, speech is genuinely attributed to the first speaker. For example, when we hear the Prophet's speech from a narrator, language scholars say it is the speech of the Prophet, because he first spoke it. Similarly, when a person recites the Qur'an, he says 'this is the speech of God' meaning that God is the first speaker, and others recite it, imitating it. The author then adds an editorial remark: he has presented the question in al-Basri's own words so that readers may appreciate the extent of his knowledge and how he surpasses his predecessors.

أول من تكلم

Correction: Imam Nasafi will later critique this view as insufficient, showing that the problem requires deeper theoretical analysis.

Why Not All Properties of the Witnessed Apply to the Unseen
Maturidi defends analogical reasoning by selective property transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars defend analogical reasoning by distinguishing between properties that transfer and those that do not.

Core Idea
Analogical reasoning does not require full equivalence between witnessed and unseen.

This passage addresses a key objection against using analogical reasoning in theology. Critics argue that what holds in the witnessed world may not hold in the unseen. The author responds by showing that even in everyday reasoning, we never transfer all properties—only relevant ones. This insight preserves the validity of analogical arguments without compromising God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Note the rhetorical structure: an objection, a requirement, and an example.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key example
Find the example about 'every agent in the witnessed is a body'. Ask yourself: why does this example support the claim that not all properties transfer?
This example refutes the idea that analogical reasoning requires full equivalence.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this defense applies to other theological arguments, such as God's speech or attributes. What principle is being established about analogical reasoning?
The principle is that only relevant attributes are transferred, not all characteristics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إن كان هذا هكذا في الشاهد فلم ينبغي أن يكون في الغائب هكذا؟ فلابد من إثبات التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب ليمكن الاستدلال وتعدية الحكم إلى الغائب، أليس أن كل فاعل في الشاهد جسم وهو لحم ودم وعظم وعصب ولا يلزم مثله في الغائب فكذا فيما نحن فيه.
Then it is said to them: If this is the case in the witnessed, why should it be the case in the unseen? So it is necessary to establish equality between the witnessed and the unseen in order for inferential reasoning and transference of the ruling to the unseen to be possible. Is it not that every agent in the witnessed is a body, consisting of flesh, blood, bone, and nerve, yet the like is not necessary in the unseen? So likewise regarding what we are discussing.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a methodological defense of analogical reasoning (tamthil) in kalam. The author anticipates an objection: if we argue from the witnessed to the unseen, we must establish equality (taswiya) between them for the inference to work. However, he reminds the critic that we do not transfer all properties—for example, in the witnessed every agent is a body, but we do not conclude that God is a body. So too, the fact that in the witnessed speaking to an absent person is foolish does not force us to say God's speech is foolish. The passage thus preserves analogical reasoning while avoiding anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
التسوية
الاستدلال
تعدية الحكم
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If analogical reasoning is used, all properties of the witnessed must apply to the unseen.
Correction: Only relevant properties transfer; e.g., being an agent does not imply being a body.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to show that not all properties of the witnessed transfer to the unseen?
A) Every agent in the witnessed is a body, but God is not a body.
B) Speaking to an absent person is foolish in the witnessed, so God's speech would be foolish.
C) The Prophet's address to future generations was foolish.
D) The unseen is completely unknowable.

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning in kalam is valid because it transfers only relevant attributes, not all characteristics of the witnessed.

Response to the Scriptural Objection about the Arabic Qur'an
Refutation of scriptural objection via expression-meaning distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a scriptural objection based on the Arabic nature of the Qur'an is refuted by distinguishing between expression and meaning.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's Arabic expression does not limit its divine reality.

In this lesson, we examine a classical response to an objection that the Qur'an being Arabic makes it a human creation or limited. The author clarifies that the verse "Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Qur'an" refers to the language of expression, not the essence of God's speech. This distinction safeguards the Qur'an's divine origin while acknowledging its Arabic form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the author begins by stating he is solving a scriptural objection.
2
Identify the objection and the solution
The objection uses the verse 'Inna ja'alnahu Qur'anan 'Arabiyyan' to argue something about the Qur'an. Find how the author dismisses the objection and what interpretation he gives.
The key move is distinguishing between expression (عبارة) and the thing expressed.
3
Focus on the key term 'عبارة'
The author says the verse means 'We made the expression of it in the tongue of the Arabs.' This term 'ibarah (expression) is crucial: it is the linguistic form, not the divine speech itself.
This distinction protects the belief that the Qur'an is uncreated and eternal in its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما حل الشبهة السمعية فنقول : لا تعلق لهم بقوله تعالى: إِنَّا جَعَلْنَهُ قُرْءَانَا عَرَبِيًّا لأن معناه والله أعلم ، جعلنا العبارة عنه بلسان العرب وأفهمنا المراد به
As for the solution of the scriptural objection, we say: They have no connection to His saying, Exalted: "Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Qur'an" because its meaning, and God knows best, is: We made the expression of it in the tongue of the Arabs and We made understood the intended meaning.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This brief passage responds to a scriptural objection (shubha sam'iyya). The opponent likely cited the verse 'Inna ja'alnahu Qur'anan 'Arabiyyan' to argue that the Qur'an is limited to Arabic and therefore subject to change or human influence. The author refutes this by explaining that the verse refers to the expression ('ibarah) in Arabic, not to the essence or truth of the divine speech. The meaning is understood through the Arabic language but is not confined by it.

Key Terms
شبهة سمعية
عبارة
لسان العرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Inna ja'alnahu Qur'anan 'Arabiyyan' proves that the Qur'an is a human creation because it is in Arabic.
Correction: The verse refers only to the expression in Arabic; the divine speech itself is beyond language and not confined to Arabic.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret the verse 'إنا جعلناه قرآنا عربيا'?
A) It means God created the Qur'an as Arabic
B) It means the expression is in Arabic, while the meaning is understood
C) It proves the Qur'an is created
D) It denies the possibility of translation

Key Takeaway
The verse about the Arabic Qur'an refers to the expression in Arabic, not to the divine speech itself.

?

How does this lesson challenge you to look beyond the words to the intended meaning (al-murad)? What will you do today to seek that deeper understanding in your recitation?
?
Have you ever felt limited by your inability to understand Arabic, thinking that the Qur'an's Arabic nature excludes you from its full reality? If so, how does this lesson's clarification that the Arabic is only the expression—not the essence—change your sense of access to God's speech? Will you now approach the Qur'an with more confidence?

Think of a recent moment when you read a verse of the Qur'an and felt it was 'just words' or culturally bound. In light of this lesson, what responsibility do you have to distinguish between the divine speech and its Arabic expression? Will you commit to studying the meanings of at least one unfamiliar verse this week?

The Linguistic Rule of Ja'ala and Its Application
Imam Nasafi uses linguistic analysis of the verb ja'ala to refute opponents' interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic rule that the verb 'ja'ala' changes meaning based on its transitivity, and how Imam Nasafi uses this to refute opponents' interpretations.

Core Idea
The verb 'ja'ala' means 'create' with one object, but 'name' with two.

This passage demonstrates the precision of Arabic grammar in theological debate. By analyzing the transitivity of a single verb, Imam Nasafi dismantles an opponent's argument. We will explore how the same word can carry different meanings, and why this matters for understanding the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi relies on authoritative linguists ('ahl al-lugha').
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios for 'ja'ala': transitive to one object vs. two objects. Find the rule associated with each.
The key difference is between 'khalq' (creation) and 'tasmiya' (naming).
3
Examine the Quranic examples
Look at the verses quoted: one for creation (darkness and light) and two for naming (angels as females, Quran as parts). See how they illustrate the rule.
The two-object verses are about disbelievers' false claims.
4
Explore the multiple interpretations
Nasafi offers three possible meanings for 'new reminder' in the verse. Note each interpretation and how they weaken the opponent's argument.
The third interpretation uses an analogy with a 'new slave'.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why the existence of multiple valid interpretations refutes the opponents' claim that the verse means something specific.
This shows the importance of linguistic analysis in theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحكامه باللسان العربي. ثم إن أهل اللغة قالوا: إذا تعدى الجعل إلى مفعول واحد كان بمعنى الفعل والخلق كقوله تعالى: الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِى خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ وَجَعَلَ الظُّلُمَاتِ وَالنُّورَ وإذا تعدى إلى المفعولين لا يكون بمعنى الخلق بل يكون بمعنى الحكم والتسمية. قال الله تعالى خبرا عن الكفرة: وَجَعَلُوا الْمَلَائِكَةَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ عِبَادُ الرَّحْمَنِ إِنَاثًا. والمراد منه التسمية لا التخليق. وكذا في قوله: الَّذِينَ جَعَلُوا الْقُرْآنَ عِضِينَ المراد منه التسمية لا التخليق، وفي هذه الآية تتعدى إلى مفعولين. والمراد من قوله تعالى: مَا يَأْتِيهِم مِّن ذِكْرٍ مِّن رَّبِّهِم مُحْدَثٍ يحتمل أن الذكر هو الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم على ما قال: ذِكْرُ رَسُولٍ فيكون تأويله ما يأتيهم من رسول محدث استمعوه أي سمعوا قوله ويحتمل أن يكون المراد منه ما يأتيهم من وعظ من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وهذا لأنهم ما كانوا يضحكون عند قراءة القرآن بل كانوا يعظمون ويفخمون شأنه، ويحتمل من ذكر حديث العهد بهم، لا أن يكون في نفسه حديثا، كمن له غلام فاشترى آخر قد يقول: ادعوا غلامي الحديث وإن كان أكبر سنا من الأول، على أن صرف هذه الآيات إلى هذه العبارات المحدثة ممكن فلم يبق للخصوم في محل النزاع دليل
And its rulings in the Arabic language. Then the linguists said: When 'ja'ala' is transitive to one object, it means action and creation, like His saying: 'Praise be to Allah who created the heavens and the earth and made the darkness and the light.' And when it is transitive to two objects, it does not mean creation but rather means judgment and naming. Allah said, informing about the disbelievers: 'And they made the angels, who are servants of the Most Merciful, females.' The intended meaning is naming, not creating. Likewise in His saying: 'Those who made the Quran into parts,' the intended meaning is naming, not creating, and in this verse it is transitive to two objects. And the intended meaning of His saying: 'No reminder comes to them from their Lord that is new' – it is possible that the reminder is the Messenger, peace be upon him, as He said: 'a reminder of a messenger,' so its interpretation is that no new messenger comes to them but they listen to him, i.e., they hear his word. And it is possible that the intended meaning is that no exhortation comes to them from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and this is because they were not laughing upon hearing the Quran but rather were magnifying and glorifying its status. And it is possible that it means a reminder of recent acquaintance with them, not that it is recent in itself, like one who has a slave and buys another; he may say: 'Call my new slave' even if he is older in age than the first. So since it is possible to direct these verses to these new expressions, no proof remains for the opponents in the point of contention.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic argument used by Imam Nasafi to refute opponents who misuse Quranic verses. He first explains the rule: the verb 'ja'ala' when transitive to one object means creation, but when transitive to two objects it means naming or declaring. He cites Quranic examples: 'He made the darkness and the light' (one object) means created, while 'they made the angels females' (two objects) means they named them females, not created them. Similarly, 'those who made the Quran into parts' means they named it parts. Then he addresses the verse 'No reminder comes to them from their Lord that is new.' He offers three interpretations: (1) 'reminder' means the Messenger himself, so 'new reminder' means a new messenger; (2) it means a new exhortation, and the disbelievers mocked it (implied elsewhere); (3) 'new' means recent in relation to them, not in itself, like calling a newly purchased slave 'my new slave' even if older. Since these interpretations are possible, the opponents cannot use this verse as evidence against the Quran's eternity. The argument shows flexibility in interpreting ambiguous verses to defend orthodox positions.

Key Terms
جعل (ja'ala)
تعدية (transitivity)
تسمية (naming)
تخليق (creating)
ذكر (reminder)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verb 'ja'ala' always means 'to create'.
Correction: When transitive to two objects, 'ja'ala' means 'to name' or 'to declare', not 'to create'.

Checkpoint
What does the verb 'ja'ala' mean when it is transitive to two objects?
A) Creation
B) Naming or declaring
C) Making
D) Doing

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi uses the transitivity of 'ja'ala' to show that opponents' interpretations of Quranic verses are not definitive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you take the time to examine the grammar and syntax, or do you jump to conclusions based on your assumptions? This lesson shows how opponents made a critical error by ignoring the transitivity of a single verb. How would embracing this humility change the way you discuss the Quran with others?
?
The opponents twisted the meaning of the Quran to support their own doctrines. Examine your own heart: have you ever misused a verse—taken it out of context or forced a meaning—to justify a personal opinion or to win an argument? What concrete step will you take this week to ensure you interpret Allah's words faithfully, even when it challenges your own views?

The Terminological Nature of the Dispute on Hearing Allah's Speech
Nasafi reports al-Isfarayini's adoption of Maturidi's view and the claim that the dispute is merely verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini argued that the apparent disagreement among scholars on hearing Allah's speech is merely a difference in expression, not in reality.

Core Idea
The dispute on hearing Allah's speech is terminological, not substantive.

This passage reports a key moment in the development of the Maturidi-Ash'ari discussion on hearing Allah's speech. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, an Ash'ari scholar, not only adopted the view that non-sound cannot be heard but also claimed that earlier scholars all agreed on this point. He argued that any differences were only in how they expressed the same underlying reality. This lesson examines his claim and the two expressions he outlines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the claim that the disagreement is only in expression (al-ibarah) and not in reality (al-haqiqah).
The phrase 'كان عا' is unclear; the meaning is likely 'it became known' or similar.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions two groups among the scholars. One group considers the reality of hearing and says only sound is heard. The other group says that when sound is heard, the inner speech (kalam nafsi) becomes known indirectly. Identify these two positions.
Both groups agree that non-sound cannot be heard directly; they differ only in what is meant by 'hearing'.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Isfarayini concludes that the disagreement is in naming (tasmiyah), not in reality. Reflect on why this matters for the broader theological debate on Allah's speech.
This claim aims to reconcile the views of the Ash'aris and the Maturidis on the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعده إلى هذا القول أبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني من جملة الأشعرية وذكر بعض الأشعرية أن أبا إسحاق أول من ذهب إلى هذا القول من متكلمى أهل الحديث. ولم يرض أبو إسحاق باختياره هذا المذهب حتى ادعى أن جميع من تقدمه من متكلمي أهل الحديث على هذا، واتفقوا على أن لا يمكن سماع ما ليس بصوت إلا أن اختلافهم في العبارة. فمنهم من اعتبر حقيقة السماع فقال : لا يسمع إلا الصوت، ومنهم من قال: لما سمع الصوت صار ما علم بعده ما دل عليه الصوت من الكلام القائم بالنفس وذات المتكلم معلوما، فلما صار ذلك معلوما بحاسة السمع بواسطة سماع الصوت كان عا، فإذا هذا منهم اختلاف في التسمية لا في الحقيقة. وذكر هذا في كتابه المسمى بترتيب المذهب. حكيته لا بلفظه.
And after him, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini went to this view, among the Ash'aris. And some of the Ash'aris mentioned that Abu Ishaq was the first to adopt this view among the mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith. And Abu Ishaq was not satisfied with his choice of this doctrine until he claimed that all those who preceded him among the mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith were upon this, and they agreed that it is not possible to hear what is not a sound, except that their disagreement is in expression. For among them are those who considered the reality of hearing and said: 'Nothing is heard except sound.' And among them are those who said: 'When the sound is heard, what is known after it becomes known – that which the sound indicates of the speech subsisting in the soul and the essence of the speaker. So when that becomes known by the sense of hearing through the intermediary of hearing the sound, it is [??].' So this among them is a disagreement in naming, not in reality. And he mentioned this in his book called Tartib al-Madhhab. I have narrated it, not in his wording.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage reports that later Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini adopted al-Maturidi's view that Allah's speech is not heard as sound. The text notes that some Ash'aris claim al-Isfarayini was the first among the mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith to hold this position. Al-Isfarayini went further and asserted that all earlier scholars of ahl al-hadith agreed that it is impossible to hear what is not sound, and that any apparent disagreement is only in expression (ibarah), not in reality (haqiqah). The passage then explains two ways of expressing the same reality: some consider the reality of hearing and say only sound is heard; others say that when sound is heard, the meaning it indicates (kalam nafsi) becomes known through hearing, and so it can be said that the speech is heard metaphorically. Thus, the disagreement is terminological. The source is al-Isfarayini's book Tartib al-Madhhab, which al-Nasafi says he has related in meaning, not verbatim.

Key Terms

الحقيقة
الكلام النفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the scholars disagreed on the substance of whether Allah's speech can be heard.
Correction: Al-Isfarayini claimed that all scholars agreed that non-sound cannot be heard; the disagreement is only in terminology—whether to call the indirect knowledge 'hearing' or not.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The apparent disagreement on hearing Allah's speech is not about the core doctrine but about how to express the same underlying reality.

The Four Topics on Takwin and Mukawwan
The author enumerates the four discussion topics on takwin and mukawwan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four interconnected topics that structure the discussion about takwin (divine bringing-into-existence) and mukawwan (the thing brought into existence).

Core Idea
The debate on takwin has four logical steps, not independent claims.

Having completed the first topic regarding the Mu'tazila's argument about an eternal attribute, the author now maps out the remaining three topics that form the core of the discussion on takwin and mukawwan. This passage serves as a roadmap, showing how the issues of distinction, eternity, and implications are linked. Let's examine each topic and see how they build on each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall structure.
Notice the author uses 'الثاني', 'الثالث', 'الرابع' for enumeration.
2
Identify the four topics
Find the four topics listed. The first is mentioned as completed. List the remaining three in your own words.
The second topic: takwin is not the same as mukawwan. Third: takwin is eternal. Fourth: eternity of takwin does not imply eternity of mukawwan.
3
Understand the logical flow
Think about how these topics are connected. Why does the order matter? The second establishes distinction, the third asserts eternity, the fourth prevents a misinterpretation.
This flow guards against the misconception that an eternal takwin means eternal created things.
4
Reflect on the broader debate
Note that the author says the discussion covers both the Mu'tazila and the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith. This shows that these topics are central to multiple schools.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتزلة في جواز وجود صفة أزلية بذات الباري وقد فرغنا عنه بحمد الله. والثاني: في أن التكون غير المكون. والثالث: في أن التكون أزلي. والرابع: أن القول بقدم التكوين لا يؤدي إلى القول بقدم المكونات. والكلام في هذه المواضع الثلاثة يعم المعتزلة ومتكلمى أهل الحديث فنبدأ ببيان شبههم فنقول:

And the fourth: that the statement of the eternity of takwin does not lead to the statement of the eternity of the things brought into existence. Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a classification that outlines the structure of the discussion about takwin (divine bringing-into-existence) and mukawwan (the thing brought into existence). The author lists four topics. The first topic, already completed, concerned the Mu'tazila's argument about whether an eternal attribute can subsist in God's essence. The second topic is that takwin is distinct from the mukawwan. The third topic is that takwin is eternal (i.e., it is an attribute that has always been with God). The fourth topic is that affirming the eternity of takwin does not imply the eternity of the created things themselves. The author notes that the remaining three topics apply to both the Mu'tazila and the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith. He then states that they will begin by clarifying the doubtful evidences (shubah) of those who claim that takwin and mukawwan are identical. This passage serves as a roadmap for the reader, showing the logical progression of the debate.

Correction: The topics are interconnected: proving that takwin is other than mukawwan and eternal does not necessitate the eternity of the world. The fourth topic explicitly addresses this potential misunderstanding.

Figurative Language in Creedal vs. Legal Matters
Refutation of Ash'ari objection on figurative language, with distinction between legal and creedal matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on using figurative language in theological arguments, specifically why it is permissible in legal rulings but not in creedal matters.

Core Idea
Figurative language gives probability, but creed demands certainty.

In this passage, the author of the Tabṣirah responds to an Ash'ari objection about using figurative interpretations. He argues that while metaphors are linguistically valid, they do not produce certain knowledge. This leads to a crucial distinction between legal rulings, which can be based on preponderant opinion, and creedal matters, which require decisive proof. The Maturidi methodology thus restricts figurative interpretation in matters of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice how the author structures his response to the objector.
The passage has a clear dialectical structure: objection, response, principle, and distinction.
2
Identify the linguistic argument
The author explains that in Arabic, an infinitive can stand for the object, as in 'knowledge' meaning 'the known thing'. Understand this grammatical point that grounds the interpretation.
This shows the author is not denying figurative language altogether, but clarifying its conditions.
3
Understand the effect of probability on certainty
The author says that metaphor does not yield certainty, and even if no evidence is present now, it may be present later. This leads to the principle of waqf (suspension) for general verses.
Waqf means withholding judgment until evidence arrives.
4
Distinguish legal from creedal matters
See how the author distinguishes between matters of law, where preponderant opinion suffices, and matters of creed, where certain proof is required. This is the core Maturidi position.
This distinction is key to understanding Maturidi methodology in dealing with ambiguous texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لنا دلائل أخر سمعية تركنا ذكرها لما يطول فيها الكلام وفيما تعلقنا به من الآية دلالة كافية معجزة للخصوم عن التفوه بما يورث أدنى شبهة. الرد على الشبه السمعية للأشعرية: ما كان محتملا ثم نشتغل بالجواب عن شبهة السمعية لنتفرغ للمحاجة العقلية فنقول : مما أنكرت على من يقول لك أن المراد بقوله : هَذَا خَلْقُ اللَّهِ فَأَرُونِي مَاذَا خَلَقَ الَّذِينَ مِن دُونِهِ وغيره من الآيات، هذا مخلوق الله إذ لا وجه لك إلى إنكار جواز إقامة المصدر مقام المفعول في اللغة، كما في العلم والقدرة إذ هما يذكران ويراد بهما ما يتعلقان به من المعلوم والمقدور؟ فإن قلت : هذا مجازا فلا يصار إليه إلا بعد قيام دليل قيل: إن للمجاز لا يكون موجبا للعلم قطعا ويقينا، وإن لم يقم بعد دليل المجاز لجواز أن يقوم، ولهذا نقول بالوقف في الآية العامة وإن لم يقارنها دليل التخصيص لما أن الاحتمال ينافي العلم ، فأما إذا كان إحدى الجهتين أرجح من الأخرى فقد يوجب علم الرأي فيجوز الاحتجاج به في مسائل الأحكام إذ هي تثبت بغالب الرأي، فأما في المسائل الاعتقادية التي لا وجه إلى القول بثبوتها إلا بإقامة دليل موجب للعمل قطعا فلا وجه إلى التعلق به وهو مذهب شيخنا أبي منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله
Then we have other auditory proofs which we have omitted mentioning because it would be lengthy, and in what we have clung to from the verse there is sufficient proof, disabling the opponents from uttering anything that would produce the least doubt. The refutation of the auditory doubts of the Ash'aris: 'What is probable.' Then we engage in the answer to the auditory doubt to free ourselves for rational disputation. We say: What do you deny of the one who says to you that the intended meaning of His saying 'This is the creation of Allah' and other verses is 'This is the created thing of Allah'? For you have no basis to deny the permissibility of replacing the infinitive with the object in language, as in 'knowledge' and 'power', for they are mentioned and what is meant by them is what they pertain to of the known and the empowered. If you say: This is a metaphor (majaz), and one should not resort to it except after the establishment of evidence. It is said: Indeed, metaphor does not yield certain and definitive knowledge, and even if evidence for metaphor has not been established, it is possible that it will be established. For this reason, we declare suspension (waqf) regarding a general verse even if it is not accompanied by evidence of specification, because probability negates certainty. But if one of the two sides is more preponderant, it may yield knowledge of opinion, so it is permissible to use it as evidence in legal matters since they are established by preponderant opinion. As for creedal matters, which have no way of being established except by the establishment of evidence that yields certain knowledge, there is no way to rely on it. And this is the doctrine of our master Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage explains the Maturidi methodology regarding figurative language (majaz) in interpreting texts. The author acknowledges that figurative language is permissible in Arabic grammar, but it does not produce certain knowledge. Therefore, in creedal matters ('aqa'id), which require certain proofs, one cannot rely on metaphorical interpretations unless there is decisive evidence. In contrast, in legal rulings (ahkam), where preponderant opinion suffices, metaphorical interpretation can be used. This is the position of al-Maturidi. The passage also mentions that if a verse is general and there is no evidence for specification, we suspend judgment (waqf) because probability prevents certainty.

عام
تخصيص
مسائل أحكام
مسائل اعتقادية
ماتريدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all figurative language is unacceptable in theological arguments.
Correction: The author limits the restriction to creedal matters; figurative language can be used in legal rulings when supported by preponderant evidence.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, when can figurative interpretation be used in creedal matters?
A) Always.
B) Only when there is no other option.
C) Never, because it does not yield certainty.
D) Only in legal matters.

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, figurative language may be used in legal reasoning but not in creedal matters, because creed requires certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Can you pinpoint the exact evidence (from the Qur'an or Sunnah) that made you certain of it, or have you accepted an interpretation that is merely probable? If the latter, what step will you take today to seek decisive proof?
?
In your daily life, you make countless decisions based on what seems likely. But when it comes to your faith, have you ever settled for 'likely' where only certainty should stand? Identify one area of your creed where you have been content with preponderant opinion. How will you move from opinion to certainty?
?
The author says that in creedal matters, evidence must yield certain knowledge (دليل موجب للعمل قطعا). Examine your heart: are there any articles of faith you hold because 'most scholars say so' rather than because you have personally verified the decisive proof? Will you commit to investigating them until certainty is reached?

The Opponent's Logical Dilemma
Refutes opponent's claim about ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma the author uses to refute the opponent's reliance on ambiguous verses.

Core Idea
The opponent's position is self-defeating: either he abandons scripture or his proof invalidates his case.

The author presents a sharp logical trap. The opponent claims the verses in question are ambiguous and that the author's metaphorical interpretation requires evidence. The author shows that any move the opponent makes leads to a dead end, revealing the weakness of his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice how the author constructs a conditional argument with two branches.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فإذا قلت إن الدلائل المعقولة تدل عليه' — this is the pivot.
2
Identify the dilemma
The opponent's claim that verses are ambiguous leads to a choice: either he provides rational proof for metaphor or he does not. Trace both outcomes and see how each weakens his position.
The author shows that if the proof is sound, the opponent no longer needs the verses; if unsound, his scriptural argument collapses.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final statement: 'كيفما دارت القصة ظهر سفهك في التعلق بهذه الآيات.' This is a strong rhetorical conclusion that exposes the opponent's foolishness.
The dilemma shows that the opponent cannot coherently argue that the verses are ambiguous without self-defeating consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا وجه لك إذا إلى التعلق بهذه الآيات المحتملة للمجاز في محل النزاع، إلا إذا أقمت الدلالة على نفي احتمال المجاز، ولا دلالة على ذلك معك فإذا قلت إن الدلائل المعقولة تدل عليه فقد تركت الاحتجاج بالآية واشتغلت بالمعقول، وإن صح دليلك فقد وقعت لك الغنية عن التعلق بهذه الآيات، وإن لم يصح بطل احتجاجك بالآيات فكيفما دارت القصة ظهر سفهك في التعلق بهذه الآيات.
Then there is no way for you to cling to these verses that are open to metaphorical interpretation in the point of contention, unless you provide evidence negating the possibility of metaphor, and you have no evidence for that. So if you say that rational proofs indicate it, then you have abandoned arguing by the verse and occupied yourself with the rational. If your proof is sound, then you have no need for clinging to these verses; if it is not sound, your argument by the verses becomes invalid. However the situation turns, your foolishness in clinging to these verses becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's position that the verses in question are ambiguous and that the author's metaphorical interpretation requires evidence. The author argues that the opponent cannot simply claim the verses are ambiguous without proof. If the opponent relies on rational arguments to deny the possibility of metaphor, he has left the scriptural argument behind. If his rational proof is sound, then he no longer needs the verses; if it is unsound, his scriptural case collapses. Thus, the opponent is caught in a dilemma, revealing the weakness of his position.

Linguistic Proof that Khalq Means Makhluq
Linguistic proof that khalq is originally for makhluq, not metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic argument that the Arabic word 'khalq' originally refers to the created thing (makhluq), not the act of creation, and how this refutes the claim that its use in certain verses is a metaphor.

Core Idea
Khalq originally means the created thing, not a metaphor for creation.

In this passage, the author presents a key linguistic argument in the debate about whether verses mentioning 'khalq' refer to the act of creation or the created entity. He challenges the common assumption that using an infinitive form for its object is always metaphorical, asserting instead that 'khalq' in its original usage names the created thing itself. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for interpreting scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the author's assertive tone and the technical terms.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Notice the author's view: 'khalq' originally means makhluq. Contrast with the opponent's view: 'khalq' is a masdar (infinitive) used metaphorically for the created thing.
The author accuses the opponent of ignorance of Arabic.
3
Understand the linguistic evidence
The author claims that all linguists agree that 'khalq' is a word for the created thing in the original language, not a metaphorical substitution.
He compares it to wujud (existence) being the same as mawjud (the existent).
4
Analyze the refutation
The opponent says 'khalq' as masdar refers to a divine attribute, and using it for the created thing is metaphor. The author refutes by saying the masdar here refers to the essence of the created thing.
This shifts the dispute from metaphor to whether the word denotes the attribute or the entity.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this linguistic point matters for interpreting Quranic verses about creation. The author argues that the opponent's reliance on such verses is futile without establishing this premise.
This shows how linguistic analysis can underpin theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نقيم الدلالة على أن المراد من الآيات هو المخلوق دون الخلق بطريق المجاز، وحمل الكلام على المجاز جائز بالدليل فمن الدلائل ما أقمنا من الدلالة السمعية التي لا مجال للشبهة في إبطال الاحتجاج بها على ما سبق ذكره، ونقيم بعد هذا الدلائل العقلية إن شاء الله تعالى على أن قولك: إن إقامة المصدر [؟] مقام المفعول مجاز فلا يصار إليه إلا بدليل كلام فاسد جدا لأنك إذا أنكرت أن لفظة الخلق ليست بمصدر والمخلوق ليس بمفعول، وذكر الخلق والمراد به المخلوق ليس بطريق إقامة المصدر مقام المفعول بل هو موضع في أصل اللغة للمخلوق. فقد نادى عليك جميع أهل اللغة بالإنكار، وظهر جهلك بلسان العرب ومواصفاتهم وموازينهم وصيغاتهم التي استعملوها لتفاهم ما في الضمائر بها، وأحد لا يمكنه إنكار هذا، وطريق سلفك أنهم يقولون : إن الخلق إن كان في اللغة مصدرا إلا أنه ينبئ عن معنى ذلك المعنى راجع إلى غير المفعول، وأنه ليس بمعنى وراءه كالوجود مع الموجود سواء، فمن حقك أن تقول ذكر الخلق وأراد به المصدر إلا أن المصدر ها هنا راجع إلى ذات المخلوق، لاستحالة أن يكون غيره، وخصمك يقول : إن المصدر ها هنا صفة الله تعالى قائمة به والمخلوق ما يتعلق بتلك الصفة إلا أنه ذكر لفظ المصدر وأراد به المخلوق مجازا، فتقول أنت : إنه ما أراد به المفعول وإنما أراد به حقيقة المصدر إلا أن المصدر ها هنا معنى راجع إلى ذات المخلوق فحاصل المنازعة بينك وبين خصمك في التعلق بهذه الآيات راجع إلى هذا، ولا وجه لك للتعلق بهذه الآيات إلا بعد ثبوت هذه المقدمة وبثبوتها يقع لك الغنية عن التعلق بالآيات على ما قررنا.
But we provide evidence that what is intended by the verses is the created thing (al-makhluq) not the act of creation (al-khalq) by way of metaphor (majaz), and carrying speech metaphorically is permissible with evidence. Among the evidences is what we have presented of scriptural evidence (al-dalāla al-sam‘iyya) that leaves no room for doubt in invalidating argumentation through it, as previously mentioned. And after this we will present rational proofs (al-dalā’il al-‘aqliyya) — God willing — that your statement: 'Using the masdar [?] in place of the maf‘ul is a metaphor (majāz) and it should not be resorted to except with evidence' is very corrupt speech. Because if you deny that the word 'khalq' is not a masdar and the created thing (al-makhluq) is not a maf‘ul, and that mentioning 'khalq' while intending the created thing is not by way of placing the masdar in the position of the maf‘ul, rather it is a position in the original language for the created thing. Then all the linguists have cried out against you in denial, and your ignorance of the language of the Arabs, their attributes, their measures, and their forms that they used to understand what is in their minds has become apparent, and no one can deny this. And the way of your predecessors is that they say: 'Indeed khalq, even if it is a masdar in the language, it indicates a meaning that returns to other than the maf‘ul, and it is not a meaning beyond it, like existence (al-wujūd) with the existent (al-mawjūd) being the same.' So it is your right to say: 'He mentioned khalq and intended by it the masdar, but the masdar here refers to the essence of the created thing (dhāt al-makhluq) because it is impossible that it be other than it.' And your opponent says: 'The masdar here is an attribute of God Most High subsisting in Him, and the created thing is that which is related to that attribute, but He mentioned the word of the masdar and intended by it the created thing metaphorically.' So you say: 'He did not intend by it the maf‘ul, rather He intended by it the reality of the masdar, but the masdar here is a meaning referring to the essence of the created thing.' So the substance of the dispute between you and your opponent concerning clinging to these verses reduces to this. And you have no way to cling to these verses except after establishing this premise, and with its establishment you have no need for clinging to the verses, as we have decided.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic proof and a classification of the word 'khalq'. The author first asserts that he provides evidence that the intended meaning in the verses is the created thing (makhluq) via metaphor, and that metaphorical interpretation is permissible with evidence. He then attacks the opponent's claim that using a masdar (infinitive) for the maf‘ul (object) is a metaphor that requires a proof. The author argues that this claim is corrupt because the opponent denies that 'khalq' is not a masdar and that the created thing is not a maf‘ul, but in fact in the original language, 'khalq' is a word for the created thing, not a metaphorical substitution. He accuses the opponent of ignorance of Arabic. He then explains the opponent's view: that 'khalq' as masdar refers to a divine attribute, and using it for the created thing is a metaphor. The author responds: the masdar here actually refers to the essence of the created thing. Thus, the dispute is about whether the word in the verses refers to the attribute or the created entity. The author concludes that the opponent's reliance on the verses is pointless without establishing this premise, and even if established, the verses become unnecessary.

Correction: The author argues that 'khalq' is originally a word for the created thing, not a metaphorical use of the masdar. In some cases, the masdar inherently refers to the entity, like wujud and mawjud.

The word 'khalq' in Arabic originally refers to the created thing, not the act of creation, so interpreting it as a metaphorical use of the infinitive is linguistically unsound.

Nasafi's Refutation Using Sibawayh's Verb Definition
Nasafi refutes an opponent who misuses Sibawayh's verb definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a grammatical argument to refute the Ash'ari denial of creaturely action.

Core Idea
Grammar witnesses for creatures' actions; contradictions expose flawed theology.

Nasafi here meets an opponent on his own ground—grammar. The opponent had cited Sibawayh's definition of the verb to argue that only God creates actions. Nasafi responds with a devastating dilemma: either you accept all of Sibawayh, which includes his affirmation of creaturely verbs, or you reject his authority, and your proof collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the structure of Nasafi's argument.
Notice the sharp tone of the critique.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Note that the opponent uses Sibawayh's verb definition to deny that creatures truly act.
3
Understand the dilemma
Nasafi gives two options: if the opponent rejects Sibawayh's statement, the proof fails; if he accepts it, then Sibawayh affirms creaturely action.
4
Analyze the conclusion
Nasafi points out that Sibawayh's own example ('Zayd was struck, it rises by its verb') shows that verbs are attributed to creatures, contradicting al-Ash'ari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما استدل به من قول سيبويه في حد الفعل أنه أمثلة أخذت من لفظة إحداث الأسماء إلى آخر الفصل. فنقول: إن هذا الرجل تعاطى صناعة نظر إليها من وراء الجبال لا من وراء الجدار، ولو عرف موضوعات أهل النحو وما هو فعل عندهم وما ليس بفعل وما هو مفعول لهم وما ليس بمفعول. لما اشتغل بمثل هذا الكلام الدال على خلوه من هذه الصناعة. وقبل أن نشرع في شرح هذا فنقول: أكل ما أطلقه مأخوذ به؟ فإن قال: لا أبطل احتجاجه به والتعلق سيبويه صحيح بقوله. وإن قال: نعم، قيل: أن سيبويه أثبت للعباد فعلا، فإنه قال في قوله: ضرب زيد ارتفع بفعله، فلم زعم صاحبكم الأشعري أن لا فعل في الحقيقة لغير الله تعالى وخالف سيبويه
And what he deduced from the saying of Sibawayh concerning the definition of the verb (al-fi'l) that it is patterns taken from the phrase of the events (ahdāth) of the names (asmā') to the end of the chapter. We say: This man has engaged in a craft he views from beyond the mountains, not from beyond the wall. Had he known the subjects of the grammarians – what is a verb according to them, what is not a verb, what is an object for them, and what is not an object – he would not have busied himself with such speech, which indicates his emptiness from this craft. Before we begin explaining this, we say: Is everything he stated to be accepted? If he says: No, his proof using it and his attachment to Sibawayh's correct statement is invalid. If he says: Yes, it is said: Indeed Sibawayh affirmed for servants an action (fi'l); for he said in his statement 'Zayd was struck, it rises by its verb (fi'l)' – so why did your companion al-Ash'ari claim that in reality there is no action for other than Allah, contradicting Sibawayh?
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Nasafi begins by describing how an opponent has misapplied Sibawayh's definition of the verb (fi'l) – that it is patterns derived from the verbal nouns of events – to argue for a theological point. He criticizes the opponent's lack of expertise in grammar, then presents a dilemma: either you accept everything Sibawayh said or not. If you reject his statement, your proof collapses. If you accept it, then Sibawayh explicitly attributes actions (fi'l) to creatures, as in his example 'Zayd was struck, it rises by its verb (fi'l)', meaning the verb indicates the action of Zayd. So why does al-Ash'ari deny real creaturely action and thus contradict Sibawayh? This exposes the inconsistency of the Ash'ari position when relying on grammatical authorities.

Sibawayh's Verb Definition and the Unity of Name and Named
Nasafi explains Sibawayh's verb definition and the Sunni position on name and named.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological significance of Sibawayh's definition of the verb and the Sunni position that the name and the named are identical, as well as al-Ash'ari's alternative view and the inconsistency it creates.

Core Idea
The name and the named are one in Sunni doctrine—a key principle with linguistic and theological roots.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla begins by clarifying a grammatical definition from Sibawayh, then reveals its deeper theological implications. By linking the name-named identity to Sunni orthodoxy, Nasafi sets up a powerful critique of al-Ash'ari's own classification. Before we examine the argument, let's understand the linguistic backdrop that makes this discussion significant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the sequence: definition, objection, defense, then Ash'ari's view, then challenge.
Pay attention to the technical terms: ism, musammā, tasmiya.
2
Identify the key positions
Identify three positions: Sibawayh's (interpreted by Sunnis), al-Mubarrad's, and al-Ash'ari's. Notice how Nasafi aligns himself with the 'people of the craft' and Ahl al-Sunna.
The Sunni position is that ism and musammā are one.
3
Understand the inconsistency argument
Nasafi ends with a rhetorical question: since al-Ash'ari rejects Sibawayh's view, how can he demand others accept it? This exposes a methodological inconsistency.
This is a common kalam tactic—using the opponent's own sources against them.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why the ism-musamma issue matters for divine attributes. If names and named are identical, then God's names are not separate from His essence, a crucial point in Sunni theology.
This connects to later discussions on the nature of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: حد الفعل أمثلة أخذت من لفظ أحداث الأسماء، وأراد بالأحداث المصادر، وسماها أحداثا لأنها تحدث في رأي العين من الفاعلين، وأضاف الأحداث إلى الأسماء وأراد به الذوات التي يحصل منها الأفعال الحقيقية التي هي عندهم المصادر وسماها الأسماء، لأن الاسم عبارة عن المسمى، وهذا مما رده عليه أبو العباس المبرد وقال: هذا خطأ لأن المصادر تحدث من المسميات لا من الأسماء، والصحيح أن يقال: أحداث أصحاب الأسماء كذا ذكر في كتابه المسمى بالانتقاد على سيبويه ذكر فيه أشياء استدركها عليه. وأهل الصناعة قالوا: الصحيح ما ذهب إليه سيبويه لأنه عنى بالأسماء المسميات دون التسميات كما هو مذهب أهل السنة أن الاسم والمسمى واحد، وذهب المبرد إلى أن الاسم غير المسمى وهو عبارة عن التسمية. ثم إن صاحبكم الأشعري لم يقبل من سيبويه أن الاسم هو المسمى ولا من أبي عبيدة، إذ روي عنه هذا بل جميع من تقدمه وقال: إن الاسم هو الصفة، وينقسم الاسم انقسام الصفات فيكون اسم هو عين المسمى واسم هو غير المسمى، واسم لا يقال هو المسمى ولا يقال هو غيره. وكذا التسمية مع الاسم، وطول هو وأصحابه في تقاسيم ذلك. وإذا كان هو يخالف سيبويه في هذا كله، كيف ألزمتم خصومكم الأخذ بقوله والنزول على حكمه؟
Then he said: The definition of the verb (al-fi'l) is patterns taken from the phrase of the events (ahdāth) of the names (asmā'). And he intended by 'events' the verbal nouns (al-maṣādir), and he called them 'events' because they occur in the eye of the beholder from the agents. He added the 'events' to the 'names' and intended by them the essences from which occur the real actions that are, according to them, the verbal nouns, and he called them 'names' because the name (al-ism) is an expression for the named (al-musammā). And this is something that Abu al-ʿAbbas al-Mubarrad rejected and said: This is an error because the verbal nouns occur from the named things (al-musammayāt), not from the names. The correct thing to say is: the events of the possessors of the names. Thus he mentioned in his book called al-Intiqād ʿalā Sībawayh, in which he mentioned things he corrected. And the people of the craft said: The correct view is what Sībawayh held, because he intended by 'names' the named things, not the act of naming, just as it is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna that the name (al-ism) and the named (al-musammā) are one. And al-Mubarrad held that the name is other than the named, and it is an expression for the act of naming (al-tasmiya). Then your companion al-Ashʿarī did not accept from Sībawayh that the name is the named, nor from Abu ʿUbayda—for this was related from him—indeed from all who preceded him. He said: The name (al-ism) is the attribute (al-ṣifa), and the name is divided according to the division of attributes: there is a name that is identical to the named, a name that is other than the named, and a name of which it is not said it is the named nor said it is other than it. Similarly for the act of naming (al-tasmiya) with the name. He and his companions went on at length about these divisions. And since he opposes Sībawayh in all this, how can you bind your opponents to accept his statement and submit to his judgment?
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and a demonstration of inconsistency. Nasafi first clarifies Sībawayh's definition: the verb (fiʿl) is patterns derived from the verbal nouns (maṣādir) of events; Sībawayh called them 'events' because they occur from agents, and he added them to 'names' meaning the essences (dhawāt) that are the subjects of real actions. He called them 'names' because a name (ism) refers to the named (musammā). Al-Mubarrad objected, saying verbal nouns arise from the named things (musammayāt), not from names. The experts (ahl al-ṣināʿa) sided with Sībawayh: he meant the named things (musammayāt), not the act of naming (tasmiya), consistent with Ahl al-Sunna's position that ism and musammā are identical. Al-Mubarrad held they are distinct (ism = tasmiya). Then Nasafi turns to al-Ashʿarī's own view: he rejects the identity, instead treating ism as a quality (ṣifa) with three categories. Since al-Ashʿarī disagrees with Sībawayh on these fundamentals, he cannot demand his opponents accept Sībawayh's authority. This exposes the Ashʿarī's inconsistency.

Key Terms
سيبويه
فعل
أحداث
مصادر
أسماء
مسميات
اسم
مسمى
تسمية
أبو العباس المبرد
أشعري
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name and the named are distinct; the name is merely a linguistic label separate from the object.
Correction: In Sunni orthodoxy, the name and the named are identical—the name is not a separate entity but refers directly to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, what is the relationship between the name (ism) and the named (musammā)?
A) They are distinct.
B) They are one and the same.
C) The name is a quality.
D) The name is created.

Key Takeaway
In Sunni theology, the identity of name and named is a foundational principle that undergirds both linguistic analysis and divine attribute theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allāh', do you truly sense that the name you utter is identical to His majestic being, or do you treat it as a mere label? How does your heart's conviction match the Sunni principle of ism and musammā? What will you do today to deepen that connection?
?
Nasafi exposes al-Ashʿarī's inconsistency: he appeals to Sībawayh while rejecting his core teaching. Is there any authority—a scholar, a book, a tradition—that you appeal to selectively, accepting parts and ignoring the foundations? Will you examine your own consistency before Allah tonight?
?
The experts sided with Sībawayh because they understood his intended meaning, not just his words. When you read the Qur'an or hadith, do you strive for the intended meaning, or do you sometimes let preconceptions color your understanding? What step will you take this week to correct that?

Grammatical Divisions of Speech
Nasafi explains the grammatical division of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the grammatical classification of speech into definition (taʿrīf) and obligation (taklīf) as a foundation for theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Grammar reveals the structure of speech, which becomes the basis for understanding God's eternal act.

Nasafi begins by laying out the grammatical division of speech (kalām) as understood by early linguists. He shows that speech is broadly divided into two categories: informing (ikhbār) and obligating (taklīf). This classification is not merely linguistic; it sets the stage for a theological argument about the nature of God's act (fiʿl). Let us examine this classification and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Notice that Nasafi starts by equating fiʿl (verb) with kalām (speech) from the grammarians' perspective.
The passage contains a repeated phrase; it is authentic to the source.
2
Identify the two main divisions
The passage divides speech into definition (taʿrīf) and obligation (taklīf). Within definition, it is informing (ikhbār) which follows tenses. Within obligation, it is command (amr) and prohibition (nahy). List these in your mind.
Note that 'definition' here is synonymous with 'informing'.
3
Connect to the fourfold division
Nasafi then states that speech is commonly divided into khabar (news), istikhbār (inquiry), amr (command), and nahy (prohibition). See how this maps to the previous classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول له: الفعل عند النحويين هو الكلام، وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين: تعريف وتكليف، ويعني بالتعريف الإخبار وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين: تعريف وتكليف ويعني بالتعريف الإخبار، وهو ينقسم انقسام الأزمنة وهي الماضي والقائم والآتي مثل ضرب زيد أمس وهو ضرب للحال، وسيضرب غدا. والتكليف ينقسم إلى إيجاب وهو الأمر، وإلى منع وهو النهي. وهذا كله من أقسام الكلام، ولهذا يقال في أقسام الكلام: إنه خبر واستخبار وأمر ونهي. فإن كنت أخذت بقول أهل اللغة، ففعل الله إذا كلامه وكلامه أزلي بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم فكان فعل الله أزليا، ولو كان الفعل حادثا عندك لكان الكلام حادثا، فإن لم تقبل هذا من أهل اللغة بطل إلزامك ثم الفعل عندك وعند أهل الكلام أجمع ما هو المصدر عند أهل اللغة.
Then we say to him: The verb (al-fiʿl) according to the grammarians is speech (al-kalām). And it is divided into two categories: definition (taʿrīf) and obligation (taklīf). He means by definition informing (al-ikhbār). And it is divided according to the division of the tenses: past, present, and future, like 'Zayd struck yesterday' (the act in the past), 'he is striking now' (the act in the present), and 'he will strike tomorrow'. And obligation is divided into imposition (ījāb), which is the command (al-amr), and prohibition (manʿ), which is the forbidding (al-nahy). All of this is among the divisions of speech. Therefore it is said concerning the divisions of speech: it is news (khabar), inquiry (istikhbār), command (amr), and prohibition (nahy). So if you have taken the statement of the linguists, then God's verb (fiʿl Allāh) would be His speech (kalāmuhu), and His speech is eternal (azalī) without dispute between us and you; thus God's verb would be eternal. And if the verb were created (ḥādith) according to you, speech would be created. And if you do not accept this from the linguists, then your binding proof is invalid. Moreover, the verb according to you and according to the theologians (ahl al-kalām) altogether is that which is the verbal noun (al-maṣdar) according to the linguists.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is an argument from linguistic classification. Nasafi begins by stating that grammarians define the verb (fiʿl) as speech (kalām). He then divides speech into two main categories: definition (taʿrīf) and obligation (taklīf). 'Definition' means informing (ikhbār), which is further divided by tense—past, present, future. 'Obligation' includes command (amr) and prohibition (nahy). So all speech is classified as news (khabar), inquiry (istikhbār), command, or prohibition. If one accepts this grammatical classification, then God's fiʿl (verb/act) is His speech (kalām), and His speech is eternal (azalī) by consensus. Therefore, God's fiʿl must also be eternal. If the opponent claims God's fiʿl is created (ḥādith), then His speech would be created—an unacceptable conclusion. If the opponent rejects this entire grammatical scheme, then he cannot validly use grammar as proof. Finally, Nasafi notes that in theological (kalām) usage, 'fiʿl' is the same as 'maṣdar' (verbal noun) in linguistic usage, indicating a confusion of terms.

Key Terms
فعل
كلام
تعريف
تكليف
إخبار
أمر
نهي
خبر
استخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, how do grammarians define the verb (fiʿl)?
A) As an action
B) As speech (kalām)
C) As a tense
D) As a verbal noun

Key Takeaway
Grammarians equate the verb (fiʿl) with speech (kalām), which has clear classifications that later support theological arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, which category—definition (informing) or obligation (commanding/prohibiting)—dominates your words? How does that pattern reflect or contradict the way God speaks in revelation?
?
The passage divides speech into news, inquiry, command, and prohibition. Which of these do you use most when talking about your faith? Are there areas where you need to more consciously align your speech with the divine pattern?
?
God's speech is eternal, yet your speech is temporal. How does recognizing the structure of speech—its categories and purposes—change the way you value every word you utter, knowing that your speech is a reflection of the Creator's attribute of speech?

God's Act is Eternal
Nasafi argues from grammar to the eternity of God's act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues from the grammatical identity of fiʿl and kalām to the conclusion that God's act (fiʿl) is eternal, and how he counters a potential objection.

Core Idea
Since God's speech is eternal, and fiʿl is speech, then God's act must also be eternal.

Having established that grammarians equate the verb (fiʿl) with speech (kalām), Nasafi now applies this to the divine realm. He argues that if one accepts the linguistic definition, then God's fiʿl is His speech, which is eternal by consensus. He also refutes the opponent's claim that fiʿl is created (ḥādith) by showing the logical inconsistency. Finally, he clarifies a terminological confusion between theological and linguistic usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the passage that starts with 'فإن كنت أخذت بقول أهل اللغة'. Notice the conditional structure: if you accept the linguistic definition, then God's act is eternal.
2
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: God's fiʿl = His speech (by linguistic definition). His speech is eternal (by consensus). Therefore, God's fiʿl is eternal. If you deny this, the opponent's proof collapses.
Nasafi also addresses the consequence of calling fiʿl created.
3
Understand the terminological clarification
At the end, Nasafi notes that in theology (kalām), 'fiʿl' is understood as the verbal noun (maṣdar) in linguistics. This explains possible confusion between the two disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول له: الفعل عند النحويين هو الكلام، وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين: تعريف وتكليف، ويعني بالتعريف الإخبار وهو ينقسم إلى قسمين: تعريف وتكليف ويعني بالتعريف الإخبار، وهو ينقسم انقسام الأزمنة وهي الماضي والقائم والآتي مثل ضرب زيد أمس وهو ضرب للحال، وسيضرب غدا. والتكليف ينقسم إلى إيجاب وهو الأمر، وإلى منع وهو النهي. وهذا كله من أقسام الكلام، ولهذا يقال في أقسام الكلام: إنه خبر واستخبار وأمر ونهي. فإن كنت أخذت بقول أهل اللغة، ففعل الله إذا كلامه وكلامه أزلي بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم فكان فعل الله أزليا، ولو كان الفعل حادثا عندك لكان الكلام حادثا، فإن لم تقبل هذا من أهل اللغة بطل إلزامك ثم الفعل عندك وعند أهل الكلام أجمع ما هو المصدر عند أهل اللغة.
Then we say to him: The verb (al-fiʿl) according to the grammarians is speech (al-kalām). And it is divided into two categories: definition (taʿrīf) and obligation (taklīf). He means by definition informing (al-ikhbār). And it is divided according to the division of the tenses: past, present, and future, like 'Zayd struck yesterday' (the act in the past), 'he is striking now' (the act in the present), and 'he will strike tomorrow'. And obligation is divided into imposition (ījāb), which is the command (al-amr), and prohibition (manʿ), which is the forbidding (al-nahy). All of this is among the divisions of speech. Therefore it is said concerning the divisions of speech: it is news (khabar), inquiry (istikhbār), command (amr), and prohibition (nahy). So if you have taken the statement of the linguists, then God's verb (fiʿl Allāh) would be His speech (kalāmuhu), and His speech is eternal (azalī) without dispute between us and you; thus God's verb would be eternal. And if the verb were created (ḥādith) according to you, speech would be created. And if you do not accept this from the linguists, then your binding proof is invalid. Moreover, the verb according to you and according to the theologians (ahl al-kalām) altogether is that which is the verbal noun (al-maṣdar) according to the linguists.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is an argument from linguistic classification. Nasafi begins by stating that grammarians define the verb (fiʿl) as speech (kalām). He then divides speech into two main categories: definition (taʿrīf) and obligation (taklīf). 'Definition' means informing (ikhbār), which is further divided by tense—past, present, future. 'Obligation' includes command (amr) and prohibition (nahy). So all speech is classified as news (khabar), inquiry (istikhbār), command, or prohibition. If one accepts this grammatical classification, then God's fiʿl (verb/act) is His speech (kalām), and His speech is eternal (azalī) by consensus. Therefore, God's fiʿl must also be eternal. If the opponent claims God's fiʿl is created (ḥādith), then His speech would be created—an unacceptable conclusion. If the opponent rejects this entire grammatical scheme, then he cannot validly use grammar as proof. Finally, Nasafi notes that in theological (kalām) usage, 'fiʿl' is the same as 'maṣdar' (verbal noun) in linguistic usage, indicating a confusion of terms.

Key Terms

حادث
مصدر
أهل الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'fiʿl' in theology refers to a temporal action in time, just as in grammar.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that in theology, 'fiʿl' corresponds to the verbal noun (maṣdar), which denotes the essence of the action, not its temporal occurrence. Thus God's act is eternal, not created.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of Nasafi's argument regarding God's act (fiʿl)?
A) God's act is created
B) God's act is eternal
C) God's act is a command
D) God's act is the same as His essence

Key Takeaway
By equating fiʿl with kalām and asserting the eternity of God's speech, Nasafi deduces that God's act is also eternal, and clarifies the theological usage of the term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God's act is eternal, do you secretly still imagine God acting in time like a powerful human? What does that say about how deeply you have internalized God's transcendence? Before you move on, ask yourself: how does my daily worship reflect the belief that God's attributes are not bound by time?
?
If God's act is eternal and identical to His speech, then what does that imply about the nature of the Quran you recite? Does your heart tremble at the thought that you are uttering words that are essentially God's eternal act? How should that change your reverence during recitation?
?
Nasafi's argument hinges on accepting the linguists' definition of 'fi'l'. Are you quick to accept or reject authority when it suits your prior beliefs? Where in your life do you pick and choose sources to justify your own positions? Repent of intellectual dishonesty and commit to following truth wherever it leads.
