The Analogy of Hitting and the Principle of Shahid-Gha'ib
Objection and answer on the darb analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the analogy between visible and unseen by arguing that analogy and distinction are applied based on evidence, not uniformly.

Core Idea
Analogy between visible and unseen is not automatic; it depends on shared evidence.

This lesson presents an objection to the author's analogy between act and object. The opponent uses the example of hitting and the one hit, arguing that act and object cannot exist separately. The author responds by clarifying that analogy between the visible and unseen is governed by a principle: it is obligatory only when the evidence for analogy exists. He then illustrates this with examples, showing consistency in his method.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The opponent argues that if act and object are like hitting and the one hit, then creation cannot exist without a created. Read the first part of the passage carefully.
Notice how the opponent uses the visible world as evidence.
2
Identify the author's response
The author challenges the opponent with a counter-analogy and states his own position: he claims the necessity of difference (mughayara) between act and object. Find where he says this.
3
Understand the principle
The author explains that analogy between shahid and gha'ib is obligatory when obligatory, and distinction is obligatory when obligatory. He gives examples from knowledge and will. Read that section.
This is the key methodological point.
4
See the application
The author applies the principle to act and object, citing the evidence that act is effective (mu'aththir) and object is affected (muta'aththir). Notice how he uses this to justify analogy.
5
Notice the criticism
The author accuses the opponent of inconsistency: they make a distinction between shahid and gha'ib in this case. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال هذا القائل: إن كان الفعل والمفعول على قياس الضرب والمضروب فنقول: إنه لا يتصور وجود ضرب مع عدم المضروب، فوجب أن لا يتصور وجود خلق ولا مخلوق فنقول له في الجواب: بم تنفصل عمن يقول لك: إن لم يكن الخلق والمخلوق على قياس الضرب والمضروب فينبغي أن يجوز خلق مع عدم المخلوق؟ وإن كان لا يجوز الضرب مع عدم المضروب، والحاصل أنا ندعي وجوب المغايرة بين ما هو فعل الذات وبين ما هو مفعوله، ونستدل بالشاهد وأنتم تقولون باستحالة فعل بلا مفعول وتستدلون بالشاهد ويدعي كل واحد بطلان قول صاحبه، لادعائه في الغائب ما هو خلاف الشاهد، ثم لا شك أن التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب واجبة عند وجوب التسوية، فإن التفرقة بينهما واجبة عند وجوب التفرقة. فإنا جميعا سوينا بين العالم الشاهد والعالم الغائب في ثبوت العلم وكذا في سائر الصفات، وفرقنا بين المريد لما هو سفه وقبيح كشتم نفسه وغير ذلك في الشاهد والغائب لوجوب التفرقة ، ثم ها هنا نحن سوينا في الفعل والمفعول بين الشاهد والغائب، وقلنا بوجوب مغايرتهما في الشاهد والغائب جميعا لقيام دليل التسوية، وهو ما مر أن الفعل مؤثر والمفعول متأثر ، فيجب تغايرهما استدلالا بالقدرة والمقدور، وهذا المعنى في الغائب موجود ، ولهذا سوى بين الشاهد والغائب في حق وجوب المغايرة بين القدرة والمقدور، وأنتم تثبتون التفرقة ها هنا بين الشاهد والغائب فتقولون : إن الفعل وإن كان غير المفعول في الشاهد ففي الغائب
Then this speaker said: If act and object are analogous to hitting and the one hit, then we say: It is inconceivable for hitting to exist without the one hit, so it must be inconceivable for creation (khalq) and created (makhluq) to exist without each other. So we say to him in response: How are you different from one who says to you: If creation and created are not analogous to hitting and the one hit, then it should be permissible for creation to exist without the created? And if hitting is not permissible without the one hit, the conclusion is that we claim the necessity of difference (al-mughayara) between what is the act of the essence (fi'l al-dhat) and what is its object (maf'uluhu), and we use the visible (al-shahid) as evidence, while you claim the impossibility of an act without an object and also use the visible as evidence. Each one claims the invalidity of the other's statement, because he claims in the unseen (al-gha'ib) what is contrary to the visible. Then there is no doubt that making analogy between the visible and the unseen is obligatory when analogy is obligatory, and making a distinction between them is obligatory when distinction is obligatory. For we all made analogy between the visible world and the unseen world in the affirmation of knowledge, and likewise in other attributes, and we made a distinction between the one who wills something foolish and ugly, such as reviling his own self, and other things in the visible and the unseen, due to the necessity of distinction. Then here we made analogy in act and object between the visible and the unseen, and we said that their difference is necessary in both the visible and the unseen due to the evidence for analogy, which is what passed: that the act (al-fi'l) is an effect (mu'aththir) and the object (al-maf'ul) is affected (muta'aththir), so they must be different, deduced from power (al-qudra) and the empowered (al-maqdur). And this meaning exists in the unseen, and therefore we made analogy between the visible and the unseen in the context of the necessity of difference between power and the empowered. But you affirm distinction here between the visible and the unseen, so you say: The act, even if it is different from the object in the visible, yet in the unseen...
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The opponent argues that if act and object are like hitting and the one hit, then they must always coexist, so creation cannot exist without a created. The author responds by asking the opponent how he differs from someone who would then say that if the analogy is not valid, then creation could exist without a created? The author then states his own position: he claims the necessity of difference between the act of the essence and its object, and he uses the visible world as evidence. He notes that both parties use the visible as evidence, but each claims the other's statement is invalid because they apply the analogy inconsistently. The author then explains the principle: analogy between visible and unseen is obligatory when the reason for analogy exists, and distinction is obligatory when the reason for distinction exists. He gives examples: we make analogy in affirming knowledge and other attributes, but we make distinction in cases of foolish will. Here, he argues, we make analogy in the case of act and object because the evidence shows that act is an effect and object is affected, necessitating difference. This reasoning applies to both visible and unseen. He criticizes the opponent for making a distinction between visible and unseen in this matter, claiming that while act and object are different in the visible, in the unseen they might not be. The passage ends mid-sentence.

Key Terms
شاهد (shahid)
غائب (gha'ib)
مغايرة (mughayara)
تسوية (taswiya)
تفرقة (tafriqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that analogy between visible and unseen is always applied uniformly in all cases.
Correction: The author shows that analogy is applied only when the evidence supports it; otherwise, distinction is made. He applies this variably as required by the evidence.

Checkpoint
What principle does the author use to argue against the opponent's objection?
A) Analogy between visible and unseen is always necessary.
B) Analogy is applied when evidence supports it, and distinction when evidence supports it.
C) The opponent is inconsistent.
D) The visible world is the only basis for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that analogy between visible and unseen must be based on shared evidence, not applied uniformly without reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own reasoning about Allah's attributes, do you apply analogies from the visible world unconsciously, without checking whether the underlying meaning is truly shared? What would it take for you to examine that today?
?
When you find yourself insisting on a similarity between Allah and creation (or a distinction), are you doing so because of genuine evidence, or because it feels comfortable or familiar? Where might you be guilty of forcing an analogy or a distinction that has no proof?
?
If the act of creation is truly distinct from its object, what does that mean for how you understand your own being as a creature? Do you live as someone whose very existence is a sign pointing to a distinct Creator, or do you blur the line between Him and what He has made?

The Linguistic Meaning of Hikma and Hakim
Nasafi introduces the linguistic definitions of hikma and hakim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main linguistic definitions of 'hikma' (wisdom) and 'hakim' (wise) as presented by early Arabic lexicographers.

Core Idea
Wisdom in Arabic is rooted either in knowledge or in perfecting things.

Imam Nasafi introduces the discussion of God's attribute of wisdom by first clarifying its linguistic meaning. The chapter is divided into two parts: the linguistic sense and the theological sense. In this passage, he begins the linguistic analysis by presenting two competing definitions from renowned Arabic philologists. Understanding these roots is essential before moving into the technical theological discussion of God's wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then follow the English translation. Notice that the passage introduces two parts of the chapter but only begins the first part.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off mid-sentence after 'And he said...'.
2
Identify the two linguistic definitions
The passage presents two views: Ibn al-Arabi defines wisdom as knowledge and the wise as knowledgeable. Others define the wise as 'the one who perfects' (muhkim), deriving it from ihkam.
Note the morphological point: fa'il can mean muf'il.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how each definition relates to God. If wisdom is knowledge, then God's wisdom relates to His omniscience. If wisdom is perfecting, then God's wisdom relates to His flawless creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في بيان أن صانع العالم حكيم يقع الكلام في هذا الباب في موضعين: أحدهما في بيان معنى الحكمة والحكيم في اللغة. والثاني في بيان معنى الحكمة والحكيم والسفه والسفيه على رأي المتكلمين. معنى الحكمة والحكيم في اللغة أما الكلام في الفصل الأول فنقول : اختلف في ذلك أهل اللغة قال ابن الأعرابي: الحكمة العلم والحكيم العالم يقال : حكم الرجل بحكم إذ تناهى في علمه وعقله، القاضي حكما، وحاكما لعلمه وعقله . وقال آخرون: الحكيم هو المحكم، وهو فعيل بمعنى مفعل كأليم بمعنى مؤلم، وسميع بمعنى مسمع. وقال
The discourse on explaining that the Maker of the world is wise. The discussion in this chapter falls into two places: the first is in explaining the meaning of wisdom (hikma) and the wise (hakim) in language. The second is in explaining the meaning of wisdom and the wise and foolishness and the fool according to the opinion of the theologians. The meaning of wisdom and the wise in language. As for the discourse on the first chapter, we say: The people of language differed on that. Ibn al-A'rabi said: Wisdom is knowledge, and the wise is the knowledgeable. It is said: the man is judicious (hakama) in his judging (hukm) when he is perfect in his knowledge and intellect; and the judge is named a judge (hakim) because of his knowledge and intellect. Others said: The wise is the one who perfects (muhkim) things, and it is a pattern of fa'il meaning muf'il, like 'alim (painful) meaning mu'lim (causing pain), and sami' (hearing) meaning musmi' (causing to hear). And he said...
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter in the text, discussing the attribute that God is wise (hakim). The author first divides the chapter into two parts: the linguistic meaning of hikma and hakim, and the technical theological meaning. He then begins the first part by citing the opinions of linguists. Ibn al-A'rabi defines hikma as knowledge (al-'ilm) and hakim as the knowledgeable one (al-'alim). He gives the example of a judge who is called hakim because of his knowledge and intellectual perfection. Another group of linguists says that hakim is derived from ihkam (perfection, making firm), so hakim means muhkim (one who perfects). They note that the Arabic pattern fa'il can have the meaning of muf'il, as in 'alim meaning mu'lim (causing pain) and sami' meaning musmi' (causing to hear). The passage cuts off with 'And he said...' indicating more opinions follow. This linguistic grounding prepares for the theological discussion of God's wisdom.

Core Attributes of God and Method for Theological Disputes
The author summarizes the established attributes of God and introduces the method for discussing disputed issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the established attributes of God according to the author and his method for resolving disputed theological issues while preserving these core doctrines.

Core Idea
The core divine attributes are settled; disputes only concern meanings compatible with these.

The author now takes stock of what has been proven about God from the beginning of the book. He summarizes the essential attributes that are beyond dispute and then explains how disagreements arise only over additional meanings that do not contradict these fundamentals. This passage sets the stage for the discussions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow.
2
Identify the list of established attributes
Notice the attributes mentioned: existent, self-subsisting, eternal, powerful, hearing, seeing, speaking, creating, willing, wise, one. Also note what God is not: body, accident, substance, or similar to created things.
3
Understand the consensus and its limits
The author states that there is agreement that denying these attributes or affirming impossible ones is false. Then note how disagreements arise on subsidiary issues.
Disagreement is allowed only on meanings that do not violate core attributes.
4
Recognize the author's method
The author will rely on both scriptural and rational proofs for each disputed issue. Consider why this method is important for maintaining orthodoxy.

So we will discuss each issue according to what the scriptural and rational proofs necessitate, to the extent of sufficiency and possibility—if Allah wills.

Commentary
This passage is a transitional summary and methodological statement. He then asserts that the evidence for these is conclusive for a fair-minded person. The author then notes that there is consensus that any statement contradicting these established attributes or affirming what is impossible for God is false. Despite this agreement, disagreements arose on subsidiary issues because some groups affirm a meaning that does not, in their view, nullify any attribute or affirm an impossibility, while others reject that meaning because they think it leads to such nullification or impossibility. The author concludes by stating his method: to discuss each issue according to what rational and scriptural evidence requires, to the extent possible.

Grammatical Argument: The Invalidity of Unsupported Implied Objects in 'Nazar'
The author argues that 'nazar' must be directly to Allah; an implied object without evidence is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why grammatical ellipsis (idmar) in the phrase 'nazar ila Allah' is not permissible without clear textual evidence, and how this reinforces the direct vision of Allah.

Core Idea
An implied object in 'nazar' is meaningless without evidence.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi employs a grammatical argument to refute those who claim that 'nazar' in the Quranic verse about seeing Allah could have an implied object such as 'reward' or 'mercy'. He establishes a fundamental rule: omitting a word and substituting its attribute is only valid when the intended meaning is clearly indicated by evidence. Without such evidence, the speech becomes pointless. This lesson unpacks that rule and its application to the theological issue of ru'yat Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's main claim and the reasoning he presents.
Notice the strong opening statement that 'nazar' is directly attributed to Allah.
2
Understand the rule of idmar
The author states that omitting an implied noun (idmar al-mudaf) is only permissible when the intended noun is clearly specified by evidence. This is the key grammatical principle.
The Arabic term 'idmar' means ellipsis or omission.
3
Examine the Quranic example
The verse 'ask the village' is used to illustrate how evidence (only people can answer) allows us to understand 'people of the village'.
This shows how evidence directs the interpretation.
4
Consider the hypothetical example
The author gives the example of saying 'I saw Zayd' meaning his turban or shirt, which is invalid because all are equally possible without specifying evidence.
This demonstrates absurdity without evidence.
5
Apply the rule to 'nazar'
Since many things can be attributed to Allah, without evidence we cannot assume an implied object for 'nazar', so it must be direct.
This clinches the argument that vision is of Allah Himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن النظر مضاف إلى الله تعالى لا إلى غيره فلا يجوز صرفه غيره، ولو جاز ذا لجاز صرف قوله: اعْبُدُوا رَبَّكُمُ إلى غير الله تعالى. وحقيقة هذا أن إضمار المضاف وإقامة المضاف إليه مقام المضاف إنما يجوز عند تعين المضاف في نفسه بدليل من الدلائل فيحصل فائدة الكلام الإفهام كما في قوله تعالى: وَسْئَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ الَّتِي كُنَّا فِيهَا أي أهل القرية لأن السؤال للجواب ولا جواب ينتظر من الأبنية والحيطان وشيء من الجمادات، فعلم أن المراد منه من ينتظر منه الجواب، وقد اختص بذلك الأهل. فأما عند انعدام دليل تعين بعض ما يضاف فلا يجوز الإضمار لانعدام الفائدة، ألا ترى أن قائلا لو قال: رأيت زيدا وأراد به عمامته أو قميصه أو دابته أو غلامه أو داره، كان مخطئا في ذلك لاستواء هذه الأشياء وما سواها أيضا في صحة الإضافة إلى زيد وانعدام دليل التعين، فكان المتكلم به المريد بعض هذه الأشياء من غير دليل التعيين متكلما بما لا فائدة منه فيعد سفيها جاهلا. ثم ما يصح إضافته إلى الله تعالى أشياء كثيرة من نحو
That which confirms it is that 'nazar' is attributed to Allah, not to other than Him, so it is not permissible to divert it to other than Him. If this were permissible, it would be permissible to divert His saying: 'Worship your Lord' to other than Allah. The reality of this is that omitting the implied noun and putting the annexed in its place is only permissible when the implied noun is specified in itself by some evidence, so that the benefit of speech, which is understanding, is achieved, as in His saying: 'And ask the village which we were in' meaning the people of the village, because asking is for a response, and no response is expected from buildings and walls and any inanimate things. So it is known that what is intended is one from whom a response is expected, and that is specific to the people. But when there is no evidence specifying some of what can be annexed, then omission is not permissible due to the lack of benefit. Do you not see that if someone said: 'I saw Zayd' and intended by it his turban or his shirt or his mount or his servant or his house, he would be mistaken in that due to the equality of these things and others as well in the validity of attribution to Zayd and the absence of evidence of specification. Then the speaker intending some of these things without evidence of specification would be speaking without benefit and would be considered foolish and ignorant. Then what can be attributed to Allah are many things, such as
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical argument to reinforce that 'nazar' in the verse directly refers to sight of Allah, not to any other object. The author states that since 'nazar' is specifically attributed to Allah (mudaf ila Allah), it cannot be diverted to something else without evidence. If such diversion were allowed, it would lead to absurdity, like interpreting 'worship your Lord' as worshipping someone else. The key principle is that omitting an implied noun (idmar al-mudaf) and substituting the annexed phrase (al-mudaf ilayhi) in its place is only valid when the intended meaning is clear from evidence. The Qur'anic example 'ask the village' logically means ask the people of the village because only people can answer. Without such specifying evidence, the omission would be meaningless. The author illustrates with a mundane example: if someone says 'I saw Zayd' but means his turban or shirt, it would be erroneous because all these are equally attributable to Zayd. Similarly, many things can be attributed to Allah, so without evidence, assuming an implied object for 'nazar' would be a meaningless statement. Thus, the direct attribution to Allah establishes that the sight is of Allah Himself.

Key Terms
إضمار (idmar)
مضاف (mudaf)
مضاف إليه (mudaf ilayhi)
تعين (ta'ayyun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Grammatical ellipsis (idmar) can be freely applied without clear contextual evidence in interpreting 'nazar'.
Correction: Idmar requires clear specifying evidence; otherwise, the speech is meaningless.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The grammatical rule that ellipsis requires evidence prevents arbitrary interpretations of 'nazar', affirming that it directly means seeing Allah.

The passage shows that without evidence, speech is worthless. How much of your own talk about religious matters is based on solid evidence versus mere assumption? Are you willing to remain silent on what you do not know?

Methodological Priority of Transmitted Proofs for Ru'ya
Author explains the methodological priority of sami proofs over rational proofs for ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author prioritizes transmitted (sam'i) proofs over rational proofs when establishing the permissibility and actuality of seeing Allah (ru'ya).

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs combine permissibility and actuality, making them sufficient and prior.

In his discussion of ru'ya (seeing Allah), Imam al-Nasafi explains his methodological choice to present transmitted proofs before rational ones. While logical order might suggest starting with rational proofs for permissibility, he argues that transmitted proofs are more comprehensive because they establish both permissibility and actuality together. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical order vs. actual order
Notice that the author acknowledges the logical order would be rational proofs first for jawaz, then transmitted for thubut, but he reverses it.
This shows pedagogical priority, not logical necessity.
2
Understand why transmitted proofs are sufficient
The author states that transmitted proofs contain both thubut and jawaz together. Someone convinced by them need not go further.
This is the key reason for priority.
3
See the broader pedagogical strategy
The author later turns to rational proofs for those who require them, showing inclusivity.
So both types are provided, but the more comprehensive is presented first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نشتغل بعد هذا ببيان جواز رؤية الله تعالى في العقول وإن كان الترتيب يقتضي إثبات الجواز أولاً بالدلائل العقلية ثم الثبوت بالدلائل السمعية إلا أنا قدمنا الدلائل السمعية لما مر أن فيها الثبوت والجواز جميعا معا، فمن اكتفى بها كان ذلك كافيا له فلا يحتاج إلى الخوض في الدلائل العقلية. ثم بعد فراغنا من إثبات ما رمنا إثباته بالدلائل السمعية نشتغل بالدلائل العقلية فنقول:
Then after this we occupy ourselves with explaining the permissibility of seeing God (ru'ya) in the intellects, even though the order requires first establishing permissibility (jawaz) by rational proofs then actuality (thubut) by transmitted proofs, except that we gave precedence to transmitted proofs because they contain, as passed, actuality and permissibility together. So whoever suffices with them, that suffices him, and he does not need to delve into rational proofs. Then after we finish establishing what we intended to establish by transmitted proofs, we occupy ourselves with rational proofs, and we say:
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note explaining the author's order of presentation. He begins by stating that he will now discuss the rational proof for the permissibility of seeing God, even though logical order would require first proving permissibility rationally and then actuality through transmitted proofs. However, he gave precedence to transmitted proofs because they simultaneously establish both actuality and permissibility. For someone who is convinced by transmitted proofs, that is sufficient and they need not engage in rational proofs. After completing the transmitted proofs, he now turns to rational proofs. This shows the author's pedagogical consideration: he provides the stronger (combined) proof first for those who accept it, while still offering rational proofs for those who require them.

The Correct Method: Consensus and Self-Subsistence Argument
Al-Nasafi recommends a method of argument relying on consensus on the invisibility of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct method for arguing that God can be seen: first secure consensus on the invisibility of accidents, then argue from self-subsistence.

Core Idea
To prove God's visibility, first agree that accidents are invisible, then argue from self-subsistence.

This passage from Tabṣirat al-Adilla offers a methodological guide for kalam arguments. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, advises that instead of jumping straight to abstract principles, one should first establish common ground with opponents on a basic point: in this world, accidents are never seen. Only then can one apply the analogy to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The author starts by noting that reliance on self-subsistence is not possible without first securing consensus on the invisibility of accidents.
This sets the stage for a method that avoids direct abstract reasoning.
2
Identify the agreed point
Both parties agree that accidents are not seen in this world, and that bodies are seen. Also, every body is self-subsistent.
These are the building blocks for the analogy.
3
Trace the analogy
The argument moves from the witnessed world (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib): bodies are seen because they are self-subsistent, not because they are bodies. Therefore, God, being self-subsistent, can be seen.
The key is to identify the cause (self-subsistence) and apply it to the unseen.
4
Note the exception
If the opponent holds that some accidents are perceptible (like sound), then the consensus argument fails. In such a case, rely on the proof of impossibility of seeing accidents as a category.
This protects the argument from diversity of opinions regarding accidents.
5
Final conclusion
Bodies are seen because they are self-subsistent, not because of composition or corporeality. This avoids attributing materiality to God.
This clarifies the true basis for visibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا التعويل على القائم بالذات غير ممكن إلا على طريق التعليق بالإجماع مع الخصوم في استحالة رؤية الأعراض فيقال لهم : اتفقنا أن ما هو مستحيل الرؤية في الشاهد هو العرض، وأن كل جسم جائز الرؤية، وكل كما هو جسم فهو قائم بالذات جسم فبنا أن نتأمل أنه لأنه جسم فلا يعدى إلى الغائب لأنه ليس أو لأنه يرى بجسم قائم بالذات فيعدى إلى الغائب لأنه قائم بالذات فينبغي أن نتكلم على هذا الوجه ولا نشتغل بدليل استحالة رؤية الأعراض إلا إذا كان المثبت للرؤية يرى في الأعراض رأي عبد الله بن سعيد فحينئذ يمكنه أن يتعلق بذلك الدليل، فأما من لم يكن يتابعه في ذلك ويجوز استدراك شيء من الأعراض بشيء من الحواس، فلا يمكنه التعويل على الدليل فينبغي أن يعتمد على إجماع الخصوم على ذلك، ثم نتكلم أن الأجسام ترى لقيامها بالذات دون التركب والجسمية
When reliance on the self-subsistent is not possible except by way of appeal to consensus with the opponents on the impossibility of seeing accidents, it is said to them: We have agreed that what is impossible to see in the witnessed world (al-shahid) is the accident, and that every body can be seen, and everything that is a body is self-subsistent. So we should consider: is it because it is a body that it is transferred to the unseen? Or because it is self-subsistent? So we should speak in this manner and not occupy ourselves with the proof of the impossibility of seeing accidents except if the one who affirms seeing holds the view of 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id regarding accidents; then he can rely on that proof. But as for one who does not follow him in that and allows the perception of some accidents by some senses, he cannot rely on that proof; rather he should rely on the consensus of the opponents on that. Then we say that bodies are seen because they are self-subsistent, not because of composition or corporeality.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
The author provides a methodological recommendation for arguing for the visibility of God. He suggests that instead of relying on the abstract principle of self-subsistence directly, one should first secure agreement (ijma') from the opponents that accidents are not visible in this world (al-shahid). Then one argues by analogy that bodies are seen in this world because they are self-subsistent, not because they are bodies per se. This transfers to the unseen (al-gha'ib): God is self-subsistent, so He can be seen. The author also notes that this approach avoids the complication of disagreements about perceptibility of accidents; if one's opponent holds that some accidents are perceptible (like sound), then one cannot use the disagreement argument. Instead, one should rely on the consensus that accidents (as a category) are not visible, and then proceed with the self-subsistence argument. Finally, the author emphasizes that bodies are seen because of self-subsistence, not because of composition or corporeality — a key point to avoid attributing materiality to God.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma', consensus)
شاهد (shahid, witnessed world)
غائب (gha'ib, unseen)
قيام بالذات (qiyam bi al-dhat)
تركيب (tarkib, composition)
جسمية (jismiyya, corporeality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think we can argue directly from self-subsistence without consensus.
Correction: The passage recommends a method that first secures agreement on the invisibility of accidents before using the self-subsistence argument.

Checkpoint
What is the correct method to argue for God's visibility according to the author?
A) Directly from self-subsistence
B) By first securing consensus on the invisibility of accidents, then arguing from self-subsistence
C) By claiming God is a body
D) By appealing to sensory experience

Key Takeaway
The correct method to argue for God's visibility is to first secure consensus on the invisibility of accidents in the witness world, then argue that bodies are seen because of self-subsistence, and apply that to God as self-subsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to affirm a truth about God, do you first ensure your starting point is agreed upon with others, or do you assume your reasoning is universally acceptable? What does this passage teach you about the humility required in theological argument?
?
If you were to defend the visibility of God to someone who rejects it, would you follow the method of consensus first, or would you rush to direct proofs? What would your instinct reveal about your reliance on shared premises?
?
The passage warns against relying on a proof that only works if the opponent already accepts certain premises. Are there beliefs you hold that depend on premises not shared by those you are trying to convince? How might you adjust your approach?

Prioritizing Scripture and Demanding Proof
Al-Maturidi prescribes holding to scripture and challenging the opponent's rational claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's initial step in arguing for ru'ya: holding fast to scriptural evidence and demanding that opponents prove their claim that rational evidence prevents vision.

Core Idea
Don't abandon scripture when challenged—make the opponent prove their case first.

Imam al-Maturidi outlines a clear methodology for defending the vision of God. Instead of abandoning scriptural evidence when opponents claim rational contradiction, he instructs to demand proof from the opponent. This lesson presents that initial move and the questioning technique that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author sets up the opponent's claim and then the response.
This passage is the first part of al-Maturidi's argument, focusing on scriptural evidence.
2
Identify the opponent's challenge
The opponent claims that scriptural evidence for ru'ya is countered by rational evidence. Find that claim and note how al-Maturidi responds.
The response is to ask 'Why did you say that?'
3
Understand the questioning technique
Al-Maturidi questions the opponent's assumption that visibility is limited to bodies. He asks for proof that a body is seen because it is a body.
This is a key move: shifting the burden of proof.
4
See the refutation strategy
The text introduces mu'arada: arguing that vision attaches to bodies not because they are bodies but because they are self-subsistent. This refutes the opponent without adopting a positive theory.
This sets the stage for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي يرى الأصوب في هذه المسألة أن يتمسك بالدلائل السمعية التي مر ذكرها. فإذا عجزت الخصوم عن الاعتراض عليها وزعمت أن الدلائل السمعية إن أوجبت الرؤية فالدلائل العقلية منعت القول بالرؤية فكانت الآيات والأحاديث متشابهة يجب التوقف فيها. فيقال لهم : لم قلتم إن الدلائل العقلية منعت القول بالرؤية؟ فإذا قالوا: لأن المرئي هو الجسم بدليل أن في المشاهدة لا يرى إلا الجسم والله تعالى ليس بجسم يطالبون بدليل. وقيل لهم : لم قلتم إن الجسم يرى لأنه جسم؟ ويعارضون أنها تعلقت بالأجسام لا لكونها أجساما بل لكونها قائمة بالذات فيعلل بها على طريق المعارضة دفعا لكلام الخصوم لا لابتداء التعليل ليقع الدليل على الخصم فيهدم حينئذ ما يقيم من الدلالة على تعلق الرؤية بالجسم على ما بينا، ولا يحتاج إلى إقامة الدليل على تعلقها بالقائم بالذات، فكان هذا أضيق على الخصم وأحسم لمادة شغبه فإنه – رحمه الله – ذكر في المقالات في مسألة المائية فقال : ثم الأصل أن ما لا يرى في الشاهد عرض يقول بغيره وإلا فكل قائم بنفسه يرى فإذا كان الله تعالى قائما بذاته يرى لا على الاعتلال بذلك ولكن على الرفع والمقابلة إذ لم يرجع في الأمر إلى ما يوجبه الدليل لكن رجع إلى الوجود في الشاهد هذا لفظه - رحمه الله - ومعناه ما بينا. وكذا لو سلكت هذه الطريقة مع المتأخرين منهم القائلين بجواز رؤية بعض الأعراض أيضا مع جواز رؤية الأجسام ، وقد أثبتنا بالدليل السمعي أنه تعالى مرئي فلم توقفت في القول بها ؟ فإن زعم لمخالفة الدليل السمعي الدليل العقلي قيل : ولم قلت إنها تخالف الدليل العقلي؟ فإذا قال: إن المرئي في الشاهد ما هو من هذه الأجناس المخصوصة وهي الأجسام والألوان والله تعالى خارج من هذه الأجناس. يقال لهم: لم قلتم : إن تعلقها بهذه الأجناس تعلق الأحكام بالعلل دون كون هذه الأجناس من أوصاف الوجود؟ ويطالبون بالدليل فما أقاموا من الدليل يهدم عليهم على ما نبين بعد هذا، فيسهل حينئذ الكلام وتقل مؤنة المجادلة وتخف.
And Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi held the most correct in this issue is to adhere to the scriptural evidences that have been mentioned. If the opponents are unable to object to them and claim that the scriptural evidences, if they necessitate the vision, then the rational evidences prevent asserting vision, and thus the verses and hadiths become ambiguous and must be suspended. So it is said to them: Why did you say that the rational evidences prevent asserting vision? If they say: Because the visible is the body, by evidence that in sensory observation nothing is seen but the body, and God Most High is not a body, they are demanded to provide proof. And it is said to them: Why did you say that the body is seen because it is a body? And they object that [vision] is attached to bodies not because they are bodies but because they are self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat); thus one reasons with it by way of objection to repel the opponents' speech, not as an initial causal attribution, so that the evidence falls on the opponent and then demolishes what he establishes of proof for the attachment of vision to the body as we have explained, and there is no need to establish proof for its attachment to what is self-subsistent. So this is more constricting for the opponent and more decisive in cutting off his mischief. For he – may God have mercy on him – mentioned in al-Maqalat concerning the issue of the essence (al-ma'iyya) and said: Then the principle is that what is not seen in the witnessed world is an accident that is predicated of something else; otherwise everything self-subsistent is seen. So since God Most High is self-subsistent, He is seen, not by way of causal attribution (i'tilal) but by way of refutation and comparison (al-raf' wa al-muqabala), since the matter does not revert to what the evidence necessitates but reverts to existence in the witnessed world. This is his wording – may God have mercy on him – and its meaning is what we have explained. And likewise if you follow this method with the later ones among them who affirm the permissibility of seeing some accidents along with the permissibility of seeing bodies, and we have established by scriptural evidence that He Most High is visible, so why did you hesitate in affirming it? If he claims opposition of the rational evidence to the scriptural evidence, it is said: And why did you say that it opposes rational evidence? If he says: The visible in the witnessed world is only from these specific categories, namely bodies and colors, and God Most High is outside these categories. They are told: Why did you say that its attachment to these categories is the attachment of rulings to causes (al-ahkam bi al-'ilal) without these categories being attributes of existence? And they are demanded to provide proof; whatever proof they present is demolished against them as we will explain after this. Then speech becomes easy, the trouble of argumentation becomes light, and it becomes easy.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Maturidi's method for arguing about the vision of God (ru'ya). He advises holding firmly to scriptural evidence (al-adilla al-sam'iyya) and not abandoning it when opponents claim that rational evidence contradicts it. Instead, the correct approach is to demand from the opponent proof that rational evidence indeed prevents vision. When the opponent says that visibility applies only to bodies, they are asked to prove that a body is seen because it is a body. The response then turns the opponent's reasoning against them: vision attaches to bodies not because they are bodies but because they are self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat). This is stated not as an initial causal theory but as a refutation (mu'arada) to demolish the opponent's argument. Al-Maturidi quotes his own earlier work, al-Maqalat, affirming that in the witnessed world, only accidents are not seen by themselves; everything self-subsistent is seen. Thus God, being self-subsistent, is seen, not by way of causal attribution but by way of refutation and comparison. The same method is applied to later opponents who allow seeing some accidents: they are asked why they think visibility is tied to specific categories (bodies, colors) as causes. The opponent is demanded to prove this causal link; if he cannot, his argument fails. The overall purpose is to make debate easier and to lighten the burden of argumentation.

Key Terms
الدلائل السمعية
الدلائل العقلية
الرؤية
الجسم
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Maturidi's method requires presenting a positive rational proof for ru'ya immediately.
Correction: Al-Maturidi's method is to first demand proof from the opponent and use refutation (mu'arada), not to offer a new proof initially.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first response when opponents claim rational evidence contradicts scriptural evidence for ru'ya?
A) Present a rational proof that God is seen because He is self-subsistent.
B) Demand that the opponents prove that rational evidence actually prevents vision.
C) Declare the scriptural evidence ambiguous and stop discussion.
D) Concede that both types of evidence are equal and seek a middle ground.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's first move is to hold to scripture and challenge the opponent to prove their rational objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone presents a rational argument that seems to contradict a clear text of the Qur'an or Sunnah, do you immediately doubt the text, or do you demand that the person prove their rational claim from its own premises? In your own life, where have you been quick to abandon scripture because of a seemingly strong intellectual objection? Write down one specific instance and commit to revisiting that issue with the methodology of al-Maturidi: hold to the text and ask for proof.
?
Al-Maturidi uses a counter-argument (mu'arada) not as his own belief but to demolish the opponent's evidence. Are you willing to set aside your need to have a perfect, positive theory first, and instead focus on exposing the flaws in false arguments? When defending your faith, do you feel pressured to have all the answers, or can you use the method of challenging the opponent's assumptions? Resolve that in your next conversation on a theological issue, you will first ask 'Why do you say that?' before providing your own explanation.
?
The method described is 'more constricting for the opponent and more decisive in cutting off his mischief.' In your own arguments about faith, do you aim to win by overwhelming the opponent with proofs, or do you aim to silence falsehood by strategically questioning their foundations? Which approach is closer to the prophetic way? Identify one area where you have been defensive rather than strategic, and decide to change your approach starting today.

The Principle of Self-Subsistence and Mu'arada
Al-Maturidi explicates the self-subsistence principle and its use as refutation, extending to later opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's use of the self-subsistence principle as a refutation (mu'arada) and how it applies to later opponents who allow seeing accidents.

Core Idea
Everything self-subsistent is seen—this refutes the opponent without being a positive theory.

Al-Maturidi deepens his method by quoting his own earlier work, al-Maqalat, to establish that in the witnessed world, only accidents are unseen, while everything self-subsistent is seen. He then applies this principle as a refutation (mu'arada) against opponents, and extends the same questioning technique to later groups who allow seeing some accidents. This lesson covers that second stage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from al-Maqalat
Read the quote from al-Maturidi's earlier work: 'what is not seen in the witnessed world is an accident... everything self-subsistent is seen.' Understand how this supports his refutation.
This is a key principle for the argument.
2
Understand the refutation role
Note that al-Maturidi clarifies this principle is used 'not by way of causal attribution but by way of refutation and comparison.' It is a mu'arada, not a positive proof.
This distinguishes his method from others.
3
Apply to later opponents
The same method is applied to those who allow seeing some accidents: they are asked why visibility is tied to specific categories (bodies, colors) as causes.
The burden of proof remains on the opponent.
4
See the ease of argumentation
The passage concludes that this approach makes argumentation easy and light. Recognize the practical benefit.

Method of Learning
intermediate

The Method of Debate: Demand Proof First
al-Nasafi advises against claiming causality without proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct method of theological debate: demanding proof from opponents before offering alternative causal claims, and the rationale for this procedural order.

Core Idea
Demand proof before offering counter-causes in debate.

This lesson presents a crucial methodological principle from Imam al-Nasafi. He warns against prematurely asserting an alternative cause of vision (self-subsistence) and instead instructs us to first require the opponent to prove their own claimed cause. This approach preserves rigorous argumentation and avoids unnecessary dispute. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the author's tone and the key terms.
2
Identify the two phases of debate
The passage distinguishes between two stages: first, demanding proof from the opponent; second, if unable to refute that proof, then offering a counter-cause. Note the order.
3
Understand 'musamaha' and 'musahala'
The author mentions that al-Maturidi's prior mention of self-subsistence was only by way of leniency and facilitation, not as a primary argument. This shows the proper method is stricter.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with a promise that the opponent's evidence can be refuted, so there is no need to offer a counter-cause prematurely. Consider how this advice protects the debater.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينبغي أن لا يدعي أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية القيام بالذات أيضا، وما ذكره الشيخ - رحمه الله - ذكر على طريق المسامحة والمساهلة، بل يطالب الخصوم بإقامة الدلالة على ما جعله علة فحسب ولا نشتغل بادعاء شيء علة فإنهم هم المدعون أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية ما زعموا، فلا معنى للاشتغال بمعارضة العلة بالعلة قبل العجز عن منع دليل الخصوم وهدمه وإنما يصار إلى ذلك عند العجز عن ذلك ولا عجز بحمد الله على ما نبين بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى - فمن أصغى إلى هذه الوصية وتمسك بهذه الطريقة فقد كفى مؤنة الخصم واندفعت عنه معرة مجادلتهم.
And it is not appropriate to claim that the absolute cause of vision is self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-dhat) either. What the Shaykh – may God have mercy on him – mentioned was mentioned by way of leniency and facilitation. Rather, the opponents should be demanded to establish evidence for what they have made a cause, and we should not occupy ourselves with claiming something as a cause. For they are the claimants that the absolute cause of vision is what they claim. So there is no meaning to occupying oneself with opposing cause with cause before being unable to prevent the opponent's evidence and demolish it. And recourse to that is only taken when unable to do that, and there is no inability, praise be to God, as we will explain after this, God willing. So whoever heeds this advice and adheres to this method has sufficed against the trouble of the opponent and the stigma of their debate is repelled from him.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage gives a practical procedural warning. The author advises not to claim outright that the absolute cause ('illa mutlaqa) of vision is being self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat). Even though al-Maturidi mentioned this in the previous passage, it was said by way of leniency (musamaha) and facilitation (musahala), not as a primary argument. The correct method is to demand from the opponents that they provide proof for the cause they themselves claim (e.g., bodies or colors). The term 'cause' here refers to the condition or attribute that makes something visible. The opponents assert that the cause of visibility is bodies or colors; the author says: do not start by saying the cause is self-subsistence; instead, force the opponent to prove their claimed cause first. Engaging in counter-causal claims before defeating the opponent's evidence is pointless. Only if one is unable to refute the opponent's evidence should one turn to offering an alternative cause. But the author asserts that there is no such inability, God willing. The passage concludes by saying that whoever follows this advice has sufficiently handled the opponent and avoided the shame of debate.

Key Terms
العلة المطلقة
القيام بالذات
المسامحة والمساهلة
المعارضة
منع الدليل
هدم الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Maturidi's mention of self-subsistence as a cause is a primary argument that should be used directly.
Correction: The author clarifies it was only a facilitative refutation; the primary method is to demand proof from opponents.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when should one resort to opposing the opponent's claimed cause with an alternative cause?
A) At the very start of the debate.
B) After demanding the opponent's proof and being unable to refute it.
C) Only when the opponent brings a clear scriptural proof.
D) Never, because such opposition is always invalid.

Key Takeaway
The correct method in theological debate is to first demand proof from the opponent before offering alternative explanations, and only resort to counter-causal claims when unable to refute their evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a religious disagreement, do you immediately rush to offer your own explanations, or do you first demand clear proof from the other person? How often have you skipped the first step and regretted it? Will you commit to practicing this method starting today?
?
Think of a recent argument where you felt the stigma of debate. Did you try to demolish the other person's evidence before offering your own cause? What would have changed if you followed al-Nasafi's advice?
?
This lesson teaches that offering a counter-cause is only a last resort. Are there areas in your life where you habitually defend your position with counter-arguments instead of first demanding proof? What would it cost you to change that habit?

Methodology for Debating the Mu'tazila on Vision of God
Advice on debating the Mu'tazila about the vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper method of debating the Mu'tazila on the vision of God, specifically the importance of first demanding evidence before proposing alternative causes.

Core Idea
Don't fight causes; demand evidence first.

In this passage, the author gives strategic advice for engaging with the Mu'tazila on the issue of seeing God. Rather than jumping into a competition of claims, he instructs the debater to first require the opponent to prove their asserted cause. This approach saves time and upholds the strength of the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the author's tone and the sequence of his advice.
The passage includes a reference to an earlier statement by a Shaykh, which the author clarifies was merely facilitatory.
2
Identify the core advice
Find the main instruction: do not claim a cause yourself; instead demand evidence from the opponents for their claimed cause.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author explains why not to engage in countering causes prematurely: because the burden of proof is on the opponent, and countering is only after refuting their evidence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends with a strong assertion that there is no inability to refute them, and whoever follows this method is spared trouble.
This gives confidence in the Sunni position.

وينبغي أن لا يدعي أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية القيام بالذات أيضا، وما ذكره الشيخ - رحمه الله - ذكر على طريق المسامحة والمساهلة، بل يطالب الخصوم بإقامة الدلالة على ما جعله علة فحسب ولا نشتغل بادعاء شيء علة فإنهم هم المدعون أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية ما زعموا، فلا معنى للاشتغال بمعارضة العلة بالعلة قبل العجز عن منع دليل الخصوم وهدمه وإنما يصار إلى ذلك عند العجز عن ذلك ولا عجز بحمد الله على ما نبين بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى - فمن أصغى إلى هذه الوصية وتمسك بهذه الطريقة فقد كفى مؤنة الخصم واندفعت عنه معرة مجادلتهم.
And it is not appropriate to claim that the absolute cause of vision is subsisting by essence also, and what the Shaykh - may God have mercy on him - mentioned, he mentioned by way of accommodation and facilitation. Rather, demand from the opponents the establishment of evidence for what they make the cause, and we do not occupy ourselves with claiming something as a cause, for they are the ones who claim that the absolute cause of vision is what they allege. Commentary
This passage gives methodological advice for debating the Mu'tazila on the issue of the visibility of God. The author warns against prematurely asserting that the cause of vision is 'subsisting by essence' (al-qiyām bi-dh-dhāt), saying that what the earlier Shaykh (likely Abu Mansur al-Maturidi) said on this was merely facilitatory. Instead, the proper approach is to demand that the opponents (the Mu'tazila) provide evidence for their claimed cause of vision. Do not engage in countering their claimed cause with another cause until you have first refuted their evidence and rendered it invalid. Only then, if unable to refute, could one propose an alternative cause. The author asserts that there is no inability to refute them, by God's grace. He concludes that whoever follows this advice will be spared the trouble and disgrace of debating them.

Surveying Attributes to Find the Cause of Vision
Nasafi introduces the method of surveying attributes to determine the cause of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method of sabr al-awsaf (surveying attributes) used by Nasafi to identify the cause that permits vision.

Core Idea
To find the cause of vision, you must survey all shared attributes and eliminate common ones.

Having established that substances and accidents are visible, Nasafi now asks: what is the underlying cause that makes them visible? He introduces a systematic method called sabr al-awsaf, which involves examining all attributes common to visible things to isolate the true cause. This lesson presents this methodological principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice that the passage is a single methodological statement.
2
Identify the key question
After stating that substances and accidents are visible, Nasafi asks: 'What is the cause that permits vision?' This is the central inquiry.
The question drives the need for a method.
3
Understand the method
He prescribes 'sabr al-awsaf' (surveying attributes). This means listing all attributes common to visible things and testing each one to find the true cause.
The term 'awsaf ittifaqiyya' refers to these common attributes.
4
Reflect on the necessity
Why not guess the cause? Because it is not obvious. The method ensures systematic identification.
This guards against assuming the cause without evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت رؤية الجواهر والأكوان والألوان فنقول: ما هي العلة المجوزة للرؤية؟ لابد من سبر الأوصاف ليتبين العلة المجوزة للرؤية من الأوصاف الاتفاقية.
And if the visibility of substances, modalities, and colors is established, we say: What is the cause that permits vision? It is necessary to survey the attributes (sabr al-awsāf) so that the cause that permits vision becomes clear from among the common attributes.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement. After having established that substances, modalities, and colors are all visible, the author poses the key question: what is the cause (illa) that makes vision possible? He then prescribes the method to answer it: 'sabr al-awsaf' (surveying the attributes). This is a standard kalam technique for identifying the cause by elimination. One lists all attributes common to the visible things (substances and accidents) and tests each candidate to see which one is the true cause. The 'common attributes' (al-awsaf al-ittifaqiyya) are those shared by all visible entities. The method will later be applied to candidates such as existence (wujud) and temporality (huduth).

Key Terms
سبر الأوصاف
العلة
الرؤية
الأوصاف الاتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the cause of vision is obvious without investigation.
Correction: The author insists on surveying attributes to methodically identify the cause.

Checkpoint

A) Prove God exists
B) Determine the cause by surveying attributes
C) Accept consensus
D) Define accidents

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's method of sabr al-awsaf is a systematic way to identify the cause of vision by eliminating common attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Critique of Mu'tazilite Reliance on Mere Existence
Nasafi critiques the Mu'tazilite reliance on mere existence without considering the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi critiques the Mu'tazilite reliance on mere existence as a flawed methodology that ignores the rational cause.

Core Idea
Mere existence is not enough—causes matter.

Nasafi continues his critique of the Mu'tazilite position on ru'ya by exposing their methodological error. They attempt to argue from empirical observation that vision only relates to specific kinds of objects, but they neglect to consider the underlying causes that make such relations possible. This lesson examines Nasafi's accusation that the Mu'tazilites, like the anthropomorphists, rely on superficial similarities rather than rational analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms like 'pure existence' and 'cause'.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazilites claim that in the witnessed world, vision pertains only to specific genera (bodies, colors) without considering the cause. Find this in the text.
3
Understand the critique
Nasafi argues that relying on mere existence without cause is a category mistake, similar to their earlier claim that every agent is a body.
This connects to the broader debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم اعتمدوا على الوجود المحض، وقالوا: وجدنا في الشاهد أن الرؤية تتعلق بهذه الأجناس المخصوصة من غير النظر إلى العلة والتميز بينهما وبين الأوصاف الإضافية التي هي أوصاف الوجود، وجعلوا مجرد الوجود في دعواهم أن كل فاعل جسم على ما مر في تلك المسألة، ولم ينظروا إلى العلة.
Then they relied on pure existence, and said: We find in the witnessed [world] that vision is related to these specific genera without looking at the cause and the distinction between them and the additional attributes which are attributes of existence, and they made mere existence in their claim that every agent is a body as has passed in that issue, and they did not look at the cause.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage is a methodological critique. The author accuses the Mu'tazilites of relying solely on 'pure existence' (al-wujud al-mahd) in their reasoning about ru'ya. They observe that in the witnessed world, vision pertains only to specific genera (bodies, colors, etc.) but they do not consider the underlying cause ('illa) or distinguish between essential attributes and mere existential attributes. By focusing only on the fact of existence, they have made a category mistake—similar to their earlier claim that every agent is a body because agents in the world are bodies. This approach, the author implies, is a method of the ignorant anthropomorphists (mujassima), who also rely on superficial similarities rather than rational analysis. The key point is that the Mu'tazilites have abandoned the search for true causes and instead cling to accidental existential properties.

Key Terms

أوصاف إضافية

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazilites ignore the cause and merely rely on the fact of existence, which is a superficial approach akin to the mujassima.

Checkpoint

A) They rely on revelation instead of reason.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi criticises the Mu'tazilites for basing their argument on mere existence without considering the rational cause, a methodological error that undermines their position on ru'ya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Mu'tazilites made a category mistake by treating the existence of something as proof of how it must be described. In your own life, have you ever assumed that because something is common or observable, it must be the only way? How can you train yourself to look beyond 'mere existence' to the hidden causes that shape truth? Write down one practical step you will take this week.

Framing the Debate: Essential Visibility vs. Vision in the Hereafter
Al-Nasafi advises a methodological shift to essential visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how shifting the debate from actual vision to essential visibility eliminates many common objections against seeing God in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Focus on essential visibility to sidestep objections about vision.

Al-Nasafi presents a crucial methodological insight: many of the intricate objections against the belief in seeing God in the Hereafter arise only when we discuss the actual event of vision. If instead we first establish that God's essence is inherently visible, those objections lose their relevance. This approach was recommended by senior scholars in Samarqand.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the contrast between the two approaches.
The passage is concise—pay attention to the conditional structure.
2
Identify the two framings
The passage presents two ways of discussing the vision of God: one focusing on the actual vision in the Hereafter, and the other on whether His essence is visible in itself. Identify each.
The phrase 'يرى الله تعالى في الآخرة' vs. 'في نفسه مرئي'.
3
Understand why objections fall away
Reflect on why framing the discussion around essential visibility avoids most objections. Consider that objections often assume conditions of actual vision (e.g., location, direction).
Essential visibility is a property of the essence, independent of external conditions.
4
Learn from the Samarqand scholars
Note that some senior scholars in Samarqand advised this methodological shift. Why would they recommend this strategy?
This shows the practical importance of proper framing in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أكثر هذه الأسئلة ترد على من يقول: يرى الله تعالى في الآخرة. فأما من فرض الكلام في أن ذاته هل هو مرئي في نفسه فيسقط عنه أكثر هذه الأسئلة. وبعض الكبراء المحققين من أئمتنا بسمرقند كان يوصي أصحابنا أن يفرضوا الكلام في إثبات كونه في ذاته مرئيا لتندفع أكثر هذه الأسئلة.
Then most of these questions arise against those who say: God is seen in the Hereafter. But as for one who frames the discussion about whether His essence is visible in itself, most of these questions fall away from him. And some of the great verifying scholars among our imams in Samarqand used to advise our companions to frame the discussion about proving that He is in His essence visible, so that most of these questions would be repelled.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage offers a methodological shift. Many of the objections concerning seeing God (like whole/part) only apply when one discusses the actual vision in the Hereafter. If instead the discussion is framed around whether God's essence is visible in itself (i.e., is He essentially visible?), then these objections lose their force because they are based on conditions of actual vision. The author reports that certain senior scholars in Samarqand recommended this approach to avoid complicated questions.

Key Takeaway

Arguing for Prophethood from Common Ground
Nasafi outlines his dialectical method and begins the proof from reason's inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's dialectical method of arguing for the necessity of prophets starting from premises accepted by the denier, particularly the premise that reason can discern wisdom from foolishness.

Core Idea
To prove prophecy, start from what the denier already accepts: reason knows good and bad, but cannot identify true harm.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi explains his approach to arguing with those who deny the necessity of prophetic mission. Rather than starting from revelation (which they reject), he begins from a premise they accept: that reason can distinguish wisdom from foolishness, good from bad. He then builds a case showing that reason alone fails to distinguish beneficial foods from deadly poisons, pointing to the need for divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's methodological statement and the shift to the argument.
The passage is continuous; pay attention to how the author sets up the debate.
2
Identify the common ground
Find where the author states what the deniers of prophecy agree upon. What does he say they accept about reason?
Look for the phrase 'فثبت من نفاة الرسالة المساعدة على القول بأن العقل مما يوقف به على الحكمة والسفه...'
3
Understand the creation argument
The author begins a proof from the fact that God created both beneficial and harmful things. Why does he say that the senses and innate reason cannot distinguish them?
This is the first part of a longer argument that will conclude that a messenger is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا معنى للاشتغال ببيان دلائل هذه المسألة وإني قد بينت ذلك على استقصاء في تصنيف لي في تلك المسألة، وإنما الحاجة هاهنا إلى بيان القول بوجوبها على منكريها، ولا شك أن من أنكر الرسالة وعدها في حيز الممتنعات فعل ذلك بالعقل دون السمع، إذ السمع بلا ثبوت الرسالة محال، فثبت من نفاة الرسالة المساعدة على القول بأن العقل مما يوقف به على الحكمة والسفه والواجب والممتنع والحسن والقبيح. فنتكلم معهم على هذه الطريقة بناء للأمر على مساعدتهم دون الاشتغال بإثبات معرفة ذلك بمجرد العقول بالدليل فنقول وبالله التوفيق إن من المعلوم الذي لا خفاء به على من اعترف بالصانع أن الصانع جل جلاله خلق الخلق ولم يجعل على ما عليه العادة بقاءهم إلا بالأغذية وحبب إليهم البقاء، وجعل في جنس ما خلق ما بالخلق إليه بطباعهم رغبة لما فيه من اللذة والشهوة، ثم إنه تعالى كما خلق ما لهم به البقاء من الأغذية، وما يحفظ عليهم الصحة التامة ويزيل عنهم العلل العارضة من الأدوية، خلق من جنس تلك الجواهر السموم القاتلة والجواهر المتلفة، وليس في قوة الحواس ولا في جبلة العقل الوقوف
There is no point in occupying oneself with explaining the proofs of this issue, and I have already explained that exhaustively in a composition of mine on that issue. The need here is only to explain the claim of its obligation to those who deny it. There is no doubt that whoever denies prophetic mission and considers it among the impossibilities does so by reason (al-'aql) and not by audition (al-sam'), because audition is impossible without the establishment of prophetic mission. So it is established from the deniers of prophetic mission an agreement on the statement that reason is something by which one comes to know wisdom (hikma) and foolishness (safah), the obligatory (wajib) and the impossible (mumtani'), the good (hasan) and the bad (qabih). So we speak with them on this basis, building the matter on their agreement, without occupying ourselves with proving knowledge of that by mere reasons through evidence. We say, and with God is success: It is known, which is not hidden from anyone who acknowledges the Maker (al-sani'), that the Maker, Majestic is His glory, created creation and did not make, as is customary, their survival except through foods, and He made them love survival, and He placed in the kind of what He created a desire in the creation by their natures for what contains pleasure and appetite. Then, as He created foods for their survival, and what preserves perfect health and removes accidental ailments from them of medicines, He created from the same kind of those substances deadly poisons and destructive substances. And it is not within the power of the senses nor in the innate disposition of the intellect to distinguish
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement and the beginning of a proof for the necessity of prophetic mission. The author first explains that he will not repeat detailed proofs he has already given elsewhere; instead, he will focus on arguing specifically with those who deny the obligation of prophetic mission, showing that even they must agree on certain rational premises. He observes that anyone who denies prophetic mission and considers it impossible does so based on reason, not revelation, because revelation itself depends on the establishment of prophethood. Therefore, the deniers implicitly accept that reason can discern wisdom, foolishness, obligation, impossibility, good and bad. The author will argue on their own terms, building on this common ground without needing to prove that reason can know these things from the start. He then begins the argument by stating a universally accepted fact for the one who acknowledges the Creator: the Creator created living beings and made their survival dependent on food, instilling in them a love for life and a natural desire for pleasure. Moreover, alongside beneficial foods and medicines, He created harmful poisons and destructive substances. The senses and the innate constitution of the intellect are incapable of distinguishing between these beneficial and harmful things on their own. (The argument is cut off here, but it clearly leads to the conclusion that guidance from the Creator through messengers is necessary.) The author's approach is dialectical: he uses premises that even the denier of prophethood accepts to build a case for the necessity of divine guidance.

Key Terms

الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that reason is completely incapable of distinguishing good from evil.
Correction: The argument only states that reason alone cannot distinguish between beneficial and harmful things in all matters, especially regarding ultimate ends; it does not deny reason's ability to know moral categories in general.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say he will argue on the basis of the deniers' own agreement?
A) Because they accept both reason and revelation.
B) Because they deny revelation but accept reason as a source of knowledge.
C) Because they agree that reason can know good and bad, wisdom and foolishness.
D) Because they have already proven the necessity of prophetic mission.

Key Takeaway
The author begins his proof for the necessity of prophets by showing that reason, though capable of moral judgment, cannot alone identify which created things are truly beneficial or harmful, thus requiring divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a major life choice, do you trust your own reasoning more than the guidance of the prophets? What would it take for you to submit your intellect to divine revelation?
?
The lesson shows that God created both beneficial and harmful things in the world, and reason cannot always tell them apart. Think of a time you thought something was good but it turned out harmful, or vice versa. How does that experience challenge your reliance on unaided reason?
?
If you were among those who deny prophethood, what would you be left with to guide you? Are you truly living as though the prophets are necessary, or do you sometimes act as if your own judgment is enough?
