The Obligation of Gratitude and Its Proportionality
Sanusi establishes the obligation of gratitude and the principle of proportionality based on the benefactor's rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that gratitude for God's blessings is obligatory and must be proportionate to the blessings, and that the measure of gratitude depends on the rank of the Benefactor.

Core Idea
Gratitude is not optional; it must match the greatness of the Giver.

This passage establishes the foundation of gratitude. Since God's blessings are gratuitous and not earned, ingratitude would be a form of injustice. But gratitude itself must be proportional to the blessing, which sets up a crucial problem: how can we ever adequately thank the Infinite? The author explains the principle that the value of a gift is tied to the status of the giver.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on gratitude
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's logical progression from the gratuitous nature of blessings to the obligation of gratitude and the requirement of proportionality.
2
Understand the principle of proportionality
The author argues that gratitude must be equivalent to the blessing in essence and measure. Consider the example of a gift from a commoner vs. a king.
This is a key premise for the later argument.
3
Reflect on the implication for God
Since God is infinitely great, proportional gratitude would be infinite. This sets up the need for revelation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الله تعالى خلق البشر في أحسن تقويم وسخر لهم جميع ما على وجه الأرض من غير أن سبق منهم ما كانت هذه النعم خارجة مخرج المكافأة أو قضاء حق مستحق فكانت نعماؤه مبتدأه، ولا وجه إلى إسقاط شكرها وإباحة كفرانها لما فيه من تضييع النعم وظلمها فيجب الشكر، ثم ينبغي أن يكون الشكر كفاً للنعمة موازيًا لها في ذاتها مساويًا لها في قدرها، ولو لم يكن كذا لم يكن معتدا به ولا مقابلاً لها أن يرضى المنعم بكرمه وجوده بأدون من حقه ثم إن النعم تتفاوت أقدارها وتتباين منازلها بتفاوت أقدار المنعمين وتباين منازلهم ودرجاتهم، فمن كان منهم أعظم قدراً أو أعلا درجة ومنزلة كانت النعم منه أجل قدرًا وأكبر منزلة، فإن واحدًا من طبقات الرعايا وأصناف السوقة لو أهدى إلى رئيس من الرؤساء أو عظيم من العظماء فرسًا جوادًا أو برذونًا فارها أو جارية حسنة أو كسوة فاخرة لم تكن درجته عند ذلك الرئيس أو العظيم ولا في أعين من نظر إليها من العقلاء، كدرجة ما لو أهدى مثل ذلك أو أدون منه من جل قدره من الملوك وعظم خطره في القلوب ممن اتسع نطاق ملكه واشتدت شوكته وملأت الصدور جلالته وهيبته. فإذا كانت هذه المقدمة صادقة جارية لدى العقلاء مجرى البداية، ثم لا نهاية لجلال الله وكبريائه وعظمته وسلطانه، ولم يكن في عقل أحد من البشر الوقوف على كنه أدنى نعمة أسداها إلى أحد من خلقه وكيف، وقد توالت نعمه وترادفت آلاؤه على عباده، بحيث لو أراد أحد من الخلق وإن كان في الفطنة والذكاء وفي وفور العقل ونفاذ البصيرة آية أن يقف على ما الله تعالى عليه من النعم التي أسداها إليه ونعمة دفع البلايا عنه في لحظة يعجز عن الوقوف على ذلك؟! فكيف ما تواتر عليه طول عمره ومدة حياته؟! وكيف يعرف ذلك ؟! والعقل الذي به نتوصل إلى معرفة الأشياء جزء من أجزاء نعمه والجزء لا يحيط بالكل الذي هو جزء منه. وإذا كان الأمر على ما قررنا لن يتصور من العبد أن يأتي بشكر يكافئ أدنى نعمة من النعم فضلاً عن أن يكون مكافئًا لما تقلب هو فيه من نعمه في جميع عمره. يحققه أنه لا يتمكن من أداء شكر نعمه إلا بتوفيق من الله وتيسير منه على ذلك، وذلك نعمة جديدة يجب بمقابلتها الشكر، ولا يتوصل إلى شكرها إلا بأثر آخر للتوفيق، وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى. فإذا على هذه القضية لا يمكن للعبد الخروج عن شكر نعمة واحدة وإن استغرق جميع عمره لشكرها، فكيف يمكنه إذا شكر جميع نعمه؟! والشكر واجب عليه فلابد من بيان قدر الشكر الذي يستوجبه الله تعالى على عباده على نعمه ليتمكنوا من أداء ذلك. وكذا العقول قاصرة عن كيفية شكر كل نعمة كما هي عاجزة عن الوقوف على كميته وكذا هي قاصرة عن الوقوف على حسن الشكر ودقته فلابد من بيان سمعي يرد بذلك كله ليمكن للعباد التقصي عما لزمهم من عهدة تكليف أداء الشكر أن الحكيم إذا أمر عبدًا من عبيده بشيء لابد من يبين ذلك بيانا يتمكن العبد من الائتمار إذ لولا البيان لكان ذلك تكليف ما ليس في وسع المكلف الائتمار له وهو خارج عن الحكمة.
Then God the Exalted created human beings in the best stature and subjected for them all that is on the earth, without any prior act from them that would make these blessings a recompense or a fulfillment of a due right; thus His blessings were gratuitous. There is no justification for neglecting gratitude for them or permitting ingratitude towards them, because that would entail wasting the blessings and wronging them, so gratitude is obligatory. Then gratitude ought to be proportionate to the blessing, equivalent to it in essence, equal to it in measure. If it were not so, it would not be considered valid nor a counterpart to it such that the Benefactor (may He be generous and munificent) would be satisfied with less than His due. Then blessings vary in their measures and differ in their stations according to the varying measures of the benefactors and the difference of their stations and degrees. So whoever among them is greater in rank or higher in degree and station, the blessings from him are more magnificent in measure and greater in station. For if one of the classes of subjects or types of commoners were to present to a chief among chiefs or a great one among the great a swift horse or a strong steed or a beautiful slave-girl or a fine garment, his rank with that chief or great one would not be, nor in the eyes of those rational persons who look upon it, like the rank of one who presents something similar or even less from one whose measure among kings is great and whose importance in hearts is weighty, whose kingdom's scope is vast and his power is strong and whose grandeur and awe fill breasts. Since this premise is true and proceeds among rational people as a starting point, then there is no end to God's majesty, grandeur, greatness, and sovereignty. It is not within the intellect of any human to comprehend the reality of the least blessing that He has conferred upon any of His creatures. How (then) when His blessings have been successive and His favors have followed one after another upon His servants, such that if any creature, even if he be a sign in acumen, intelligence, abundant reason, and penetrating insight, were to try to comprehend the blessings that God the Exalted has conferred upon him and the blessing of repelling calamities from him in a moment, he would be unable to comprehend that?! So how (can he comprehend) what has been continuous upon him throughout his long life and the duration of his existence?! And how can he know that? The intellect by which we come to know things is a part of the parts of His blessings, and the part does not encompass the whole of which it is a part. And since the matter is as we have established, it is inconceivable that the servant should come with gratitude that equals the least of blessings, let alone be equivalent to what he has been immersed in from His blessings throughout his entire life. It verifies this that he cannot perform the gratitude for His blessings except by success (tawfīq) from God and facilitation from Him for that, and that is a new blessing which requires gratitude in return, and one cannot reach gratitude for it except by another effect of tawfīq, and so on ad infinitum. So upon this proposition, the servant cannot escape the gratitude for a single blessing even if he spends his entire life in thanking it, so how can he thank all his blessings?! And gratitude is obligatory upon him, so there must be an explanation of the measure of gratitude that God the Exalted requires from His servants for His blessings, so that they may be able to perform that. Likewise, intellects are incapable of knowing the modality (kayfiyya) of gratitude for each blessing, just as they are incapable of comprehending its quantity (kammiyya), and likewise they are incapable of comprehending the goodness (ḥusn) of gratitude and its subtlety, so there must be a revealed statement (bayān samʿī) that conveys all of that, so that the servants can discharge what is incumbent upon them from the obligation of performing gratitude. For the Wise One (al-Ḥakīm) when He commands a servant of His with something, He must explain it in an explanation such that the servant can comply, because if there were no explanation, that would be a charge of what is not within the capacity of the charged to comply with, and that is outside of wisdom (ḥikma).
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument for the necessity of gratitude to God and the insufficiency of human reason to determine how to fulfill it. The author begins by stating that God created humans and subjected all things to them without any prior merit, so His blessings are gratuitous. Therefore, ingratitude is wrong and gratitude is obligatory. He then explains that gratitude must be proportionate to the blessing. Since blessings vary according to the rank of the benefactor, and God is infinitely great, no human can fully comprehend even the least of His blessings. The intellect itself is a part of God's blessings, so it cannot encompass the whole. Moreover, each act of gratitude requires further divine facilitation, leading to an infinite regress. Thus, reason alone cannot determine the proper measure or manner of gratitude. Therefore, a revealed explanation (bayān samʿī) is necessary, because a wise commander must make the command understandable for the commanded. This sets up the refutation of the Brahmins in the next section.

Key Terms
شكر
نعمة
كفران
مكافأة
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gratitude is simply saying 'thank you' without regard to the value of the blessing.
Correction: The author argues that gratitude must be proportionate to the blessing, reflecting the status of the Benefactor.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is gratitude obligatory?
A) Because God commands it.
B) Because blessings are gratuitous and ingratitude would be wasting and wronging them.
C) Because it is a rational duty.
D) Because the Brahmins say so.

Key Takeaway
Gratitude for God's blessings is obligatory and must be proportionate, but the infinite gap between God and us makes this impossible by reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the single greatest blessing in your life right now. Did you earn it? Did you deserve it? If not, what does your current level of gratitude reveal about your relationship with the Giver? And what specific act will you do today to make your gratitude more proportionate?
?
You are quick to thank a friend for a small favor, yet how often do you pause to acknowledge the countless gifts from God that sustain you every moment? The passage demands that gratitude match the Benefactor's rank. If God is infinitely greater than any human, why does your actual behavior show more gratitude to people than to Him? What will you do to change this starting now?
?
The passage warns that insufficient gratitude 'would not be considered valid nor a counterpart to it that the Benefactor would be satisfied with less than His due.' Examine your heart: are there blessings you have taken for granted, treating them as though they were ordinary? Will you let this lesson convict you to repent and begin a life of proportional gratitude, even though you know you can never fully match God's greatness?

The Insufficiency of Reason and the Need for Revelation
Argument for the insufficiency of reason and the necessity of divine revelation to define gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human reason cannot determine the proper gratitude for God's blessings and why divine revelation is necessary.

Core Idea
Reason is part of God's blessings and cannot encompass the whole; only revelation can guide us.

After establishing the obligation of proportional gratitude, the author now demonstrates the impossibility of fulfilling it through reason alone. He argues that we cannot even comprehend the least blessing, and each act of gratitude requires further divine facilitation, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, a revealed explanation is necessary for a wise command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on human limitation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the arguments: the intellect cannot grasp even a single blessing; it is a part of the blessings; infinite regress of tawfiq.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
Each act of gratitude requires divine tawfiq, which is itself a new blessing requiring further gratitude, ad infinitum. This shows the impossibility of fulfilling gratitude by reason alone.
3
Recognize the need for revelation
The author concludes that a revealed statement (bayān samʿī) is necessary to explain the measure, modality, and goodness of gratitude. Without it, the command would be impossible, which contradicts divine wisdom.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human reason can independently determine how to thank God properly.

According to the author, why does the infinite regress of tawfīq show the need for revelation?
A) Because tawfīq is not needed.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank Allah for a blessing, do you ever feel that your gratitude is inadequate? Knowing that every act of gratitude is itself a blessing requiring further gratitude, does this realization humble you or overwhelm you? How will you change your approach to thanking Allah today?
?
You rely on your intellect to understand your faith, but this lesson shows that your intellect is part of God's blessings and cannot encompass them. In what specific area of your life—like dealing with trials or expressing thanks—do you assume you know best, ignoring that only revelation can guide you? Will you seek the Qur'an and Sunnah to learn the proper gratitude for every blessing?
?
If you ever thought that you could independently determine the goodness of an action, this passage confronts you with the infinite regress of gratitude. Where in your daily practice do you follow your own opinion rather than divine guidance on how to thank Allah? What is one concrete change you will make to align your gratitude with what Allah has revealed?

Maturidi vs Mu'tazila: The Basis of Prophethood
Al-Nasafi distinguishes the Maturidi method from the Mu'tazila on the necessity of sending prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between Maturidi and Mu'tazila reasoning on the necessity of sending prophets and why the Maturidi method avoids the flawed principle of al-aslah.

Core Idea
Prophethood is known by reason, not by God's obligation to do the most beneficial.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi draws a sharp methodological boundary between the Maturidi school and the Mu'tazila on how to establish prophethood. The Maturidis affirm that reason can discern good and evil, but they reject the Mu'tazila claim that God must send prophets because it is 'most beneficial' for people. Al-Nasafi warns his companions to avoid falling into this corrupt principle. Let's examine the passage to understand the key dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall argument. Notice how al-Nasafi presents the Maturidi method first, then contrasts the Mu'tazila, then gives his verdict.
The passage is a warning and a methodological statement.
2
Identify the two schools' positions
Find what each school says about the basis for sending prophets. Note the key terms: 'al-hasan wa al-qabih' (good and ugly) for Maturidis, and 'al-aslah' (most beneficial) for Mu'tazila.
3
Understand the critique
Al-Nasafi calls the Mu'tazila view 'corrupt speech' and promises to refute it later. Why is it corrupt? Because it imposes an obligation on God based on human benefit, which undermines divine sovereignty.
This connects to the wider kalam debate on God's justice and wisdom.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on why it matters that Maturidis base prophethood on rational discernment (good/evil) rather than obligation of al-aslah. This protects the correct understanding of God's will and power.
This lesson helps you avoid a common theological error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على مثل هذه الطريقة يتكلم الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي، وكل من يقول من أصحابنا بإمكان استدراك الحسن والقبيح بالعقل. فأما المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون بوجوب إرسال الرسل، لما أن ذلك أصلح للعباد وما هو الأصلح للعباد فهو واجب التحصيل على الله تعالى وذلك كلام فاسد نبين فساده عند بلوغنا إلى إبطال القول بالأصلح، فينبغي لمن يتكلم من أصحابنا في هذه المسألة أن يكون بصيرًا بذلك ليصون نفسه في إثبات الرسالة عن الوقوع في القول بالأصلح، والله الموفق.
In such a way speaks the Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and everyone among our companions who says that it is possible to discern the good and the ugly by reason. As for the Mu'tazila, they say that sending messengers is obligatory, because that is most beneficial (aslah) for the servants, and what is most beneficial for the servants is obligatory to be achieved by God—exalted is He. And that is corrupt speech; we will show its corruption when we reach the refutation of the statement about al-aslah. So it is befitting for anyone among our companions who speaks about this issue to be insightful about that, in order to protect himself in establishing prophethood from falling into the statement about al-aslah. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage is a methodological clarification. Al-Nasafi states that Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and the author's school (the Hanafi Maturidis) speak on the basis that good and ugly (al-hasan wa al-qabih) can be known by reason ('aql). In contrast, the Mu'tazila assert that sending messengers is obligatory upon God because it is the most beneficial (al-aslah) for people, and what is most beneficial must be done by God. The author declares this speech corrupt and promises to refute it later when discussing the principle of al-aslah. He advises his fellow scholars to be careful not to fall into the Mu'tazila error when proving prophethood. This shows the importance of maintaining the correct rational foundation for prophethood.

Correction: Maturidis base prophethood on rational discernment of good and evil, not on God's obligation to do the most beneficial for creation.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school affirms prophethood through rational discernment of good and evil, rejecting the Mu'tazila claim that God is obligated to send prophets for the sake of human benefit.

Mutawatir Reports: The Certainty of Mass Testimony
The author reaffirms that mutawatir reports produce necessary knowledge, citing poetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mutawatir reports produce necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī) and how poetic examples illustrate this epistemological certainty.

Core Idea
Mutawatir reports yield certainty as direct as seeing the thing reported.

In this passage, the author reaffirms a foundational principle of Islamic epistemology: mass-transmitted reports (mutawatir) engender necessary knowledge, leaving no room for doubt. He supports this with lines from two pre-Islamic poets who vividly describe how multiple reports eliminate uncertainty. This lesson will help you grasp the strength of mutawatir evidence in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author uses the poets' words to reinforce his point.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that the mutawatir report is a cause of necessary knowledge. Find where he says this and note the phrase 'موجبا للعلم الضروري'.
Necessary knowledge (علم ضروري) is knowledge the mind cannot deny, like sensory experience.
3
Analyze the poetic evidence
Read the lines from Tufayl al-Ghanawi and Suwayd ibn Sabi'. How do they describe the effect of many reports? They mention accumulation until doubt disappears. This illustrates the psychological effect of tawātur.
The poets are not theologians, but their intuitive description aligns with the epistemological concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متباينة، ولهذا قال طفيل الغنوي وهو يذكر موت رجل من قومه: وجاء من الأخبار ما لا يكذب تأويني هم من الليل منصب تظاهرن حتى لم تكن لي ريبة ولم يك غما أخبروا متعقب ألا نراه قال وجاء من الأخبار ما لا يكذب ثم نبه عن العلة فقال: تظاهرن حتى لم تكن لي ريبة ولم يك عما أخبروا متعقب أتتك وقال سويد بن صبغي من الأخبار عين جلية ترادفن حتى ما لقلبك صارف وهذا ظاهر، وقد أقمنا الدلالة في أول الكتاب على كون الخبر المتواتر موجبا للعلم الضروري جاعلاً المخبر عنه كالمنظور إليه للسامع، فلا نشتغل بإعادة ذلك، والله الموفق.
They are diverse, and for this reason Tufayl al-Ghanawi said, while mentioning the death of a man from his people: 'And there came from reports that which does not lie, sheltering me in the night from distress, they accumulated until I had no doubt, and no grief was informed that was followed by a sequel.' Do you not see that he said 'And there came from reports that which does not lie' and then pointed to the cause saying 'they accumulated until I had no doubt, and no grief was informed that was followed by a sequel'? And Suwayd ibn Sabi' said: 'From reports are clear-eyed truths, they follow one after another until your heart has no turning aside.' This is evident, and we have already established the proof at the beginning of the book that the mutawatir report is a cause of necessary knowledge, making the reported thing like something seen by the listener, so we do not engage in repeating that, and Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 703

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark that reaffirms the epistemological status of mutawatir (mass-transmitted) reports. The author argues that such reports produce necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge, comparable to direct sensory observation. He supports this by quoting two pre-Islamic poets who describe the overwhelming certainty produced by multiple corroborating reports. The first quote is from Tufayl al-Ghanawi, who says that multiple reports came that cannot lie, and they accumulated until no doubt remained. The second is from Suwayd ibn Sabi', who says that reports are like clear-eyed truths that follow one another until the heart has no doubt. The author states that this is obvious, and he has already provided the proof at the beginning of his book (referring to an earlier section on the sources of knowledge), so he will not repeat it.

Key Terms
خبر متواتر (mutawatir report)
علم ضروري (necessary knowledge)
تظاهر (accumulation of reports)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutawatir reports only provide probable (ẓannī) knowledge, not certainty.
Correction: The author insists that mutawatir reports produce necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge, as certain as direct observation.

Checkpoint

A) Speculative knowledge
B) Necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī)
C) Only probable knowledge
D) Knowledge that requires further investigation

Key Takeaway
Mutawatir reports produce necessary knowledge (علم ضروري) that leaves no room for doubt, as illustrated by pre-Islamic poetic imagery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What will you do this week to silence that turning?
?
The author says the mutawatir report makes the reported like something seen. If you truly saw the Day of Judgment with your own eyes, how would you live differently right now? Since you have the same certainty through tawatur, what is holding you back from that transformation?

Three Conditions for a Valid Claim
Nasafi enumerates three conditions for a valid claim and explains each briefly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three necessary conditions for a claim to be valid in Islamic theological reasoning.

Core Idea
A valid claim must be possible, supported by matching evidence, and free from defects.

Nasafi lays out the basic criteria by which any claim, especially in theology, is to be judged. These conditions ensure that only rationally sound claims are entertained. They form a methodological filter for evaluating evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its structure. Then read the translation. Notice that Nasafi states three conditions, though he mentions four initially.
The passage starts with 'ثم نزيد في البيان' indicating an additional explanation.
2
Identify each condition
Find the three conditions: possibility of the claim, evidence matching the claim, and absence of defects in the evidence. Note how each is introduced with 'أولها', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
The first condition is about logical possibility; the second about correspondence; the third about integrity.
3
Understand the verification statement
Read the final sentence: 'يحققه : أنها ذكرت ليصح بها غيرها وهو الدعوى...' This explains that evidence exists to validate the claim, and it must itself be sound.
This reinforces the third condition.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why these conditions are important. They prevent acceptance of impossible or unsupported claims, ensuring that only sound arguments are considered in theological discussions.
This connects to the methodology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نزيد في البيان فتقول : إن كل من ادعى دعوى كانت صحة دعواه متعلقة باستجماع معاني أربعة: نفسه :أولها كون الدعوى في حيز الإمكان، فإن من ادعى ما هو ممتنع محال في رد قوله في أول وهلة وأعرض عن سماع بينته. والثاني: أن يأتي ببينة مطابقة للدعوى. والثالث: أن تكون دواعي الجرح منتفية عن بينته إذ لو تمكن فيها جرح بوجه من الوجوه لردت إذ لا قبول إلا لما هو صحيح في نفسه. يحققه : أنها ذكرت ليصح بها غيرها وهو الدعوى، ولا وجه إلى تصحيح شيء ما بما ليس في نفسه بصحيح.
Then we add in explanation and say: Indeed, everyone who makes a claim, the validity of his claim depends on encompassing four meanings: [First:] The claim being within the realm of possibility, for if one claims something impossible, his word is rejected at the outset and one turns away from hearing his evidence. Second: To bring evidence that matches the claim. Third: That the grounds for rejection are absent from his evidence, for if a defect is possible in it in any way, it would be rejected, because there is no acceptance except for that which is sound in itself. He verifies: It was brought to validate other than it, namely the claim, and there is no way to validate something by that which is not sound in itself.
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage sets out the logical conditions for any claim to be considered valid. It states that a claim's soundness depends on four conditions (though only three are explicitly given in the text, which may be a scribal error or omission). The first condition is possibility: the claim must be logically possible; impossible claims are rejected outright. The second is correspondence: the evidence presented must directly match the claim. The third is integrity of evidence: the evidence must be free from any defects or grounds for rejection, because only sound evidence can validate something else. The explanation confirms that evidence exists to validate the claim, and it cannot be based on unsound material. These conditions are used in kalam to evaluate claims, especially prophetic claims, forming a methodology for testing the validity of evidence.

Key Terms
دعوى
بينة
إمكان
جرح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any evidence is acceptable for a claim, regardless of its soundness or relevance.
Correction: The passage insists evidence must be sound (free from defects) and directly match the claim; otherwise it is rejected.

Checkpoint
According to the three conditions, what happens if a claim is impossible?
A) It is considered but needs strong evidence.
B) It is rejected at the outset.
C) It requires divine revelation.
D) It can be accepted if many people believe it.

Key Takeaway
The three conditions—possibility, matching evidence, and absence of defects—are essential for evaluating the validity of any claim in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a claim about Islam—whether in debate or in your own heart—do you ensure that your claim is logically possible, that your evidence matches it exactly, and that your evidence is sound? Which of these three conditions do you most often neglect, and what will you do to fix that starting today?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you have never seriously questioned. If you applied Nasafi's conditions to it, would it pass? If not, are you willing to abandon it, or will you continue to hold it despite being impossible or unsupported? What concrete step will you take this week to test one of your beliefs against these conditions?

The Four Conditions for a Valid Claim
Imam al-Nasafi enumerates and explains four conditions for a valid claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four essential conditions that must be met for any claim to be considered valid in Islamic theological argumentation.

Core Idea
A claim is only as strong as its evidence, and evidence must be flawless.

Before we can accept any claim—whether about God, the world, or a legal matter—we need a clear standard for what makes a claim valid. Imam al-Nasafi provides four conditions that must all be present. These conditions form the logical backbone of argumentation in kalam, ensuring that claims are not accepted blindly but are tested against reason and consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without worrying about every word. Let the rhythm of the author's enumeration sink in. Then read the translation.
Notice the author's phrase 'لابد من شروط أربعة' – this signals an enumeration.
2
Identify each condition
As you read, pick out the four conditions: possibility, matching evidence, no grounds for rejection, no contradiction. See how each is explained with a brief reasoning.
The author uses 'أولها', 'والثاني', 'والثالث', 'والرابع' to mark them.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each condition
For each condition, ask: Why is this necessary? For example, why can't an impossible claim be considered? The author gives a logical justification that makes sense.
Focus on the thālith (third) condition: evidence must be sound in itself.
4
Reflect on the fourth condition
The fourth condition about contradiction is the longest. The author explains why contradictory statements invalidate a claim. Try to follow the logic: if A and B are opposites, affirming both means accepting their mutual falsity.
This is a powerful principle in logic and theology.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
Consider how these conditions apply to the central claim of prophethood. The author is setting up a test for any claim to prophethood.
This framework will be used later to evaluate the prophethood of Muhammad (PBUH).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لابد من شروط أربعة لتصح الدعوى: ثم نزيد في البيان فتقول : إن كل من ادعى دعوى كانت صحة دعواه متعلقة باستجماع معاني أربعة: أولها كون الدعوى في حيز الإمكان، فإن من ادعى ما هو ممتنع محال في رد قوله في أول وهلة وأعرض عن سماع بينته. والثاني: أن يأتي ببينة مطابقة للدعوى. والثالث: أن تكون دواعي الجرح منتفية عن بينته إذ لو تمكن فيها جرح بوجه من الوجوه لردت إذ لا قبول إلا لما هو صحيح في نفسه. يحققه : أنها ذكرت ليصح بها غيرها وهو الدعوى، ولا وجه إلى تصحيح شيء بما ليس في نفسه بصحيح. والرابع سلامة الدعوى والبينة عن مناقضة ترد عليهما أو على أحدهما، إذ المناقض معترف بنقض ما ادعاه وفساده، إذ هو بادعائه صحة أحد المتناقضين شاهد ببطلان الآخر، فصار بادعائه صحة كل واحد منهما في محل شاهدا بفساد الآخر، فإذًا صار معترفا بفساد الآخر، فإذا صار معترفًا بفساد كل واحد من الأمرين ثم لا شك في كون صحة الدعوى متعلقة بهذه المعاني.
There must be four conditions for a claim to be valid. Then we add in explanation and say: Indeed, every person who makes a claim, the validity of his claim is dependent on the gathering of four meanings: First: that the claim be in the realm of possibility, for whoever claims something impossible is rejected at first glance and one turns away from hearing his evidence. Second: that he brings evidence that matches the claim. Third: that the grounds for rejection be absent from his evidence, for if any ground for rejection is found in it in any way, it would be rejected, because nothing is accepted except what is sound in itself. Its verification: that evidence is brought so that something else (the claim) becomes sound, and there is no way to make something sound by means of what is not itself sound. Fourth: the safety of the claim and the evidence from a contradiction that invalidates both or one of them, for the contradictory person acknowledges the invalidity and corruption of what he claimed, because by claiming the validity of one of two contradictories, he witnesses to the invalidity of the other. Thus, by claiming the validity of each of them, he becomes in a position of witnessing to the invalidity of the other. Thus, he becomes an acknowledger of the invalidity of each of the two matters. Then there is no doubt that the validity of a claim is dependent on these meanings.
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage enumerates and explains the four necessary conditions for any claim to be considered valid. The author (Imam al-Nasafi) is establishing a logical framework that will later be applied to the prophetic claim. The four conditions are: 1. The claim must be possible (imkān), not impossible (mumtani‘). If someone claims something logically impossible, their statement is rejected immediately without requiring evidence. 2. The claimant must bring evidence (bayyina) that corresponds exactly to the claim. The evidence must be on the same subject and support the claim directly. 3. The evidence must be free from any grounds for rejection (jarḥ). If there is any flaw in the evidence itself, it cannot be used to establish the claim. The author gives a reasoning: evidence is brought to make the claim sound; you cannot make something sound using something that is not itself sound. 4. There must be no contradiction (munāqaḍa) between the claim and the evidence, or within the evidence itself. The author explains that if a person makes contradictory statements, they effectively invalidate their own claim, because affirming one false opposite implies denying the other. The passage concludes that the validity of a claim is indeed tied to these four meanings.

Key Terms
دعوى
إمكان
بينة
جرح
مناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that as long as there is some evidence, the claim is valid.
Correction: The author shows that evidence must be flawless and consistent; a single flaw or contradiction invalidates the evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if someone makes a claim about something impossible?
A) It is considered but requires strong evidence.
B) It is rejected at first glance without hearing evidence.
C) It is accepted if supported by a scholar.
D) It is postponed for further investigation.

Key Takeaway
A valid claim must be possible, supported by matching evidence, free from flaws, and free from contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last tried to convince someone of a truth about Allah, did you first check whether your claim was actually possible? What impossibility might you have unknowingly defended?
?
Your evidence for a belief—does it exactly match the claim you're making, or have you stretched it? Recall a specific instance; how does it measure up to the second condition?
?
Have you ever held two opposing beliefs that contradict each other? How does the fourth condition challenge you to resolve that tension before you present your argument to others?

The Fourth Condition: Freedom from Contradiction
Al-Nasafi states the fourth condition and explains its rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a claim and its evidence must be free from contradiction to be valid, and how internal contradiction implicitly admits the corruption of the claim.

Core Idea
A self-contradictory claim already admits its own falsehood.

Al-Nasafi now presents the fourth condition for a valid da'wa (claim). It might seem obvious that a claim should not be contradictory, but he goes deeper: even an implicit contradiction—where the claimant's own statements in different contexts conflict—suffices to invalidate it. This passage explains the logical mechanism behind that invalidation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi states the condition and then argues for it.
Pay attention to the phrase 'المتناقضين' (the two contradictories).
2
Follow the reasoning chain
Al-Nasafi builds his argument step by step. Identify how claiming the truth of one contradictory makes the claimant a witness against the other, and how this leads to admitting corruption of both.
Try to map out the logical steps in your own words.
3
Reflect on the condition's importance
Consider why this condition is fundamental for any valid argument. How does it prevent sophistry? Think about how it applies to theological or legal debates.
This connects back to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع سلامة الدعوى والبيئة عن مناقضة ترد عليهما أو على أحدهما، إذ المناقض معترف بنقض ما ادعاه وفساده، إذ هو بادعائه صحة أحد المتناقضين شاهد ببطلان الآخر، فصار بادعائه صحة كل واحد منهما في محل شاهدا بفساد الآخر، فإذًا صار معترفا بفساد الآخر، فإذا صار معترفًا بفساد كل واحد من الأمرين ثم لا شك في كون صحة الدعوى متعلقة بهذه المعاني.
And the fourth [condition] is the safety of the claim and the evidence from a contradiction that refutes both of them or one of them, for the one who contradicts admits the contradiction of what he claimed and its corruption, because by his claiming the correctness of one of the two contradictories he is a witness to the invalidity of the other, so by his claiming the correctness of each one of them in a place he becomes a witness to the corruption of the other, so then he becomes an admitter of the corruption of the other, so when he becomes an admitter of the corruption of each of the two matters, then there is no doubt that the correctness of the claim is connected to these meanings.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth condition for a valid claim (da'wa) and its evidence (bayyina). The condition is that both the claim and the evidence must be free from any contradiction (munaqada) that would refute them, either both or one of them. Al-Nasafi explains the reasoning: a person who contradicts himself implicitly admits that what he claimed is contradictory and therefore corrupt. How so? If someone claims the truth of two contradictory statements, his claim that one is true is itself evidence that the other is false. By claiming each one separately in a particular context, he effectively bears witness against the other. Thus he becomes an implicit confessor to the corruption of each. Since the soundness of a claim depends on these meanings (i.e., freedom from contradiction), any internal conflict in the claim or evidence undermines the case.

Key Terms
سلامة الدعوى
مناقضة
المتناقضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only explicit and obvious contradictions invalidate a claim.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that even an implicit contradiction—where the claimant's own statements in different contexts conflict—suffices to invalidate it, because claiming one implies falsity of the other.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why does a contradiction in the claim or evidence invalidate the claim?
A) Because the contradiction confuses the audience.
B) Because the claimant, by claiming the truth of one contradictory, implicitly testifies to the falsehood of the other, thus admitting the corruption of both.
C) Because contradiction is a logical error that makes the claim impossible.
D) Because the evidence must be consistent to be considered valid in court.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a claim demands that it and its evidence be internally consistent; any contradiction, even implicit, renders the claim unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a claim about your faith—like 'I trust Allah's plan'—do you ever act in ways that betray that trust? How is that a living contradiction that this lesson exposes?
?
Think of a time you used evidence to defend a belief but later realized that evidence conflicted with itself. How does al-Nasafi's condition force you to see that as a sign the belief itself may be unsound?
?
If your own statements about your beliefs were put on trial, would they be internally consistent? What one contradiction in your life will you resolve today to make your testimony more truthful?

The Quran's Inimitable Eloquence and the Theologians' Limit
Highlights the miraculous style of the Quran and notes the limited extraction by theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's style differs from human argumentation and why theologians have only scratched the surface of its proofs.

Core Idea
The Quran's arguments are fathomless; scholars have barely tapped its depths.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla reflects on the divine speech of the Quran. It contrasts human argumentation with the majestic, masterful style of the Quran, and then humbly notes that even the greatest theologians have only extracted a fraction of its meanings. As you read, reflect on the awe that the Quran inspires and its role as the ultimate source of theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the rhythm and awe of the language sink in.
2
Observe the contrast in speech
Notice how Allah's speech is described as master-to-servant, not like human debate. What does this say about the Quran's authority?
The terms 'wondrous arrangement' and 'eloquent description' highlight its miraculous nature.
3
Consider the theologians' role
The passage states that theologians did not add to the Quran but only extracted a fraction. Reflect on what this means for the study of kalam.
This emphasizes the Quran as the primary source of all arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن الله تعالى لا يخرج كلامه مخرج كلام الواحد منا إذا ناظر خصمه بل يخرج مخرج مخاطبة السادة العبيد، والأرباب المماليك، فأخرج هذه المعاني على هذا النظم العجيب والوصف البليغ الذي تكاد العقول الوافرة، والألباب الكاملة تتحير في رونقه وفخامته وبلاغته وفصاحته، بل تذهل من هيبته وجلالته، ثم إن المتكلمين من سلف الأمة منذ تكلموا في هذه الخصوم وأثبتوا دلائلها في التصاريف لم يزيدوا على ما في الكتاب من مع الإشارة إلى ذلك، بل لم يستخرجوا من قعور تلك البحور وإن غاصت فيها أفكارهم عشر ما فيها من درر الحجج وغرر المعاني والنكت
However, Allah, the Exalted, does not bring forth His speech in the manner of the speech of one of us when he argues with his opponent; rather, He brings it forth in the manner of addressing masters to servants, and lords to slaves. So He brought forth these meanings in this wondrous arrangement and eloquent description which ample intellects and complete minds almost become bewildered in its splendor, grandeur, eloquence, and clarity, rather they become astounded by its awe and majesty. Then indeed the theologians (mutakallimun) from the predecessors of the community, since they spoke in these disputes and established their proofs in the compilations, did not add to what is in the Book with reference to that; rather, they did not extract from the depths of those oceans, even if their thoughts dived into them, a tenth of the gems of proofs and choice meanings and subtleties that are in it.
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage reflects on the unique style of the Quran. The author notes that Allah's speech is not like human debate; it is masterly and majestic, overwhelming the intellect. Then he comments that the early theologians (mutakallimun) who engaged in these discussions and wrote proofs merely drew from the Quran without adding anything beyond it. In fact, despite their deep efforts, they only extracted a fraction of the Quran's arguments and subtleties. This underscores the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz) and its primacy as the source of all kalam arguments.

Key Terms
النظم العجيب
الوصف البليغ
المتكلمون
درر الحجج
غرر المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theological arguments are independent inventions separate from the Quran.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that all kalam proofs are derived from the Quran; theologians only extracted a fraction of its abundant arguments.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the theologians (mutakallimun) regarding the Quran?
A) They invented new arguments not found in the Quran
B) They only extracted a fraction of the Quran's arguments
C) They improved upon the Quran's arguments
D) They rejected the Quran's style

Key Takeaway
The Quran contains an inexhaustible treasury of proofs, and even the deepest scholarship only scratches its surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you approach it as a limitless ocean or as a shallow pool you've already mastered?
?

?
Are you willing to set aside your intellectual pride and admit that the Quran's depths far exceed your grasp, letting it transform you rather than merely confirming what you already believe?

The Source of Prophetic Knowledge and Divine Attributes
Concluding the proof of prophethood with divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet's knowledge is from Allah, who has absolute power and control, as a conclusion to the proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Allah's power is absolute; the Prophet's knowledge is a gift from Him.

After presenting the proof of prophethood, the author draws a conclusion about the source of the Prophet's knowledge. This passage emphasizes that all knowledge came from Allah, the Mighty and Praiseworthy, and describes His absolute sovereignty. Understanding this reinforces the divine origin of the message.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic text from the beginning of the chunk up to 'tawfiq wa tasdid'. Notice how it connects to the previous proof.
This is a doxological passage.
2
Identify the attributes of Allah mentioned
Look for phrases like 'al-aziz al-hamid', 'la yumani'u fi tadbirihi', etc. List the attributes described.
These attributes highlight Allah's absolute control.
3
Reflect on the implications for prophethood
Consider why the author ends the proof with this description. It confirms the divine origin of the Prophet's knowledge.
This reinforces the authenticity of his message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة يعلم أنه علم ما علم من عند العزيز الحميد الذي يكرم من يشاء من عباده (بما يشاء) (1) ويعلم من يشاء ما يشاء لا يمانع في تدبيره ولا يغالب في سلطانه، ولا يدافع في مشيئته، ولا يعاز في تنفيذ قضيته، له البسط والقبض والإبرام والنقض، يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد، وهو ولي التوفيق والتسديد. [ما هو راجع إلى شريعته] وأما ما هو راجع إلى شريعته التي أتى بها فنقول: إن مدار أمور الدين والملة عند جميع أرباب الأديان يكون متعلقا بالاعتقادات والعبادات والمعاملات والمزاجر والآداب الحسنة والشيم الحميدة، وأن محمدا عليه السلام أتى في كل باب من هذه الأبواب الخمسة بما يشهد له العقل الصريح بالصحة، يظهر ذلك عند بيان كل جملة من هذه الجمل على التفصيل، والنظر في كل من ذلك يعين الإنصاف فنقول : [أقسام الاعتقادات]: أما الاعتقادات فمنقسم عند أرباب الديانات إلى الأركان الخمسة بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله واليوم الآخر.
And by pausing upon this summary, it is known that he knew what he knew from the Mighty, the Praiseworthy, Who honors whom He wills of His servants (with whatever He wills) (1) and teaches whom He wills whatever He wills—He is not opposed in His planning, nor overcome in His authority, nor resisted in His will, nor hindered in executing His decree. To Him belongs expansion and contraction, binding and unbinding. He does what He wills and judges what He desires. He is the Guardian of success and guidance. [What pertains to His Shari'ah] And as for what pertains to His Shari'ah with which he came, we say: the pivot of the affairs of religion and the community according to all adherents of religions is related to beliefs, acts of worship, transactions, deterrences, good manners, and praiseworthy traits. And Muhammad, peace be upon him, brought in every one of these five categories what sound intellect testifies to its correctness. That becomes clear when each of these categories is explained in detail, and considering each of them helps one to be fair. So we say: [Sections of Beliefs] As for beliefs, among adherents of religions they are divided into the five pillars: faith in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, and the Last Day.
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage serves as a transition from the proof of prophethood to the systematic exposition of the Shari'ah. The author first concludes that from the previous argument, it is clear that the Prophet's knowledge was from Allah, who has absolute power and control. Then he introduces the discussion of the Shari'ah, stating that the foundation of religion among all religious communities revolves around five categories: beliefs (i'tiqadat), acts of worship (ibadat), transactions (mu'amalat), deterrences (mazajir), and good manners and traits (adab hasana wa shiyam hamida). He asserts that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) brought in each of these categories that which sound intellect confirms to be correct. This will be demonstrated by detailing each category. Then he begins with beliefs, which are divided among the adherents of religions into five pillars: belief in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, and the Last Day. This sets the stage for the subsequent detailed discussion of the five pillars of faith.

Knowledge of Religion is from Allah
Nasafi concludes that the Prophet's knowledge is from Allah and describes His absolute sovereignty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet's knowledge comes directly from Allah, not through natural acquisition, and that Allah is described with attributes of absolute power and sovereignty.

Core Idea
The Prophet's knowledge is a divine gift, not a human achievement.

Having established the impossibility of knowledge through natural acquisition, Imam Nasafi now affirms that true religious knowledge is granted by Allah. He describes Allah's absolute power and will in bestowing knowledge upon whomever He chooses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping at the translation. Let the rhythm of the original sink in. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the series of negations that emphasize Allah's incomparable authority.
2
Identify the key attributes of Allah
The passage describes Allah with phrases like 'لا يمانع في تدبيره' and 'له البسط والقبض'. List these attributes. What do they tell us about the source of the Prophet's knowledge?
3
Connect to the previous argument
Recall that the author previously argued that knowledge cannot be acquired naturally. Now he concludes that it must come from Allah. Why is this conclusion important for the study of kalam?
This step ties the passage to the broader theological context.

وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة يعلم أنه علم ما علم من عند العزيز الحميد الذي يكرم من يشاء من عباده بما يشاء ويعلم من يشاء ما يشاء لا يمانع في تدبيره ولا يغالب في سلطانه، ولا يدافع في مشيئته، ولا يعاز في تنفيذ قضيته، له البسط والقبض والإبرام والنقض، يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد، وهو ولي التوفيق والتسديد. وأما ما هو راجع إلى شريعته التي أتى بها فنقول: إن مدار أمور الدين والملة عند جميع أرباب الأديان يكون متعلقا بالاعتقادات والعبادات والمعاملات والمزاجر والآداب الحسنة والشيم الحميدة، وأن محمدا عليه السلام أتى في كل باب من هذه الأبواب الخمسة بما يشهد له العقل الصريح بالصحة، يظهر ذلك عند بيان كل جملة من هذه الجمل على التفصيل، والنظر في كل من ذلك يعين الإنصاف فنقول :

The Five Categories of Religion
Nasafi enumerates the five categories of religion and states that they are attested by sound reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five categories of religion that Nasafi identifies and understand that they are universal and validated by sound reason.

Core Idea
Religion covers beliefs, worship, transactions, punishments, and manners.

After affirming the divine origin of the Prophet's knowledge, Imam Nasafi turns to the content of the shari'a. He identifies five universal categories that form the core of religious life, and asserts that the Prophet's teachings in each are attested by sound reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the five listed categories. Try to memorize them in Arabic.
Note that the author includes both 'good manners' and 'praiseworthy traits' as one category.
2
Identify the five categories
List the five categories mentioned: الاعتقادات, العبادات, المعاملات, المزاجر, and الآداب الحسنة والشيم الحميدة. Understand each term.
3
Reflect on the claim of rationality
The author says each category contains teachings that 'sound reason' testifies to their correctness. Why is this claim important for the defense of the Prophet's message?
This connects to the kalam project of showing that Islamic teachings are rational.

And by comprehending this summary, it is known that he knew what he knew from the Almighty, the Praiseworthy, who honors whomever He wills from His servants with whatever He wills and teaches whomever He wills whatever He wills; there is no opposition to His planning, nor any overcoming of His authority; there is no repelling of His will, nor any difficulty in executing His decree; to Him belongs expansion and contraction, ratification and abrogation; He does as He wills and judges as He desires, and He is the Guardian of success and guidance. As for what pertains to His law that he brought, we say: The pivot of the affairs of religion and the community, according to all adherents of religions, is connected to beliefs, acts of worship, transactions, punishments, good manners, and praiseworthy traits. And Muhammad, peace be upon him, brought in every one of these five categories what sound reason testifies to its correctness. That becomes clear when each of these categories is explained in detail, and consideration of all of this aids fairness. So we say:
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage first serves as a conclusion to the previous proof: by understanding the impossibility of natural acquisition, one knows that the Prophet's knowledge comes from Allah, who is described with attributes of absolute power and sovereignty. The author then transitions to the content of the Prophet's law (shari'a). He states that the affairs of religion revolve around five categories: beliefs (i'tiqadat), acts of worship (ibadat), transactions (mu'amalat), punishments (mazajir), and good manners and praiseworthy traits (adab wa shiyam). These five are universal among all religious communities. The Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) brought teachings in each of these categories that sound reason testifies to their correctness. The author promises to detail each category. The passage ends by introducing the next sections of the book.

The Five Categories of Transactions
The author enumerates five categories of transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of transactions (mu'amalat) and how Islamic jurisprudence classifies them.

Core Idea
Islamic transactions fall into five clear categories: finance, marriage, litigation, trusts, and bequests.

The science of Islamic transactional law (fiqh al-mu'amalat) can seem vast, but it becomes manageable when you see its foundational structure. In this passage, the author gives a clear five-fold classification that organizes all transactions. This classification is a key tool for any student of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification list
Read the Arabic passage once to see the five categories. Notice the flow from financial to personal to legal to trust to inheritance.
Each category is introduced with 'wa' (and).
2
Examine each category and its examples
The author gives two examples for each category. Understand these examples as typical representatives.
For instance, 'financial negotiations' includes sales and rentals.
3
Consider the comprehensiveness
This classification is meant to cover the major areas of transactional law. Ask if any transaction could fall outside these five.
The classification is representative, not exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعاملات تنقسم إلى خمسة: وهي المفاوضات المالية كالبيوع والإجارات، والمناكحات كالتزويج والتطليق والمخاصمات كالدعاوي والبينات والأمانات كالودائع والعواري، والتركات كالوصايا والمواريث.
And transactions are divided into five: financial negotiations like sales and rentals, marriages like marriage and divorce, litigations like claims and evidences, trusts like deposits and loans, and bequests like wills and inheritances.
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage classifies transactions (mu'amalat) into five categories. The first is financial negotiations (al-mufawadat al-maliyya) which include sales and rentals. The second is marital matters (al-munakahat) which include marriage and divorce. The third is litigations (al-mukhasamat) which include claims and evidence. The fourth is trusts (al-amanat) which include deposits and loans. The fifth is bequests (al-tarikat) which include wills and inheritances. This classification is a comprehensive division of transactional jurisprudence.

Five Categories of Deterrents (Mazajir)
The author enumerates five categories of deterrents (mazajir).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of deterrents (mazajir) in Islamic penal law and their associated punishments.

Core Idea
Deterrents are classified into five types based on the protected interest.

The author presents a concise classification of deterrents (mazajir) in Islamic criminal law. This passage organizes the major prescribed punishments under five categories corresponding to the rights or interests they protect. Understanding this classification helps in grasping the structure of Islamic penal theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to see the five categories, then read the translation. Notice the structure: each category begins with 'مزجرة' followed by the violation and punishment.
The Arabic uses 'مزجرة' for each type.
2
Identify each category
List the five categories: homicide, theft, violation of honor, defamation, and apostasy. Note the specific penalties for each.
Penalties range from retaliation to flogging and execution.
3
Analyze the pattern
Observe how each deterrent pairs a specific violation with a prescribed punishment. The five cover major hudud crimes.
This classification is systematic, not exhaustive.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why these five are highlighted—they protect the core interests of life, property, honor, lineage, and religion.
This aligns with the maqasid al-shari'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمزاجر تنقسم أيضا إلى خمسة أقسام: وهي مزجرة قتل النفس كالقود والدية، ومزجرة أخذ المال كالقطع أو الصلب، ومزجرة هتك الستر كالجلد أو الرجم، ومزجرة ثلب العرض كالجلد مع التفسيق ورد الشهادات على التأبيد، ومزجرة خلع البيضة كالقتل على الردة.
And the deterrents are also divided into five divisions: the deterrent for killing the soul like retaliation and blood money, the deterrent for taking wealth like amputation or crucifixion, the deterrent for violating honor like flogging or stoning, the deterrent for defaming honor like flogging with disqualification and permanent rejection of testimonies, and the deterrent for abandoning the religion like execution for apostasy.
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage classifies deterrents (mazajir) into five categories. The first is for homicide (qatl al-nafs), with penalties of retaliation (qisas) or blood money (diya). The second is for theft (akhdhal-mal), with penalties of amputation (qat') or crucifixion (salb). The third is for offenses against modesty (hatk al-sitr), with penalties of flogging (jald) or stoning (rajm). The fourth is for slander (thalb al-'ird), with penalties of flogging, disqualification (tafsiq), and permanent rejection of testimony. The fifth is for apostasy (khala' al-bayda), with the penalty of execution (qatl). This systematic classification covers major hudud punishments.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five categories listed are the only deterrents in Islamic law.
Correction: The passage focuses on major prescribed punishments (hudud); other deterrents exist, such as discretionary punishments (ta'zir).

Checkpoint

A) Homicide
B) Theft

Key Takeaway
The five deterrents systematically cover major hudud crimes protecting life, property, honor, lineage, and religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The deterrent for killing includes retaliation and blood money. Do you truly believe that these earthly punishments are the most just system for the human soul, or do you secretly think your own sense of mercy is superior?
?

?
These deterrents protect five essential interests. Which of these five have you personally prioritized least in your life? What would it cost you to honor that interest more deeply starting today?

Imam Nasafi enumerates four divisions of manners and ethics.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Manners and ethics encompass character, etiquette, policies, and social conduct.

This short passage from Imam Nasafi presents a foundational classification of ethics into four distinct but complementary domains. Before exploring each in depth, the author provides this overview to show the comprehensive scope of ethical teachings. Let's examine this division.

Guided Path

These terms have deeper implications in Islamic ethics.

The common misunderstanding is that they are separate; the passage hints at their unity under 'manners and ethics'.

Why Imam Nasafi Omits Detailed Discussion on Transactions
Imam Nasafi explains why he omits detailed discussion of transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasons behind Imam Nasafi's decision to omit detailed discussion of transactions and other secondary legal topics in his work.

Core Idea
Sometimes a scholar omits not from ignorance but from wisdom and consideration of the reader's capacity.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi explains his methodological choice to skip detailed treatments of transactions, marriages, and other legal areas after covering acts of worship. He cites both practical constraints—avoiding excessive length and reader disinterest—and a deeper theological reason: the divine law is so profound that even the most dedicated thinkers cannot fully grasp it. Understanding his reasoning helps us appreciate the author's pedagogical awareness and humility before the vastness of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Nasafi structures his explanation: first stating his omission, then acknowledging the potential benefit of including it, then giving the reasons for omission.
2
Identify the two reasons for omission
The passage gives two primary reasons: the length of the book and the lack of enthusiasm of readers. Also note the deeper point about the ineffable wisdom of the religion.
3
Consider the deeper theological argument
Nasafi argues that the religion's wisdom is so deep that even the best minds cannot fully comprehend it. This serves as a proof that the law is divine, not human-made.
4
Reflect on the author's audience awareness
Notice how Nasafi adapts his work to his audience's capacity ('weakness of desires of seekers'). This is a pedagogical principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في المعاملات:
وإذ أتينا على ذكر العبادات فنترك ذكر ما في المعاملات والمناكح والمآكل والمشارب وفي تحريم ما حرم وتحليل ما حلل، وذكر الكفارات، وتقسيم الجرائم التي في بعضها كفارة، وفي بعضها حد وبيان الحكمة من ذلك كله لئلا يطول الكتاب. وإن كنا لو ذكرنا بعض ما أورده علماء الأمة وحكماء أهل الملة وفرسان الكلام وقادة أهل الإسلام لكانت الفائدة متضاعفة والعائدة متوفرة، غير أن فتور رغبات الملتمسين وقصور همهم، حالا بيني وبين الإبانة عن كل ذلك. وأن دينًا بني على الحكمة المحضة والمصلحة التامة على وجه تقصر دون الإحاطة بعشر من أعشار ذلك عقول ألف ألف من المرتاضين الذين أذابوا مطايا أفكارهم سنين كثيرة في التأمل في ذلك، والبحث عنه لتحقيق أن لا يظن عاقل له حظ من الإنصاف وجانب الإلف أو العصبية الجنسية أو الأنفة أو الفرار عن لزوم ربقة التكليف، أن مثله من أوضاع من لم يعرف بتعلم نوع من أنواع العلوم والارتياض بشيء من المعارف بل يتعين أنه هو الوحي الإلهي والشرع السماوي.
Discussion on transactions:
Since we have come to mention acts of worship, we leave off mentioning what is in transactions, marriages, foods, drinks, and the prohibition of what is forbidden, and the analysis of what is permitted, and the mention of expiations, and the division of crimes – some of which have expiation, and some have fixed punishments – and the explanation of the wisdom behind all that, so that the book not become lengthy. For if we were to mention some of what the scholars of the community and the wise men of the religion and the knights of kalam and the leaders of the people of Islam have presented, the benefit would be multiplied and the return abundant. However, the weakness of the desires of the seekers and the deficiency of their intentions prevented me from explaining all that. And that a religion built on pure wisdom and complete benefit in a way that the minds of a thousand thousand of ascetics, who have worn out the mounts of their thoughts for many years in reflecting on that and researching it, fall short of encompassing a tenth of its tenths – so that no rational person with a share of fairness, free from familiarity, tribal fanaticism, haughtiness, or escape from the yoke of obligation, should think that it is from the inventions of one who did not know any type of learning or take up any knowledge. Rather, it is necessarily divine revelation and heavenly law.
Source pages p. 775

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note where Imam Nasafi explains why he will not go into detailed discussions of transactions (mu'amalat), marriages, foods, drinks, and other legal topics. He begins by stating that since he has already covered acts of worship (ibadat), he will omit the rest to avoid lengthening the book. He acknowledges that if he were to present the insights of earlier scholars and experts in kalam, the benefit would be great, but the lack of enthusiasm and limited capacity of the readers prevents him. He then provides a deeper reason: the religion is founded on pure wisdom and complete benefit, so profound that even the most dedicated thinkers cannot fully grasp it. Therefore, no fair-minded person could attribute this religion to human invention; it is undoubtedly divine revelation and heavenly law. The author thus justifies his omission by emphasizing the ineffable depth of Islamic law and the practical constraints of his audience.

Key Terms
المعاملات
الحكمة المحضة
الوحي الإلهي
الشرع السماوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Imam Nasafi omitted detailed topics because he was not knowledgeable about them.
Correction: In reality, he acknowledges the great benefit that could come from including such discussions, but he refrains out of consideration for the reader's limited capacity and to avoid excessively lengthening the book. He also emphasizes that the depth of the divine law is beyond human comprehension, further justifying his omission.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Nasafi omit detailed discussion of transactions and other topics?
A) He considers them unimportant
B) He fears the book becoming too long and readers lacking enthusiasm
C) He does not know enough about them
D) He believes they are not part of the religion

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi's omission of detailed discussions on transactions is a deliberate pedagogical choice rooted in concern for the reader's capacity and a profound appreciation for the ineffable wisdom of divine law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a topic or a book because it seemed too 'lengthy' or 'deep'? What does Imam Nasafi's decision to omit for the sake of the reader's capacity teach you about your own impatience with knowledge?
?
Imam Nasafi states that even the most dedicated ascetics fall short of encompassing a tenth of the wisdom of this religion. Does this humble you? In what area of your life are you acting as if you have fully understood Islam, when this lesson shows you its infinite depth?
?

Mu'tazilite Proofs for Precedence of Ability: Quran and Rational Evil
The author presents Mu'tazilite arguments from Quranic verses and rational repulsiveness of commanding the incapable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs the Mu'tazilites used to argue that ability must precede action, based on Quranic verses and rational reasoning.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites argue that ability must come before action, or else God's commands would be unjust.

In this passage, the author presents the Mu'tazilite case for the precedence of ability (istita'a) over the action they enable. They draw on two Quranic verses and a rational argument about the impossibility of commanding what is impossible. This sets the stage for the Maturidi response, which we will encounter later. Let us examine each proof carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the proofs
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it contains three distinct arguments: two from the Quran and one from reason.
The first proof uses verse 64:16, the second uses verse 7:171, and the third is a rational proof using analogy.
2
Examine the first Quranic proof
The Mu'tazilites interpret 'fear Allah as much as you are able' to mean that even those who lack taqwa must have the ability to attain it. This shows that ability precedes the act of taqwa.
Focus on the phrase 'with the absence of taqwa' – ability is present even when the act is not.
3
Examine the second Quranic proof
The verse 'take what We have given you with strength' is used to argue that strength (ability) must be prior to the action of taking, just as the hand must precede the act of grabbing.
This is an analogy from everyday experience.
4
Understand the rational proof
The Mu'tazilites argue that if ability were not prior, commanding a disbeliever to believe would be demanding the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq), which is rationally evil. The analogy of commanding a lame person to run illustrates this.
This proof is key: it shows that their argument is not only scriptural but also based on reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاحتجت المعتزلة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَاتَّقُوا اللَّهَ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ ) فينبغي أن يكون كل من لزمه التقوى (مع عدم ) (۳) التقوى (إذ غير المتقي لزمه التقوى، فينبغي أن يكون معه استطاعة التقوى وفي وجود استطاعة التقوى (٤) ولا تقوى، قول متقدم استطاعة التقوى على التقوى) (۹) واحتجوا أيضا بقوله: ﴿ خُذُوا مَا ءَاتَيْنَكُم بِقُوَّةٍ (٢) والأخذ بقوة (۲) لن يتصور أن تكون القوة سابقة على الأخذ كالأخذ باليد لن يتصور إلا وأن تكون اليد سابقة عليه. والمَعْقُولُ لَهُمْ أن الْقُدْرَةَ لَوْ لَمْ تَكُنْ سَابِقَةً عَلَى الْفِعْلِ وَلَمْ تَكُنْ مَعَ الْكَافِرِ قُدْرَةُ الْإِيمَانِ سَابِقَةً عَلَى الْإِيمَانِ لَكَانَ الْأَمْرُ لَهُ بِالْإِيمَانِ مُحَالاً لِمَا فِيهِ (۸) مِنْ تَكْلِيفِ مَا لَا يُطَاقُ، وَهُوَ قَبِيحٌ فِي بَدِيهَةِ الْعَقْلِ فَإِنْ مَنْ أَمَرَ عَبْدَهُ الْمُقْعَدَ بِالْعَدْوِ وَعَبْدَهُ الْأَعْمَى بِالنَّظَرِ يُعَدُّ سَفِيهًا خَارِجًا عَنِ الْحِكْمَةِ
For the Mu'tazilites argued by His saying, the Exalted: 'So fear Allah as much as you are able' (Q. 64:16). So it must be that everyone who is obliged to have taqwa (with the absence of taqwa) (since the one not possessing taqwa is obliged to have taqwa, so he must have with him the ability for taqwa, and in the presence of the ability for taqwa and no taqwa, this is a statement that the ability for taqwa precedes the taqwa). And they also argued by His saying: 'Take what We have given you with strength' (Q. 7:171). And taking with strength cannot be conceived unless the strength is prior to the taking, like taking with the hand cannot be conceived unless the hand precedes it. And the rational proof for them (is that) if ability were not prior to the action and if the disbeliever did not have the ability for faith prior to faith, then the command to him for faith would be impossible because it contains the imposition of what is not possible, and that is evil in the immediate reason, for whoever commands his lame slave to run and his blind slave to see is considered a fool, outside of wisdom.
Source pages p. 792

Commentary
This passage presents the first set of proofs that the Mu'tazilites bring for their doctrine that ability (istita'a) must precede the action. The author first cites two Quranic verses. The first, 'So fear Allah as much as you are able,' is interpreted to mean that the person who does not have taqwa is still commanded to have it, so he must possess the ability for taqwa even though he does not perform it, indicating that the ability precedes the act. The second, 'Take what We have given you with strength,' is taken to show that the strength must be prior to the taking, as the hand must be prior to the act of taking. Then the author presents the rational argument: if ability were not prior, commanding the disbeliever to believe would be demanding what is impossible (taklif ma la yutaq), which is rationally evil. He gives a clear analogy: a master who commands a lame servant to run or a blind servant to see is considered foolish. Thus the Mu'tazilites conclude that ability must be prior to the action, otherwise divine commands would be unjust and impossible.

Key Terms

تقوى
قوة
قدرة
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite argument is purely scriptural.
Correction: But they also use a rational argument about the impossibility of commanding the incapable, which they consider evident to reason.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, what does the verse 'Take what We have given you with strength' indicate about ability and action?
A) Strength is created at the time of taking.
B) Strength must precede the taking.
C) Strength is irrelevant to the action.
D) The verse proves the opposite.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites use both scripture and reason to argue that ability must precede action, otherwise divine commands would be unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often have you told yourself, 'I can't do this for Allah,' when in fact this lesson shows ability comes before the command? Which command of Allah are you avoiding right now because you think it's too hard? Will you commit tonight to start obeying it, trusting that the ability is already there?

The analogy of the lame slave commanded to run: Are you using your own weaknesses or circumstances as excuses to avoid obedience? If you truly believed that Allah never commands the impossible, what is one thing you would stop making excuses about and simply do?
?
If the Mu'tazilites are wrong and ability does not precede the act, then what does that mean for your responsibility? Can you honestly claim that any sin you commit is because Allah did not give you the ability to avoid it? Reflect on one sin you blame on circumstances, and ask yourself: Did I actually have the ability to resist? What will you change tonight?
