The Rule for Accepting a Non-Apparent Intended Meaning
Rule for accepting a non-apparent intended meaning in speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when a speaker's claim of a non-apparent intended meaning is accepted and how this applies to interpreting divine speech.

Core Idea
A speaker's later clarification is trusted if plausible and they are not suspected.

This passage addresses a key principle in interpreting speech: what happens when a speaker says one thing but later claims they meant something else? The author outlines the conditions under which such a claim is accepted, and then extends the principle to God's own speech in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from human speech to divine speech.
2
Identify the key conditions
The passage states two conditions for accepting a non-apparent intended meaning. Find them: the meaning must be linguistically possible, and the speaker must not be under suspicion.
Also note the contrast: if the meaning is impossible, it is never accepted.
3
Apply the principle to God
The author applies the same logic to God. Since God is not suspect, His indications of specification (takhṣīṣ) are valid from the time of revelation.
Think about how this affects interpretation of Quranic verses that seem general but have specific applications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيما يدعيه من إرادته خلاف الظاهر، فكان هذا مذهبًا منهم أن من تكلم بلفظ ثم عنى به ما يحتمله اللفظ يصدق، وإن كان فيه عدول عن ظاهرة إذا لم يكن متهما في ذلك، وفيه دليل أنهم ما جعلوا ظاهر اللفظ عند تعريه عن دليل يدل على أن المراد به خلاف الظاهر دليلاً على إرادة الظاهر، إذ لو كان كذلك لما قبل منه قوله أني عنيت خلاف الظاهر وإن لم يكن متهمًا في ذلك، كمن ادعى ما لا يحتمله اللفظ لا يصدق وإن لم يكن متهمًا في ذلك . ثم الله تعالى غير متهم فيما يقيم من دلالة الخصوص ينبغي أن يكون ذلك دليل إرادة الخصوص من وقت نزوله، والله أعلم.
Regarding what he claims of his intention contrary to the apparent meaning, so this was a position of theirs that whoever speaks with an expression then intends by it what the expression can bear is believed, even if it is a departure from its apparent meaning, if he is not under suspicion in that. And in it is evidence that they did not make the apparent meaning, when stripped of evidence that indicates that the intended meaning is contrary to the apparent meaning, a proof for intending the apparent meaning. For if it were so, his statement 'I intended contrary to the apparent meaning' would not be accepted from him even if he is not under suspicion in that. Like one who claims what the expression does not bear is not believed even if he is not under suspicion in that. Then God Most High is not under suspicion in what He establishes as an indication of specification; it should be that it is a proof for intending the specification from the time of its revelation. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 1070

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the rules of interpreting speech. The author states that according to a group of scholars, if a person utters a word and then later claims that he intended a meaning that the word can possibly bear (even if it is not the apparent meaning), his claim is accepted provided he is not suspected of dishonesty. This shows that they do not consider the apparent meaning, when there is no evidence indicating a different intended meaning, as definitive proof that the apparent meaning is intended. If it were definitive, then the speaker's claim of a non-apparent meaning would never be accepted even if he is trustworthy. However, if the claimed meaning is not even linguistically possible, then it is not accepted. Then the author applies this to God: since God is not under suspicion, any indication of specification (takhṣīṣ) that He provides should be taken as proof that the specification was intended from the very beginning, not later.

Key Terms
عنَى
ظاهر
عدول
دليل
إرادة
خصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The apparent meaning of a statement is always what the speaker intended unless there is strong evidence to the contrary.
Correction: The passage shows that if the speaker later claims a non-apparent meaning that is linguistically possible, and the speaker is trustworthy, the claim is accepted. The apparent meaning is not considered definitive proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a speaker's claim of a non-apparent meaning accepted?
A) Always, regardless of the possibility
B) Only if the apparent meaning is ambiguous
C) If the claimed meaning is linguistically possible and the speaker is not suspected
D) Never, the apparent meaning always stands

Key Takeaway
A speaker's claim of a non-apparent intended meaning is accepted if the meaning is linguistically possible and the speaker is not suspected; this applies to God, so His indications of specification are valid from the start.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran that seems clear, do you ever pause to consider that God might have intended a deeper meaning beyond the apparent? How does this lesson challenge your assumption that you always know exactly what God means? Will you now be more humble in interpreting His words?
?
If you were to claim that you meant something different from what you said, would others believe you? Does your character make you trustworthy? How does this lesson about God's trustworthiness move you to seek sincerity in your own speech?
?
This lesson shows that God's indications of specification are valid from the start. How does this affect your view of abrogation or apparent contradictions in the Quran? Do you trust that God's intended meaning was always consistent, even if it seems to change?

The First Interpretation: 'Day' as Non-Literal or the Prophet's Era
First interpretation: 'day' is not literal but either rhetorical or referring to the Prophet's era.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the word 'day' in the verse does not necessarily refer to a specific 24-hour day but can be interpreted as a rhetorical opening or the entire era of the Prophet.

Core Idea
The verse's 'day' may not mean a 24-hour day at all.

The opponents of the Maturidi school claim that the verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' proves that Islam was incomplete before that specific day. In this lesson, we explore the first of three interpretations: the word 'day' is not literal. It either functions as a common opening phrase or refers to the Prophet's entire era. Understanding this will help you see how the verse is understood metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of the argument. Notice the two sub-interpretations discussed.
2
Identify the two aspects
The author presents two ways to understand 'day' in the first interpretation. Find them and note the king analogy.
3
Ask: Why does this matter?
Consider how this interpretation refutes the opponent's claim that religion was incomplete before that day.
This connects to the overall refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوه تأويل آية اليوم أكملت والله أعلم على وجوه:
أحدها - أنه لا يريد باليوم يوما يشار إليه كما لا يريد بقوله: ﴿ وَرَضِيتُ لَكُمُ الإِسْلَامَ دِينًا ﴾ يومًا يشار إليه بل كان الدين مرضيا عنده ثم بعد ذلك وجهان:
أحدهما: إسقاط معنى اليوم وحمله على ما جرت العادة في التكلم به لافتتاح الكلام دون تحقيق الوقت.
والثاني: أن يراد به عصر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذ كان قبل ذلك فترة وقد كانت مست الحاجة إلى من يبين لهم الحق فيبين ذلك برسوله عليه السلام في ذلك العصر وهذا ظاهر في مبتذل الكلام، فإن ملكًا من الملوك لو من على أهل مملكته فقال: إن من كان سبقكم من آبائكم كانوا في زمن من كان ملك الولاية قبلي في أنواع الفتن وصنوف من المصائب والمحن بسبب سوء مملكته وخوفه في معاملة وتعديه عن الصواب في تدابير سياسته ورسوم إمارته، وأنتم اليوم في ظل السلامة وتحت جناح الأمنة وكنف الدعة والراحة بيمن سياستي وشفافتي ورأفتي على طبقات رعيتي وأهل مملكتي لا يريد بذلك اليوم الذي يخاطبهم فيه بذلك بل جميع عصره وزمان ثبوت سلطانه ودولته فكذا هذا والله أعلم.
والثاني من وجوه التأويل: أن يكون قوله : (أَكْمَلْتُ) أي أظهرت لكم دينكم حتى قدرتم على إظهاره في كل مشهد، وأظفرتكم بعدوكم حتى ييأسوا من ترككم الدين كقوله تعالى: الْيَوْمَ يَبِسَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا مِن دِينِكُمْ وقوله : (وَاللَّهُ مُتِمُّ نُوره) وقوله: وَيَأْبَى اللَّهُ إِلَّا أَن يُتِمَّ نُورَهُ ولا يجوز أن يكون نور الله تعالى غير تام في نفسه حتى يتمه ولكن يتمه في الخلق لأن يظهره فيهم.
والثالث: أن يكون تمامه بما أرعب عدوهم وأكرم الرسول عليه السلام برعب العدو وآمن المؤمنين عنهم، فعلى هذا أكمل ذلك اليوم والأمر بالمعروف فمن يقتل عدوه من الملوك يقول اليوم تم ملكي وعزتي وسلطاني فمثله الأول، على أن تمام الشيء بوجهين:
أحدهما: باستكمال ما لا يقوم دونه.
والثاني: باستيعاب ما له بعد التزين والتحسين مما هو في حق النبع، ثم ما كان واحدًا في نفسه أو متناهيا يتم بشيء آخر فضلاً كان ذلك أو شرطًا فأما الذي لا يحد في نفسه فلا كمال له لا بفضله ولا شرطه فكان كماله على أصلهم محالاً فكان تعلقهم به باطلاً، والله الموفق.
The interpretations of the verse 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' – and Allah knows best – are according to [several] aspects:
One of them: that He does not intend by 'day' a specific day that is pointed to, just as He does not intend by His saying: 'And I am pleased with Islam as your religion' a specific day that is pointed to; rather, the religion was pleasing to Him. Then after that, there are two aspects:
The first: dropping the meaning of the day and carrying it upon what is customary in speech to open the discourse without specifying the time.
The second: that what is intended by it is the era of the Prophet ﷺ, since before that there was a period of intermission and there was a need for someone to clarify the truth for them, so He clarified that through His Messenger عليه السلام in that era. This is apparent in common speech. For if a king were to bestow favor upon the people of his kingdom and say: 'Indeed, those who preceded you from your fathers were in the time of whoever was the king of the province before me in various kinds of tribulations and types of calamities and hardships due to the evil of his rule and his fear in dealing and his deviation from correctness in the strategies of his policy and the customs of his emirate, while you today are in the shadow of safety and under the wing of security and the shelter of ease and comfort by the blessing of my policy, my compassion, and my kindness toward the classes of my subjects and the people of my kingdom' – he does not intend by that the specific day on which he addresses them, but rather his entire era and the time of the establishment of his authority and his state. So likewise is this, and Allah knows best.
The second of the aspects of interpretation: that His saying 'I have perfected' means: I have made your religion manifest to you until you became able to manifest it in every gathering, and I gave you victory over your enemy until they despaired of your abandoning the religion, like His saying: 'Today those who disbelieve have despaired of your religion' and His saying: 'And Allah will perfect His light' and His saying: 'But Allah refuses except to perfect His light.' And it is not permissible that the light of Allah Almighty is imperfect in itself until He perfects it; rather, He perfects it in the creation by making it manifest among them.
The third: that its perfection was by His terrifying their enemy and honoring the Messenger عليه السلام by the terror of the enemy and giving security to the believers from them. So on this basis, that day perfected the matter of enjoining good. So whoever kills his enemy from among the kings says: 'Today my kingship, my might, and my authority have been perfected.' So the first is similar to it. Indeed, the perfection of a thing is in two respects:
One of them: by completing that without which it cannot stand.
The second: by encompassing that which belongs to it after adornment and improvement in terms of what is in the right of the origin. Then whatever is one in itself or finite is perfected by another thing, whether that be a bounty or a condition. As for that which is not delimited in itself, it has no perfection neither by its bounty nor by its condition. So its perfection according to their principle is impossible, so their reliance upon it is false. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1096

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of an unnamed opponent's claim that the verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' indicates that the religion was incomplete before that day. The author presents three interpretations (وجوه) showing that perfection refers to manifestation and victory, not essential completion.
First interpretation: The word 'day' is not literal; it either is merely an opening phrase (as in common speech) or refers to the entire era of the Prophet. The author illustrates with a king's speech: when a king says 'today you are safe', he means his entire reign, not just that moment.
Second interpretation: 'Perfected' means 'made manifest' and 'given victory'. The religion was always perfect in itself; the completion is in its outward establishment. Quoting Quranic verses about God perfecting His light, the author argues that God's light is never imperfect; He perfects it in the creation by making it apparent.
Third interpretation: Perfection here means terrifying enemies and honoring the Messenger, so the religion was perfectly manifested on that day of victory. Then the author provides a philosophical analysis of 'perfection' (تمام). Perfection can be of two types: (1) completing what is necessary for existence, (2) encompassing all that belongs to a thing after adornment. Finite things can be perfected by something else, but the infinite (like God's attributes) cannot be perfected. Therefore, the opponent's claim that the religion was imperfect before this verse is false because religion has no delimitation (حد) in itself; it is from God, who is infinite. Thus, their argument collapses. The author concludes that their reliance on this verse is invalid.

Key Terms
تأويل
اليوم
التمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the word 'day' in the verse must refer to a specific 24-hour day.
Correction: The author shows that 'day' can be interpreted as a rhetorical opening or as the entire Prophet's era.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, what is one possible meaning of 'day' in the verse?
A) A specific Friday when the verse was revealed.
B) A rhetorical opening phrase without reference to time.
C) The first day of the Islamic calendar.
D) The day of Judgement.

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation shows that the verse's 'day' need not be taken literally, undermining the opponent's argument based on a specific date.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Today I have perfected for you your religion,' do you truly feel that your religion is complete for you today? Or do you secretly crave something more to fulfill you? What does this lesson teach you about the completeness of Islam being a continuous reality in your life, and what one concrete change can you make today to live as if your religion is truly sufficient?
?
The author says that the religion was already pleasing to Allah before this verse. How does that challenge your view of previous religions or even your own past before you were guided? Are you able to see Allah's hand in all times and not just in your 'now'? What practice can you adopt to recognize His blessings throughout your entire journey?
?
If you were to describe your current state of faith using a king's analogy as in the lesson, would you say you are in the 'shadow of safety' or still in 'tribulations'? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's promise of perfection? What step will you take today to anchor your security in His religion rather than in circumstances?

The Second Interpretation: 'Perfected' Means Made Manifest and Victorious
Second interpretation: 'perfected' means made manifest and given victory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation that 'perfected' refers to making the religion manifest and granting victory, not essential completion.

Core Idea
God's perfection of religion is about making it manifest, not repairing a deficiency.

In this lesson, we examine the second interpretation of the verse. The author argues that 'I have perfected' means 'I have made manifest' and 'given victory.' He supports this with Quranic verses about Allah perfecting His light, emphasizing that God's light is never imperfect—only its manifestation is completed in creation. This further refutes the idea that the religion was incomplete before.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author redefines 'perfected' as 'made manifest'.
2
Examine the Quranic proofs
The author quotes three verses about Allah perfecting His light. What do these verses teach about perfection?
3
Reflect on the refutation
How does this interpretation show that the opponents' claim is invalid?

Source Text

The interpretations of the verse 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' – and Allah knows best – are according to [several] aspects:
One of them: that He does not intend by 'day' a specific day that is pointed to, just as He does not intend by His saying: 'And I am pleased with Islam as your religion' a specific day that is pointed to; rather, the religion was pleasing to Him. Then after that, there are two aspects:

One of them: by completing that without which it cannot stand.
The second: by encompassing that which belongs to it after adornment and improvement in terms of what is in the right of the origin. As for that which is not delimited in itself, it has no perfection neither by its bounty nor by its condition. Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of an unnamed opponent's claim that the verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' indicates that the religion was incomplete before that day. The author presents three interpretations (وجوه) showing that perfection refers to manifestation and victory, not essential completion.

Then the author provides a philosophical analysis of 'perfection' (تمام). Perfection can be of two types: (1) completing what is necessary for existence, (2) encompassing all that belongs to a thing after adornment. Finite things can be perfected by something else, but the infinite (like God's attributes) cannot be perfected. Therefore, the opponent's claim that the religion was imperfect before this verse is false because religion has no delimitation (حد) in itself; it is from God, who is infinite. Thus, their argument collapses. The author concludes that their reliance on this verse is invalid.

Key Terms

أظهرت
التمام

Correction: The author explains that 'perfected' means 'made manifest and victorious', not that the religion was incomplete in itself.

?

?

The Third Interpretation and the Nature of Perfection
Third interpretation and the twofold nature of perfection leading to refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third interpretation and the philosophical analysis of perfection, which demonstrates that the religion cannot be imperfect because it is from God.

Core Idea
Perfection of finite things requires something else; but God's religion has no delimitation, so it cannot be perfected.

In this final lesson, we examine the third interpretation: that perfection refers to terrifying the enemies and honoring the Messenger. Then the author delves into a deeper analysis of two types of perfection: completing what is necessary and encompassing all that belongs after adornment. He argues that finite things can be perfected by something else, but the religion—being from the infinite God—cannot be said to have been imperfect. Thus, the opponents' argument collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the third interpretation and the subsequent philosophical discussion.
2
Identify the two types of perfection
The author defines two ways a thing can be perfect: by completing its essential requirements (شرط) or by adding bounty (فضل). Find these.
3
Apply the analysis
Why does the author conclude that the opponent's belief is false? Note that religion from God has no limit, so it cannot be perfected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوه تأويل آية اليوم أكملت والله أعلم على وجوه:
أحدها - أنه لا يريد باليوم يوما يشار إليه كما لا يريد بقوله: ﴿ وَرَضِيتُ لَكُمُ الإِسْلَامَ دِينًا ﴾ يومًا يشار إليه بل كان الدين مرضيا عنده ثم بعد ذلك وجهان:
أحدهما: إسقاط معنى اليوم وحمله على ما جرت العادة في التكلم به لافتتاح الكلام دون تحقيق الوقت.
والثاني: أن يراد به عصر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذ كان قبل ذلك فترة وقد كانت مست الحاجة إلى من يبين لهم الحق فيبين ذلك برسوله عليه السلام في ذلك العصر وهذا ظاهر في مبتذل الكلام، فإن ملكًا من الملوك لو من على أهل مملكته فقال: إن من كان سبقكم من آبائكم كانوا في زمن من كان ملك الولاية قبلي في أنواع الفتن وصنوف من المصائب والمحن بسبب سوء مملكته وخوفه في معاملة وتعديه عن الصواب في تدابير سياسته ورسوم إمارته، وأنتم اليوم في ظل السلامة وتحت جناح الأمنة وكنف الدعة والراحة بيمن سياستي وشفافتي ورأفتي على طبقات رعيتي وأهل مملكتي لا يريد بذلك اليوم الذي يخاطبهم فيه بذلك بل جميع عصره وزمان ثبوت سلطانه ودولته فكذا هذا والله أعلم.
والثاني من وجوه التأويل: أن يكون قوله : (أَكْمَلْتُ) أي أظهرت لكم دينكم حتى قدرتم على إظهاره في كل مشهد، وأظفرتكم بعدوكم حتى ييأسوا من ترككم الدين كقوله تعالى: الْيَوْمَ يَبِسَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا مِن دِينِكُمْ وقوله : (وَاللَّهُ مُتِمُّ نُوره) وقوله: وَيَأْبَى اللَّهُ إِلَّا أَن يُتِمَّ نُورَهُ ولا يجوز أن يكون نور الله تعالى غير تام في نفسه حتى يتمه ولكن يتمه في الخلق لأن يظهره فيهم.
والثالث: أن يكون تمامه بما أرعب عدوهم وأكرم الرسول عليه السلام برعب العدو وآمن المؤمنين عنهم، فعلى هذا أكمل ذلك اليوم والأمر بالمعروف فمن يقتل عدوه من الملوك يقول اليوم تم ملكي وعزتي وسلطاني فمثله الأول، على أن تمام الشيء بوجهين:
أحدهما: باستكمال ما لا يقوم دونه.
والثاني: باستيعاب ما له بعد التزين والتحسين مما هو في حق النبع، ثم ما كان واحدًا في نفسه أو متناهيا يتم بشيء آخر فضلاً كان ذلك أو شرطًا فأما الذي لا يحد في نفسه فلا كمال له لا بفضله ولا شرطه فكان كماله على أصلهم محالاً فكان تعلقهم به باطلاً، والله الموفق.
The interpretations of the verse 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' – and Allah knows best – are according to [several] aspects:
One of them: that He does not intend by 'day' a specific day that is pointed to, just as He does not intend by His saying: 'And I am pleased with Islam as your religion' a specific day that is pointed to; rather, the religion was pleasing to Him. Then after that, there are two aspects:
The first: dropping the meaning of the day and carrying it upon what is customary in speech to open the discourse without specifying the time.
The second: that what is intended by it is the era of the Prophet ﷺ, since before that there was a period of intermission and there was a need for someone to clarify the truth for them, so He clarified that through His Messenger عليه السلام in that era. This is apparent in common speech. For if a king were to bestow favor upon the people of his kingdom and say: 'Indeed, those who preceded you from your fathers were in the time of whoever was the king of the province before me in various kinds of tribulations and types of calamities and hardships due to the evil of his rule and his fear in dealing and his deviation from correctness in the strategies of his policy and the customs of his emirate, while you today are in the shadow of safety and under the wing of security and the shelter of ease and comfort by the blessing of my policy, my compassion, and my kindness toward the classes of my subjects and the people of my kingdom' – he does not intend by that the specific day on which he addresses them, but rather his entire era and the time of the establishment of his authority and his state. So likewise is this, and Allah knows best.
The second of the aspects of interpretation: that His saying 'I have perfected' means: I have made your religion manifest to you until you became able to manifest it in every gathering, and I gave you victory over your enemy until they despaired of your abandoning the religion, like His saying: 'Today those who disbelieve have despaired of your religion' and His saying: 'And Allah will perfect His light' and His saying: 'But Allah refuses except to perfect His light.' And it is not permissible that the light of Allah Almighty is imperfect in itself until He perfects it; rather, He perfects it in the creation by making it manifest among them.
The third: that its perfection was by His terrifying their enemy and honoring the Messenger عليه السلام by the terror of the enemy and giving security to the believers from them. So on this basis, that day perfected the matter of enjoining good. So whoever kills his enemy from among the kings says: 'Today my kingship, my might, and my authority have been perfected.' So the first is similar to it. Indeed, the perfection of a thing is in two respects:
One of them: by completing that without which it cannot stand.
The second: by encompassing that which belongs to it after adornment and improvement in terms of what is in the right of the origin. Then whatever is one in itself or finite is perfected by another thing, whether that be a bounty or a condition. As for that which is not delimited in itself, it has no perfection neither by its bounty nor by its condition. So its perfection according to their principle is impossible, so their reliance upon it is false. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1096

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of an unnamed opponent's claim that the verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' indicates that the religion was incomplete before that day. The author presents three interpretations (وجوه) showing that perfection refers to manifestation and victory, not essential completion.
First interpretation: The word 'day' is not literal; it either is merely an opening phrase (as in common speech) or refers to the entire era of the Prophet. The author illustrates with a king's speech: when a king says 'today you are safe', he means his entire reign, not just that moment.
Second interpretation: 'Perfected' means 'made manifest' and 'given victory'. The religion was always perfect in itself; the completion is in its outward establishment. Quoting Quranic verses about God perfecting His light, the author argues that God's light is never imperfect; He perfects it in the creation by making it apparent.
Third interpretation: Perfection here means terrifying enemies and honoring the Messenger, so the religion was perfectly manifested on that day of victory. Then the author provides a philosophical analysis of 'perfection' (تمام). Perfection can be of two types: (1) completing what is necessary for existence, (2) encompassing all that belongs to a thing after adornment. Finite things can be perfected by something else, but the infinite (like God's attributes) cannot be perfected. Therefore, the opponent's claim that the religion was imperfect before this verse is false because religion has no delimitation (حد) in itself; it is from God, who is infinite. Thus, their argument collapses. The author concludes that their reliance on this verse is invalid.

Key Terms
تمام
الحد
فضلاً
شرطاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That religion can be essentially perfected by an event (e.g., the verse).
Correction: The author shows that perfection applies only to finite things; religion from God is not finite, so it cannot be said to have been imperfect and then perfected.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophical analysis, why is the opponents' reliance on the verse false?
A) Because the verb 'أكملت' indicates past tense.
B) Because that which is not delimited in itself cannot be perfected.
C) Because the verse was abrogated.
D) Because the word 'today' means the Day of Judgment.

Key Takeaway
The third interpretation and the analysis of perfection definitively refute the opponents' claim by showing that the religion's source is not subject to perfection.

Two Interpretations of 'Akmaltu' and the Nature of Completion
Al-Nasafi presents two interpretations of 'akmaltu' and a general discussion on completeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of 'akmaltu' and why God's perfection cannot involve literal completion.

Core Idea
God's perfection is absolute; 'completion' in revelation means manifestation, not increase.

Al-Nasafi continues his discussion of the verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' by offering two more interpretations. He then provides a philosophical analysis of what it means for something to be 'complete', arguing that the concept cannot apply to God in a literal sense. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage lists a second and third interpretation of 'akmaltu'. Find the verses quoted to support each and note how Al-Nasafi reframes 'completion' as manifestation or victory.
The second interpretation cites Quran 5:3 and 9:32; the third uses a king metaphor.
3
Examine the general discussion on completion
After the interpretations, Al-Nasafi gives a two-aspect definition of completion and applies it to finite vs. infinite beings. Understand why he says the opponents' view is false.
Key point: only finite things can be completed by something external; the infinite cannot.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني من وجوه التأويل: أن يكون قوله : (أَكْمَلْتُ) أي أظهرت لكم دينكم حتى قدرتم على إظهاره في كل مشهد، وأظفرتكم بعدوكم حتى ييأسوا من ترككم الدين كقوله تعالى: الْيَوْمَ يَئِسَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا مِن دِينِكُمْ وقوله : ( وَاللَّهُ مُتِمُّ نُورِهِ) وكقوله: وَيَأْبَى اللَّهُ إِلَّا أَن يُتِمَّ نُورَهُ ولا يجوز أن يكون نور الله تعالى غير تام في نفسه حتى يتمه ولكن يتمه في الخلق لأن يظهره فيهم. والثالث: أن يكون تمامه بما أرعب عدوهم وأكرم الرسول عليه السلام برعب العدو وآمن المؤمنين عنهم، فعلى هذا أكمل ذلك اليوم والأمر بالمعروف فمن يقتل عدوه من الملوك يقول اليوم تم ملكي وعزتي وسلطاني فمثله الأول، على أن تمام الشيء بوجهين: أحدهما : باستكمال ما لا يقوم دونه. والثاني: باستيعاب ما له بعد التزين والتحسين مما هو في حق النبع، ثم ما كان واحدًا في نفسه أو متناهيا يتم بشيء آخر فضلاً كان ذلك أو شرطًا فأما الذي لا يحد في نفسه فلا كمال له لا بفضله ولا شرطه فكان كماله على أصلهم محالاً فكان تعلقهم به باطلاً، والله الموفق.
The second of the modes of interpretation: that His saying 'I have perfected' means I have manifested to you your religion until you were able to manifest it in every gathering, and I gave you victory over your enemy until they despaired of your leaving the religion, like His saying, exalted: 'Today those who disbelieve have despaired of your religion' and His saying: 'And Allah is the perfecter of His light' and His saying: 'And Allah refuses except to perfect His light.' And it is not permissible that the light of Allah, exalted, be incomplete in itself such that He perfects it, but He perfects it in creation to manifest it among them. And the third: that its perfection was by His terrifying their enemy and honoring the Messenger, peace be upon him, with the terror of the enemy and securing the believers from them. Thus, 'I have perfected that day and the command to good' – just as the king who kills his enemy says: 'Today my kingdom, my might, and my authority are perfected.' And its like is the first. Moreover, the completion of a thing is in two aspects: One: by attaining what it cannot do without. The second: by encompassing what belongs to it after adornment and improvement, in the manner of a prophet. Then whatever is one in itself or finite is completed by something else, whether that be a virtue or a condition. As for that which is not delimited in itself, it has no completion, neither by its virtue nor by its condition, so its completion according to their principle is impossible, and thus their clinging to it is false. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 1097

Commentary
This passage discusses two interpretations of the word 'أَكْمَلْتُ' (I have perfected) from the Quranic verse 'اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم' (Today I have perfected your religion for you). The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, presents the second and third interpretations. The second interpretation holds that 'perfection' here means God manifested the religion to the believers, enabling them to proclaim it openly, and granted them victory over their enemies. This is supported by Quranic verses about the disbelievers' despair and God perfecting His light. The author stresses that God's light is inherently perfect; its 'completion' means making it manifest among creation. The third interpretation takes perfection as God terrifying the enemies and honoring the Prophet, securing the believers. The author then provides a general philosophical discussion on completion: a thing is completed either by acquiring what is essential or by receiving additional adornments. Only finite things can be completed by something external. That which is infinite and undelimited in itself cannot be completed, as it lacks nothing. Thus, applying the concept of completion to God in a literal sense is false. The passage refutes an opposing view that likely claimed God's religion needed completion, asserting that God's perfection is absolute and not subject to increase or decrease.

Key Terms
التأويل
الإكمال
التمام
النبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' implies that the religion was deficient before, and that God's own perfection can increase.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that God's perfection is absolute and cannot increase. The 'completion' refers to manifesting the religion and granting victory, or to terrifying enemies and honoring the Prophet. Moreover, completion in the sense of acquiring what is lacking only applies to finite things, not to God.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi, what does 'completion' mean for that which is not delimited in itself?
A) It can be completed by a virtue
B) It can be completed by a condition
C) It has no completion at all
D) It is completed by adornment

Key Takeaway
The verse 'I have perfected your religion' does not imply that God's religion was deficient; rather, it signifies manifestation, victory, and the terrifying of enemies, and the concept of completion cannot apply to God's essence.

The Necessity of the Imam and His Functions
Nasafi enumerates functions of the Imam and argues via Companions' consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical functions of the Imam and how the Companions' consensus establishes the necessity of the imamate.

Core Idea
The Imam's functions are essential for communal order, proven by the Companions' unanimous agreement.

Nasafi begins by acknowledging the vastness of the topic but commits to brevity. He then lists the many functions an Imam must perform, from governing and defense to social justice and worship. Finally, he points to the Companions' consensus as the decisive proof that an Imam is obligatory. This lesson explores that chain of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author's methodological note about brevity.
2
Identify the Imam's functions
List each duty Nasafi assigns to the Imam. They range from executing rulings and guarding frontiers to managing social services like marriage.
These functions show the Imam's role is both religious and worldly.
3
Understand the consensus proof
Nasafi cites the Companions' agreement as the key evidence. Note that their dispute was only over the person, not the principle.
Consensus (ijma') is a primary source of Islamic law.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these specific functions are highlighted and how they support the necessity of the imamate.
Think about the consequences if these functions were not fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الرابع
الكلام في الإمامة
الكلام في هذا الباب يطول جدًا، ولا وجه للاستقصاء فيه في هذا الكتاب الذي من شرطه الاختصار والإيجاز، ونبين ما لابد منه وقدر ما يقع به للراغب في معرفة الكفاية بمشيئة الله تعالى وعونه فنقول – وبالله التوفيق:

إن المسلمين لابد لهم من إمام يقوم بتنفيذ أحكامهم، وإقامة حدودهم وسد ثغورهم، وتجهيز جيوشهم، وأخذ صدقاتهم، وحماية بيضتهم، وقطع مادة شرور المتغلبة والمتلصصة وقطاع الطريق، وإقامة الجمع والأعياد، وقطع المنازعات الواقعة بينهم، وقبول الشهادات القائمة على الحقوق لئلا يؤدي التمادي في المنازعة إلى التقاتل والتفاني، وتزويج الصغار والصغائر الذين لا أولياء لهم وقسمة ما أفاء الله عليهم من الغنائم، ولهذا أجمعت الصحابة على نصب الإمام، وكذلك طلبوه ولم يجر بينهم نزاع في وجوب طلبه وتعيينه بل جرى ذلك في التعيين إلى أن انعقد الإجماع على نصب أبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه ثم مع ما بينا من إجماع الصحابة على ذلك والحاجة الماسة إليه لما بينا من الأمور.
The Fourth Chapter: Speech on the Imamate. Speech in this chapter is very lengthy, and there is no scope for full investigation in this book, whose condition is conciseness and brevity. We will explain what is necessary and sufficient for the seeker in knowing the sufficiency, by the will of Allah and His help. We say – and with Allah is success: Indeed, the Muslims must have an Imam who executes their rulings, establishes their prescribed punishments, guards their frontiers, equips their armies, collects their charities, protects their community, cuts off the source of evils of usurpers, thieves, and highway robbers, establishes Friday and 'Eid prayers, settles disputes among them, accepts testimonies based on rights so that prolonged dispute does not lead to fighting and destruction, marries off young boys and girls who have no guardians, and distributes the spoils that Allah has granted them. For this reason, the Companions unanimously agreed on the appointment of an Imam, and likewise they sought him, and there was no dispute among them about the obligation of seeking and appointing him; rather, the dispute occurred only regarding the specific person until consensus was reached on the appointment of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him). Then, with what we have mentioned of the Companions' consensus on that and the pressing need for it due to the matters we have explained.
Source pages p. 1109

Commentary
This passage combines a methodological introduction and a substantive argument for the imamate. It first explains the author's approach of brevity, then enumerates the functions of an Imam, showing that the community cannot function without a leader. It then cites the consensus of the Companions as definitive proof. The author notes that the dispute among the Companions was only about the identity of the Imam, not the principle, further strengthening his argument.

Correction: The list includes many secular functions like guarding frontiers and distributing spoils, showing it covers all community affairs.

Key Takeaway
The imamate is necessary for both religious and worldly affairs, as shown by the Companions' consensus.

Which of these responsibilities are you personally avoiding in your own spheres of influence? How does your silence or inaction contribute to the very disorder the Imam is meant to prevent?
?
If the first priority of the Companions after the Prophet's death was choosing an Imam, what does that say about your own priorities? Have you ever placed personal comfort or worldly ambition above establishing Allah's order in your family or circle? What would it look like to repent and realign your priorities here and now?

Methodology: Text vs. Rational Unveiling in Maturidi Theology
Nasafi contrasts two methodological approaches to the caliphate ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two approaches of the Maturidi scholars: simple reliance on transmitted text and the deeper rational unveiling practiced by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi goes beyond plain text to uncover divine wisdom.

In this concluding passage, Imam Nasafi summarizes a key methodological distinction. While most Maturidi theologians accept the textual ruling without further inquiry, al-Maturidi delves into the underlying rationale, exemplifying the school's balance of revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between 'all the mutakallimun' and 'Shaykh Abu Mansur'.
This is a concise concluding remark, so every word matters.
2
Identify the two methods
The passage presents two approaches: the majority relying solely on the transmitted text (shar') and al-Maturidi performing a full 'kashf' (unveiling) of its meaning. Compare the depth of each.
3
Reflect on the praise
The author praises al-Maturidi with a lengthy supplication, indicating the high value placed on rational investigation within revelation.
This shows that uncovering wisdom is seen as a virtue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فجميع المتكلمين من أصحابنا رحمهم الله تعالى - بنوا الأمر على مجرد الشرع الوارد في تعيين قريش دون الكشف عن معنى ذلك، والشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله - وشكر سعيد وأحسن عن أهل الحق جزاءه - كشف كل هذا الكشف،

Commentary
This concluding passage distinguishes two methodologies. The author states that most Maturidi theologians (mutakallimun min ashabina) relied solely on the transmitted text (shar') that specifies Quraysh for the caliphate, without delving into the underlying rationale (kashf 'an ma'na). In contrast, Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (the eponym of the school) performed a full unveiling (kashf) of the wisdom behind this ruling, as expounded in the previous sections. The author praises al-Maturidi for this deeper analysis, which aligns with the Maturidi emphasis on rational investigation within the framework of revelation. This shows that while the school accepts revealed texts as authoritative, it also seeks to understand their inner meanings through reason when possible.

The Wisdom Behind the Quraysh Imamate Condition
The author distinguishes the purely textual approach from Abu Mansur’s deeper investigation of imamate conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Mansur al-Maturidi went beyond mere acceptance of the revealed text to uncover the wisdom behind the Quraysh condition for the imamate.

Core Idea
Abu Mansur revealed the hidden wisdom in the text that others accepted blindly.

In this passage, the author contrasts the approach of most Maturidi theologians with that of their founder, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. While the majority accepted the imamate condition of Quraysh purely from revelation, Abu Mansur delved deeper to uncover the underlying wisdom. This lesson explores the methodological significance of this difference and invites us to appreciate the depth of al-Maturidi's scholarship.

1
Read the passage and identify the two approaches

Commentary

They did not investigate why the law specified Quraysh. In contrast, Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi thoroughly uncovered the meaning and explained it. The author emphasizes that he has quoted Abu Mansur's own words verbatim. He does this so that the reader can appreciate Abu Mansur's great effort in reviving the religion, strengthening the truth, and his deep thinking in researching the realities of the religion and extracting subtle meanings and profound wisdoms. The passage ends with a prayer that Allah guides those who strive in His path and prefer His pleasure over worldly trusts and desires.

The Correct Method of Imamate and Refutation of Nass Claims
Nasafi states the method of imamate and rejects sectarian nass claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi asserts that imamate is established by ijtihad and not by explicit designation, and why he rejects the claims of the Rafida and Rawandiyya.

Core Idea
No explicit text exists for imamate; it is determined by qualified human reasoning.

Nasafi begins by laying down the methodological foundation: imamate, unlike certain ritual rulings, lacks an explicit textual designation. He then directly confronts the sectarian claims that the Prophet designated Ali or al-Abbas. This lesson covers that foundational assertion and its immediate refutation, setting the stage for the rational proof to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on method and refutation
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note how Nasafi first states the correct method (ijtihad) and then immediately labels the opposing views as false.
Pay attention to the term 'nass' and its negation in the phrase 'la nass hahuna'.
2
Identify the two groups being refuted
The Rafida (Shia) claim nass for Ali; the Rawandiyya claim nass for al-Abbas. Nasafi dismisses both as batil. Consider why he names them specifically.
3
Connect to the broader argument
This part establishes the negative claim (no nass) that the subsequent proofs will support. Think about how the proof from public necessity and the Companions' behavior build on this starting point.
The negation of nass here is absolute; the next parts provide evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طريق ثبوت الإمامة لمن يصلح أهلاً لها على سبيل الاجتهاد واستعمال الرأي على ما يستعمل في الحوادث الشرعية التي بالناس إلى معرفة أحكامها حاجة وقد عدموا فيها النص إذ لا نص ها هنا، وقول الروافض بوجود النص من النبي عليه السلام على علي وقول الراوندية بوجود النص على العباس باطل. والدليل على بطلان النص أن أمر الخلافة أمر عام يقع بكل الناس إلى معرفته حاجة ماسة، وما هذا سبيله كان النص فيه لو كان ثابتا يشتهر اشتهارا لا يبقى معه على أحد من الناس خفاء، ولا اضطر الناس إلى معرفته كالنص على القبلة وعلى أعداد ركعات الصلاة ومقاديرها وأوقاتها ومقادير النصيب والواجبات في باب الزكاة، وغير ذلك من الأمور العامة، ولما لم يوجد في ذلك خبر هذا سبيله، دل أن لا نص فيه البتة، يدل عليه أن النص لو كان ثابتا لما أعرضت الصحابة مع جلالة أقدارهم في الدين وشدة ورعهم وتمسكهم بالدين وتحرزهم عن مخالفة الشريعة عن قبوله والعمل به وتفويض الأمر إلى المنصوص عليه، بل بادروا إليه وولوه الأمر ولم ينازعه أحد في ذلك إذ لم يكن تقلد من تقلد للأمر منهم رغبة في الجاه والمنزلة والترأس ولا حرصا على جمع الحطام والاستمتاع باللذات البدنية والشهوات الطبيعية بل كان تقلدهم لذلك للقيام بحقوق الدين واستيفاء حقوق الله تعالى وتقوية الملة والسعى في إعلاء كلمة الله تعالى وقهر أعدائه وتشييد بنيان الإسلام ألا ترى الصديق كيف كانت معاملته الخلق ورفضه الشهوات والاكتفاء باليسير من القوت والخشن من الثياب والاجتزاء بقليل
Then, the way of establishing imamate for those who are fit for it is by way of ijtihād and the use of opinion, as is done in the case of shar‘ī events whose rulings people need to know, and they lack naṣṣ therein, for there is no naṣṣ here. The statement of the Rāfiḍa that there is naṣṣ from the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning ‘Alī, and the statement of the Rawandiyya that there is naṣṣ concerning al-‘Abbās, are false. The proof of the falsehood of naṣṣ is that the matter of the caliphate is a general matter whose knowledge is a pressing need for all people. Any matter of this kind, if naṣṣ concerning it were established, it would become so widely known that no one would remain unaware of it, and people would have no need to investigate it, like the naṣṣ on the direction of prayer (qibla), on the numbers of rak‘as of prayer, their amounts and times, the amounts of shares and obligations in the chapter of zakāt, and other general matters. Since no report of this kind has been found for it, this indicates that there is absolutely no naṣṣ concerning it. This is supported by the fact that if naṣṣ had been established, the Companions, despite their great status in religion, intense piety, adherence to religion, and caution against violating the Sharī‘a, would not have disregarded accepting it and acting upon it and entrusting the matter to the person designated. Rather, they would have hastened to him and entrusted him with authority, and no one would have disputed him in that, because those who assumed authority among them did not do so out of desire for status, rank, and leadership, nor out of greed for collecting worldly goods and enjoying physical pleasures and natural desires. Rather, their assumption of authority was for the purpose of fulfilling the rights of religion, exacting the rights of Allah Almighty, strengthening the religious community, striving to elevate the word of Allah Almighty, subduing His enemies, and reinforcing the edifice of Islam. Do you not see how al-Ṣiddīq dealt with creation, his rejection of desires, his contentment with little sustenance and coarse clothing, and his satisfaction with a small amount?
Source pages p. 1124

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the claim that the imamate was established by explicit designation (naṣṣ) from the Prophet. The author first states the correct method: imamate is established by ijtihād, as there is no naṣṣ on the matter. He then declares the claims of both the Rāfiḍa (for ‘Alī) and the Rawandiyya (for al-‘Abbās) to be false. The main proof is an argument from the public nature of the caliphate: it is a matter of universal concern, like the direction of prayer or the number of rak‘as. If there were a naṣṣ, it would have been transmitted so widely that no one could remain ignorant of it. Since no such widespread report exists, there is no naṣṣ. A second supporting argument: the Companions were pious and careful to follow the Sharī‘a; if there had been a naṣṣ, they would have immediately accepted and implemented it, and they would not have chosen Abu Bakr. The author emphasizes that the Companions’ motivations were purely religious, not worldly. He cites the example of Abu Bakr (al-Ṣiddīq) as evidence of his piety and lack of worldly desire, implying that he would not have taken the caliphate if it had been designated for another. The passage follows a logical order: statement of the principle, declaration of error, main proof (public necessity), corollary proof (behavior of the Companions), and an illustrative example.

Key Terms
نص
إمامة
اجتهاد
روافض
راوندية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Nasafi denies the possibility of any nass in Islam; actually he only denies nass for imamate specifically.
Correction: Nasafi is not rejecting the concept of nass altogether; he is specifically arguing that there is no nass for the imamate, as opposed to matters like prayer directions.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, how is the imamate established?
A) By explicit textual designation (nass) from the Prophet
B) By ijtihad and use of opinion
C) By consensus of the community
D) By hereditary succession

Key Takeaway
Imamate is a matter of ijtihad, not nass, and the claims of specific designation for Ali or al-Abbas are false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself preferring to follow a charismatic leader simply because they claim divine endorsement, without critically examining whether your method of accepting authority is sound? What would it take to change that?
?
This lesson teaches that imamate is determined by human reasoning, not explicit text. Are there areas of your religious life where you secretly wish for a 'textual shortcut' to certainty, avoiding the effort of ijtihad? Will you embrace the responsibility of reasoning?
?
The Rafida and Rawandiyya clung to nass claims despite clear contradiction. Is there a belief you hold that, upon honest examination using the method of Ahl al-Sunna, you might discover is baseless? Are you willing to be shown wrong?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
The Proof from Public Necessity: Why Nass Must Be Widely Known
Nasafi uses analogy with universally known nass rulings to prove no nass for imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's main proof: that if the imamate were designated by nass, it would have to be as universally known as other public nass rulings, but since it is not, there is no nass.

Core Idea
A nass on a universal need would be universally known; the caliphate is such a need.

Nasafi now presents the core rational argument. He draws an analogy between the caliphate and other matters of public necessity like the qibla and prayer rak'as. If there were a nass on the caliphate, it would be as famous as those rulings. Since there is no such widespread report, the claim of nass is false. This lesson unpacks that logical analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the main proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the conditional statement: 'if nass existed, it would be widely known...'.
This is a classic deductive argument about transmission.
2
Identify the analogies used
Nasafi compares the caliphate to the qibla direction, numbers of rak'as, and zakāt shares. These are all matters of general necessity with universally known rulings.
Think about why these examples are effective: they are uncontroversial and well-known.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Since no such widely known nass exists for the caliphate, the premise that there is a nass must be false. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The conclusion is that there is absolutely no nass (la nass fihi al-battata).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طريق ثبوت الإمامة لمن يصلح أهلاً لها على سبيل الاجتهاد واستعمال الرأي على ما يستعمل في الحوادث الشرعية التي بالناس إلى معرفة أحكامها حاجة وقد عدموا فيها النص إذ لا نص ها هنا، وقول الروافض بوجود النص من النبي عليه السلام على علي وقول الراوندية بوجود النص على العباس باطل. والدليل على بطلان النص أن أمر الخلافة أمر عام يقع بكل الناس إلى معرفته حاجة ماسة، وما هذا سبيله كان النص فيه لو كان ثابتا يشتهر اشتهارا لا يبقى معه على أحد من الناس خفاء، ولا اضطر الناس إلى معرفته كالنص على القبلة وعلى أعداد ركعات الصلاة ومقاديرها وأوقاتها ومقادير النصيب والواجبات في باب الزكاة، وغير ذلك من الأمور العامة، ولما لم يوجد في ذلك خبر هذا سبيله، دل أن لا نص فيه البتة، يدل عليه أن النص لو كان ثابتا لما أعرضت الصحابة مع جلالة أقدارهم في الدين وشدة ورعهم وتمسكهم بالدين وتحرزهم عن مخالفة الشريعة عن قبوله والعمل به وتفويض الأمر إلى المنصوص عليه، بل بادروا إليه وولوه الأمر ولم ينازعه أحد في ذلك إذ لم يكن تقلد من تقلد للأمر منهم رغبة في الجاه والمنزلة والترأس ولا حرصا على جمع الحطام والاستمتاع باللذات البدنية والشهوات الطبيعية بل كان تقلدهم لذلك للقيام بحقوق الدين واستيفاء حقوق الله تعالى وتقوية الملة والسعى في إعلاء كلمة الله تعالى وقهر أعدائه وتشييد بنيان الإسلام ألا ترى الصديق كيف كانت معاملته الخلق ورفضه الشهوات والاكتفاء باليسير من القوت والخشن من الثياب والاجتزاء بقليل
Then, the way of establishing imamate for those who are fit for it is by way of ijtihād and the use of opinion, as is done in the case of shar‘ī events whose rulings people need to know, and they lack naṣṣ therein, for there is no naṣṣ here. The statement of the Rāfiḍa that there is naṣṣ from the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning ‘Alī, and the statement of the Rawandiyya that there is naṣṣ concerning al-‘Abbās, are false. The proof of the falsehood of naṣṣ is that the matter of the caliphate is a general matter whose knowledge is a pressing need for all people. Any matter of this kind, if naṣṣ concerning it were established, it would become so widely known that no one would remain unaware of it, and people would have no need to investigate it, like the naṣṣ on the direction of prayer (qibla), on the numbers of rak‘as of prayer, their amounts and times, the amounts of shares and obligations in the chapter of zakāt, and other general matters. Since no report of this kind has been found for it, this indicates that there is absolutely no naṣṣ concerning it. This is supported by the fact that if naṣṣ had been established, the Companions, despite their great status in religion, intense piety, adherence to religion, and caution against violating the Sharī‘a, would not have disregarded accepting it and acting upon it and entrusting the matter to the person designated. Rather, they would have hastened to him and entrusted him with authority, and no one would have disputed him in that, because those who assumed authority among them did not do so out of desire for status, rank, and leadership, nor out of greed for collecting worldly goods and enjoying physical pleasures and natural desires. Rather, their assumption of authority was for the purpose of fulfilling the rights of religion, exacting the rights of Allah Almighty, strengthening the religious community, striving to elevate the word of Allah Almighty, subduing His enemies, and reinforcing the edifice of Islam. Do you not see how al-Ṣiddīq dealt with creation, his rejection of desires, his contentment with little sustenance and coarse clothing, and his satisfaction with a small amount?
Source pages p. 1124

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the claim that the imamate was established by explicit designation (naṣṣ) from the Prophet. The author first states the correct method: imamate is established by ijtihād, as there is no naṣṣ on the matter. He then declares the claims of both the Rāfiḍa (for ‘Alī) and the Rawandiyya (for al-‘Abbās) to be false. The main proof is an argument from the public nature of the caliphate: it is a matter of universal concern, like the direction of prayer or the number of rak‘as. If there were a naṣṣ, it would have been transmitted so widely that no one could remain ignorant of it. Since no such widespread report exists, there is no naṣṣ. A second supporting argument: the Companions were pious and careful to follow the Sharī‘a; if there had been a naṣṣ, they would have immediately accepted and implemented it, and they would not have chosen Abu Bakr. The author emphasizes that the Companions’ motivations were purely religious, not worldly. He cites the example of Abu Bakr (al-Ṣiddīq) as evidence of his piety and lack of worldly desire, implying that he would not have taken the caliphate if it had been designated for another. The passage follows a logical order: statement of the principle, declaration of error, main proof (public necessity), corollary proof (behavior of the Companions), and an illustrative example.

Key Terms
خلافة
قبلة
ركعات
زكاة
نص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi use as evidence that a hypothetical nass on imamate would have to be widely known?
A) The shared piety of the Companions.
B) Analogous examples like the qibla and prayer rak'as.
C) The consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate.
D) The inheritance rules of imamate.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a universally known nass for imamate proves that no such nass exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the caliphate was a matter of universal need, yet Muslims have fiercely disputed it, how does this lesson challenge your own willingness to accept claims without widespread evidence? Will you examine what you hold as certain from the Quran and sunna with the same rigor?
?
Nasafi uses the examples of prayer times and zakāt—matters every Muslim knows without investigation. In your own life, are there beliefs or practices you assume are divinely mandated but have not verified through widely transmitted sources? What would it cost you to honestly re-evaluate them?
?
The absence of a nass means no one can claim divine right to rule. Has your desire for a clear, authoritative leader ever led you to overlook the lack of textual foundation? Repent today from following charismatic figures without asking for their proof from the Book and sunna.

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
The Behavior of the Companions as Evidence Against Nass
Nasafi uses the Companions' piety and actions to argue against nass.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's supporting argument: the pious Companions, especially Abu Bakr, would never have acted contrary to a clear nass, so their choice of Abu Bakr demonstrates there was no nass.

Core Idea
If nass existed, the Companions would have immediately obeyed; they did not.

Nasafi strengthens his case with an argument from the behavior of the Companions. Given their known piety, if there had been a nass designating a specific person, they would have hastened to follow it. Instead, they appointed Abu Bakr through consultation, and his own ascetic life shows he was not seeking power. This lesson examines that historical and moral reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the companion argument passage
Read how Nasafi describes the Companions' characteristics: jalalat aqdar, shiddat wara', etc. Notice the counterfactual: if nass existed, they would not have disregarded it.
The passage emphasizes their religious sincerity.
2
Understand the counterfactual reasoning
Nasafi says: if nass existed, they would have transferred authority to the designated person and no one would have disputed. Their actual behavior (choosing Abu Bakr) proves the absence of nass.
3
Reflect on the example of Abu Bakr
Nasafi cites al-Siddiq's simple lifestyle as proof that he did not desire worldly power, reinforcing that his acceptance was not personal ambition but religious duty.
This supports the idea that he would not have taken it if it were designated for another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why would the Companions not have disregarded a nass on imamate if it existed?
A) Because they were eager for worldly power.
B) Because they were pious and careful to follow the Sharia.
C) Because they feared civil war.
D) Because the nass was widely known.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' piety and their actual choice of Abu Bakr confirm that no nass existed for the imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah has designated a specific path for you, would you follow it immediately and without hesitation? What does your actual response to divine commands reveal about your belief?
?
The Companions' actions were motivated purely by religious duty, not personal gain. When you act in religious matters, what is your true motive? Can you honestly say it is for Allah alone?
?
The proof here hinges on the consistency of the Companions' behavior with their beliefs. How consistent is your own behavior with your claimed beliefs in daily life, especially when no one is watching?

Method of Learning
beginner
4 min
Reason and Revelation in Islamic Law
Al-Nasafi states that law is not limited to text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Islamic law is not confined to explicit texts but includes rational obligation and testing.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom includes using reason where there is no text.

Al-Nasafi opens his argument by rejecting the notion that every ruling must be explicitly stated in revelation. He asserts that God's wisdom includes imposing obligations that require people of understanding to use their reason, just as in other matters without a clear text. This sets the foundation for the role of rational thought in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the two sentences slowly to grasp the negation (ليس بمقصور) and the positive statement (بل من الحكمة).
Notice the rhetorical structure: 'not confined... rather from wisdom'.
2
Identify the key principle
Understand that the passage establishes a general principle: texts are not the only source; rational testing and obligation are parts of divine wisdom.
The phrase 'people of understanding' (أولو الألباب) refers to those who reflect.
3
Consider the implication
Reflect on why this principle matters: it defends the use of reason in matters where revelation is silent, including theological reasoning.
This will be applied later to the caliphate issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس طريق بيان الشرائع بمقصور على النص ليقال إنه لو لم ينص فقد ضيع أمر الأمة بل من الحكمة إيجاب الامتحان والتكليف بوجوه يختلف فيها أولو الألباب كما في سائر المواضع التي لا نص فيها، أليس أن كل ذي عقل يعقل بالضرورة حدوث نفسه ثم يعرف بالتدبير والتأمل حدوث كل محسوس لم يشهد ابتداءه، ثم يعقل كون كل حدوث بمحدث أحدثه بفضل تدبر فيه ونظر، ثم يعرف المحدث بعد طول النظر في أمره أنه يوصف بأنه ما هو أجسم أم عرض أم غير جسم ولا عرض؟ ويوصف بالعلم في الأزل أو الحدوث، وكذا القدرة وجميع الصفات ثم معرفة الرسل والأنبياء عليهم السلام، ثم معرفة صدق الأخبار وكذبها، ثم قبول الشهادات وردها. إن ذلك كله أو تمامه كان طرق الحق في ذلك من الباطل النظر والفكر مع التضرع إلى رب العالمين في التوفيق لذلك والهداية إليه فإنه الجواد الكريم، وإذا ثبت ذا لم يكن لأحد منع جواز محنة الصحابة بعد قبضه عليه السلام بمعرفة الأصلح للخلافة، والعقد له بما سبق من أدلة نظره للمسلمين وشفقته عليهم وتعظيمه أمر الله تعالى وقلة مبالاته في ذات الله تعالى بلومة اللائمين وطعن الملحدين واهتمامه لما عليه من أمر المؤمنين، وليست الروافض بأول طائفة من أهل الزيغ جحدت أمرًا قصرت عقولهم عن الوقوف على وجه الحكمة فيه، غير أنهم الجهالة اختصوا بالطعن على الصحابة، وعلى من أرادوا إثبات الحق له وعلى سلف الأمة بل على الله تعالى وعلى رسوله ما قررنا وبالله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة.
The way of explaining the laws is not confined to the text, so that it be said that if He had not specified (it) by text, He would have neglected the affair of the community. Rather, it is from wisdom to obligate testing and obligation (taklif) through aspects concerning which the people of understanding differ, as in other matters where there is no text. Is it not that every rational person necessarily knows the temporality (huduth) of his own self, then knows through reflection and contemplation the temporality of every sensed thing whose beginning he did not witness, then comprehends that every temporal occurrence has a temporalizer (muhdith) that brought it about through increased reflection and examination, then knows the temporalizer after long consideration of its affair: that it is described as whether it is a body, an accident, or neither body nor accident? And He is described as possessing knowledge eternally or temporally, and likewise power and all attributes. Then (comes) knowledge of the messengers and prophets, peace be upon them, then knowledge of the truthfulness of reports and their falsehood, then acceptance of testimonies and their rejection. Indeed, all of that or its completion was the ways of truth in that from falsehood: reflection and thought along with supplication to the Lord of the worlds for success therein and guidance to it, for He is the Generous, the Noble. And if this is established, then no one can prevent the permissibility of the examination of the Companions after his death, peace be upon him, by knowing the most suitable for the caliphate, and the contract for him based on what preceded of the proofs of his care for the Muslims, his compassion for them, his magnifying the command of Allah, his lack of concern in the essence of Allah Almighty from the blame of the blamers and the criticism of the heretics, and his concern for the affairs of the believers. And the Rafida are not the first sect among the people of deviation that denied a matter which their intellects fell short of comprehending the aspect of wisdom therein, except that they—out of ignorance—are distinguished by criticizing the Companions, and those whom they intended to establish the truth for, and the predecessors of the community, indeed Allah Almighty and His Messenger, as we have established. And with Allah is protection and salvation from every misguidance and innovation.
Source pages p. 1132

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument that the way of knowing divine laws is not limited to revealed texts; reason also plays a crucial role. The author (al-Nasafi) begins by asserting that it is not necessary for every ruling to be explicitly in the text; divine wisdom includes obligating people to use their reason in matters where there is no explicit text. He then provides a systematic progression of rational knowledge: every rational person necessarily knows the temporality (huduth) of his own self, then through reflection knows the temporality of all sensed things, then realizes that every temporal thing requires a temporalizer (muhdith), and through further reflection knows the attributes of that temporalizer—whether He is a body, accident, or neither, and whether His attributes like knowledge and power are eternal or temporal. This leads to knowledge of the prophets and messengers, and then to distinguishing truth from falsehood in reports and accepting or rejecting testimonies. Al-Nasafi emphasizes that all this knowledge is attained through reflection and thought (nazar wa fikr) combined with supplication to Allah for success. After establishing this rational method, he applies it to the case of the Companions' selection of the caliph: since they used their reason and acted with care and sincerity, their action is justified and cannot be criticized. He then refutes the Rafida (a sect that reviles the Companions) by arguing that their rejection stems from intellectual shortcoming, not from any fault in the Companions. The passage thus defends the legitimacy of the Companions' ijtihad in choosing Abu Bakr and warns against innovation.

Key Terms
النص
الامتحان
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine guidance is limited to explicit scriptural texts.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that wisdom includes rational obligation where there is no text, so reason is a valid source of divine knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be implied if the way of explaining laws were confined to text?
A) That God neglected the community
B) That reason is unnecessary
C) That the community is perfect
D) That wisdom is complete

Key Takeaway
Islamic law is not confined to explicit texts; reason, through testing and obligation, is a divinely sanctioned path to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a religious question you have faced where you felt unsure because there was no clear verse or hadith. Did you honestly engage your intellect to seek the answer, or did you simply wait for someone else to tell you? What holds you back from treating your own reasoning as a form of worship?
?
Al-Nasafi says that obligating 'testing' through aspects that people of understanding differ on is from divine wisdom. In what area of your life are you avoiding the discomfort of intellectual struggle? What would it look like for you to embrace that 'testing' as a sacred duty starting today?
?
If you truly believed that using your reason in matters without a text is an obligation from Allah, how would that change the way you study your faith? What is one specific step you will take this week to act on that belief?
